801 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Robinson


No. A024100


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


185 Cal. App. 3d 528; 229 Cal. Rptr. 851; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2019



September 16, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 111082, Lucy Kelly McCabe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted defendant of possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11357.


OVERVIEW: An officer received an anonymous telephone call regarding narcotics activity, and was given defendant's name and address. Without a search warrant or an arrest warrant, the officer went to defendant's hotel room and attempted to purchase some marijuana; however, defendant denied having any marijuana. Through the partial open door, the officer could see an open suitcase on a bed with a large plastic bag containing a brown substance. Believing that the substance was marijuana, the officer then identified himself and demanded entry. Defendant was charged with possession of marijuana for sale. After defendant's motions to suppress evidence and to dismiss the information were denied, he withdrew his plea and entered a guilty plea to the lesser charge of possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11357. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found Cal. Penal Code § 836 authorized the warrantless search, and that an emergency situation was present as a matter of law. The totality of the circumstances demonstrated that the delay resulting from an eventual obtaining of an arrest warrant would have irrevocably resulted in the loss or destruction of evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction because defendant's arrest and the seizure of the marijuana were lawful. The record established the existence of exigent circumstances and the matter fell within the exception to the rule requiring a warrant.

 

802 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Prison Law Office v. Koenig


No. A026985


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


186 Cal. App. 3d 560; 233 Cal. Rptr. 590; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2132



September 11, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 31, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 794165, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prison law office challenged the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that granted summary judgment in favor of respondent chairman of the board of prison terms, in appellant's action to enjoin implementation of the board's policy of releasing parolees to the county of commitment, subsequently codified as Cal. Penal Code § 3003, and to declare § 3003 unconstitutional as a matter of law.


OVERVIEW: Respondent board of prison terms formulated a policy of releasing parolees to the county of commitment, later codified as Cal. Penal Code § 3003. Appellant prison law office sought to enjoin implementation of this policy on procedural grounds and to declare it unconstitutional as a matter of law. Summary judgment was entered in favor of respondent. This court affirmed because § 3003 was constitutional on its face. A parolee's return to the commitment county was not mandated in every case as § 3003 provided a non-inclusive list of factors to determine if the best interests of the public and parolee mandated release to another county or state. Thus, § 3003 was a reasonable means to achieve the legitimate governmental ends of effective parole supervision and successful reintegration of parolees into society. Special scrutiny of § 3003 was not required because the state did not relinquish its right to regulate the parolee's basic conduct during the remainder of the parole term. Finally, the statute was not overbroad as appellant did not demonstrate that the provisions inevitably posed a present total and fatal conflict with applicable prohibitions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that entered summary judgment in favor of respondent board of prison terms as the statute governing the release of parolees to the county of commitment was not unconstitutional.

 

803 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Alim v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 25088


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


185 Cal. App. 3d 144; 229 Cal. Rptr. 599; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1995; 13 Media L. Rep. 1528



September 4, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is granted. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to enter summary judgment in petitioners' favor.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, reporter, editor, and publisher, sought a writ of mandate to require respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) to enter summary judgment in their favor in an action for invasion of privacy brought by public official, a real party in interest.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, reporter, editor, and publisher, printed a news article which disclosed information about public official, a real party in interest. Real party in interest sued for infliction of emotional distress, interference with economic advantage, libel, and invasion of privacy under Cal. Civ. Code § 1798.53. The trial court dismissed all counts except for the invasion of privacy cause, on grounds that there was no triable issue of fact regarding actual malice. Petitioners' motion for summary judgment of the invasion of privacy action was denied on grounds that lack of actual malice was not a defense. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate to compel the trial court to grant the summary judgment. The court granted the writ, and directed the trial court to enter summary judgment in favor of petitioners, finding that the truthful disclosure of the information regarding real party in interest, as a public official, was privileged, despite the seriousness of the invasion of his privacy.


OUTCOME: Petitioners, reporter, editor, and publisher's request for writ of mandate requiring respondent trial court to enter summary judgment in their favor was granted because their disclosure of information regarding public official, real party in interest, was not motivated by malice, and therefore, it was privileged and not actionable as an invasion of privacy.

 

804 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Patterson v. County of Tehama


Civ. No. 23162.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


184 Cal. App. 3d 1546; 229 Cal. Rptr. 696; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1988



August 29, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Sept. 16, 1986; Rehg. granted Sept. 29, 1986. (See 190 Cal. App. 3d 1298)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tehama County, No. 24092, Reginald M. Watt, Judge. * 


*  Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: intervenor appellants challenged an order from the Superior Court of Tehama County (California) that issued a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent county officials to perform duties imposed by state law without regard to the ordinance at issue.


OVERVIEW: This lawsuit presented a facial challenge to the constitutionality of a county ordinance enacted by initiative. The ordinance purported to limit governmental authority to regulate the use of privately owned real property. The court found that certain provisions of the ordinance conflicted with general law in violation of Cal. Const. art., § 7, and were therefore invalid. Other provisions of the ordinance did not conflict with general law and remained enforceable. Respondent citizens challenged the constitutionality of the ordinance by bringing suit against the county. Respondent citizens sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent county officials to perform duties imposed by state law without regard to the provisions of the ordinance. The writ was granted. In determining whether a partially valid enactment may be enforced as to the remainder, a declaration of severability in the enactment, although not conclusive, was persuasive evidence of the enacting body's intent. The judgment was reversed because not all of the provisions of the initiative were unconstitutional, therefore the valid portions were severed.


OUTCOME: The order granting the writ of mandate compelling respondent county officials to perform duties imposed by state law was reversed and remanded because the several provisions in the ordinance passed by initiative were valid, and could be severed from the unconstitutional provisions.

 

805 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Nat'l Auto. & Casualty Ins. Co. v. Superior Court


No. B019064


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


184 Cal. App. 3d 948; 229 Cal. Rptr. 366; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1793



August 25, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 12, 1986. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: ORIGINAL PROCEEDING; application for a writ of mandate. Writ granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent to vacate its order of November 22, 1985, in Los Angeles Superior Court case No. NCP 12100 G, entitled In re Shant Mardirosian, which denied the minor's petition for confirmation, and issue a new and different order granting that petition. Petitioner to recover costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following an uninsured motorist arbitration, petitioner insurer initiated an original proceeding seeking a writ of mandate ordering respondent superior court (California) to vacate its order denying real party in interest minor's petition to confirm an arbitration award.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest minor was involved in a car accident. Real party and his father's uninsured motorist claims were submitted for arbitration. An arbitration award was made to real party, but respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County refused to confirm the arbitration award due to new medical evidence presented, and vacated the award. Petitioner insurer instituted an original proceeding. The court granted petitioner's request for a writ and directed respondent to issue an order granting real party's petition for confirmation of the arbitration award. The court held that Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.3 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1286.2 did not deprive real party of property without due process. The court concluded that the record did not reflect evidence adduced at the arbitration about real party's then existing symptoms. Neither was there proof that the award exceeded the power of the arbitrator.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County to vacate its order denying real party in interest minor's petition for confirmation of an arbitration award. The court held there was no proof the award exceeded the power of the arbitrator.

 

806 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Brewster


No. A026721


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


184 Cal. App. 3d 921; 229 Cal. Rptr. 352; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1950



August 25, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 20, 1986. Bird, C. J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Francisco County, No. 111864, Claude D. Perasso, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction from the Superior Court of San Francisco County (California) for robbery under Cal. Penal Code, § 211, contending that the trial court erred in failing to conduct a sincere and reasoned evaluation of the prosecutor's justifications for exercising four of his five peremptory challenges against black veniremen, and that evidence suppressed under Cal. Penal Code 1538.5 was improperly used at sentencing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with armed robbery. At trial, he challenged the prosecutor's use of peremptory challenges to black jury veniremen. The trial judge accepted the prosecutor's claim that the challenges were for specific bias, denied defendant's challenge, and defendant's subsequent motion for a new trial on the same grounds. Defendant was convicted, and at sentencing, the trial judge admitted evidence, under Cal. Const. art. I, § 28 (Proposition 8), over defendant's objection, that a gun used in an earlier murder was found in defendant's possession, a fact that had been suppressed at trial. On appeal, the court first held that the trial record disclosed sufficient evidence to support the trial judge's ruling on the peremptory challenges issue. Next the court examined the scope of the exclusions provided by the state and federal Constitutions, and held that because the rule was judicial in nature, defendant had no absolute right in addition to his rights under U.S. Const. amend. IV to suppression of the firearm evidence, and the court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence for robbery, holding that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant's motion for a new trial challenging the prosecutor's objection to certain veniremen, and that use of evidence suppressed at trial was not barred at sentencing.

 

807 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Phillips v. State Pers. Bd.


Civ. No. 25830


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


184 Cal. App. 3d 651; 229 Cal. Rptr. 502; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1934



August 20, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 17, 1986, and the petitions of all parties for review by the Supreme Court were denied December 11, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 330601, Joseph A. DeCristoforo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to issue the writ of mandate compelling the State Personnel Board to accept jurisdiction over the matter of Phillips v. California State University at Fresno.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public employee challenged a judgment from the Superior Court, Sacramento County (California) that denied his request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent state personnel board to accept jurisdiction over his complaint against the real party in interest, his employer, for wrongful termination. Appellant claimed that a collective bargaining provision for automatic resignation waiving his right to a hearing was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed a judgment that denied appellant public employee's request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent state personnel board to accept jurisdiction over appellant's complaint against the real party in interest, his employer, for wrongful termination. Appellant was terminated under an automatic resignation provision of a collective bargaining agreement that allowed his employer to terminate employees, without any hearing, who were absent without leave for five consecutive days. Appellant claimed that he had informed his supervisor of his leave. The court held that appellant had a property right in his position and was entitled to due process prior to his termination. The court found that when appellant disputed that he was absent without leave, he could not be held to have abandoned that property right. The court found that the provision in the collective bargaining agreement was unconstitutional because it waived appellant's right to due process. The court ordered that appellant be provided a hearing as described in Cal. Educ. Code § 89541.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that denied appellant public employee's request for a writ of mandate and ordered that appellant be provided a hearing on his termination. The court found that appellant had not waived his due process rights by abandoning his job when he disputed that his leave was unauthorized. The court held that a collective bargaining provision waiving appellant's due process rights was unconstitutional.

 

808 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Stephens


No. F004956


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


184 Cal. App. 3d 616; 229 Cal. Rptr. 238; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1931



August 19, 1986 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II, IV, and V.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 174676, Charles V. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the lower court is reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings in accordance with the views expressed herein, and to determine an appropriate award, if any, to Wife of attorney fees plus costs. Wife is awarded costs on this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California) which denied appellant's petition to modify a judgment of dissolution to allow division of respondent husband's military retirement pay.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife challenged the trial court decision denying her petition to modify a judgment of dissolution to include a division of respondent husband's military retirement pay. The parties had agreed for the trial court to retain jurisdiction of the issue of divisibility of respondent's military retirement pay, pending the outcome of a United States Supreme Court case. The Supreme Court held that federal law precluded a state court from dividing military retired pay pursuant to state community property law. Congress responded by enacting the Federal Uniformed Services Former Spouses' Protection Act (FUSFSPA), 10 U.S.C. § 1408, the effect of which was that California law treating military retirement pensions as community property was no longer preempted. The court held that although the parties agreed to be bound by the Supreme Court resolution of the preemption issue, it would be unfair to ignore FUSFSPA and subsequent case law. The court held that by their agreement and FUSFSPA, appellant was entitled to one-half of respondent's military pension attributable to longevity of service, but not to benefits attributed to service-connected disability or to service prior to marriage.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court because, where the parties had agreed the trial court could retain jurisdiction on the issue of division of respondent husband's military retirement pay, it was unfair and inequitable to decide the issue on the basis of case law, which had been effectively nullified by legislation and subsequent case law. The matter was remanded.

 

809 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Referendum Comm. v. City of Hermosa Beach


No. B014109


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


184 Cal. App. 3d 152; 229 Cal. Rptr. 51; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1901



August 8, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 23, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 543798, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Each party is to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which enjoined appellant from holding a special election in which voters were asked to decide an issue by initiative, on the grounds that Cal. Elec. Code § 4014 barred the election.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city enacted an ordinance authorizing a lease with a general partnership, which was to construct a hotel on property owned by appellant. Respondent voter committee filed a referendum petition; at a special referendum election, voters defeated the ordinance. Less than one year later, appellant called a special election in which voters were to decide by initiative whether a revised agreement should be entered with the general partnership regarding the same property. Respondent filed suit, and the trial court entered an order precluding appellant from holding the election on the grounds that it was barred by Cal. Elec. Code § 4014. Appellant sought review. The court held that Cal. Elec. Code § 4014 only barred special initiative elections held within 12 months of each other, and did not bar a special referendum election followed by a special initiative election on the same subject. Cal. Elec. Code §§ 4014, 4055 and 4057 were unambiguous, required no construction, and did not preclude the election appellant proposed to hold. The court therefore reversed the order enjoining appellant from holding the special initiative election.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order enjoining appellant city from holding a special initiative election was reversed because the statute did not did not bar initiative special elections held within 12 months of a special referendum election on the same subject.

 

810 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Graczyk v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. B015557


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


184 Cal. App. 3d 997; 229 Cal. Rptr. 494; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1956; 58 A.L.R.4th 1245; 51 Cal. Comp. Cases 408



August 8, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 23, 1986.



DISPOSITION: The June 27, 1985, decision after reconsideration of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner student appealed an order of the California Workers' Compensation Appeals Board that denied his application for benefits on the ground that he was not an employee of respondent university when he was injured in a football game.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner student was injured while playing football for respondent university and sought workers' compensation benefits. Respondent agency found petitioner was not an employee of respondent university under Cal. Lab. Code § 3352(k) and was not entitled to benefits. Petitioner appealed. The court held that Cal. Lab. Code § 3352(k), which was adopted after applicant was injured and which precluded an athlete's recovery of benefits, applied retroactively. Because the law regarding athletes was in a state of flux at the time petitioner was injured, and because petitioner's right to compensation was a purely statutory right, petitioner did not have a vested right in the unamended version of Cal. Lab. Code § 3352. With no vested right in his employee status at the time of his injury, he had no constitutional objection to retroactive application of the statute. The court found that retroactive application of the statute furthered a compelling state interest in clarifying the statutory definition excluding athletes as employees and did not grievously impair whatever rights petitioner had before the statute was amended. The order denying petitioner benefits was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order denying petitioner student's request for workers' compensation benefits was affirmed because the statutory section that excluded petitioner from the definition of employee applied retroactively and precluded petitioner's recovery of benefits.

 

811 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Howard


No. F006180


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


184 Cal. App. 3d 1; 228 Cal. Rptr. 813; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1888



August 4, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Merced County, No. 59907, George C. Barrett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs are awarded to Ann as the party prevailing on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which granted respondent wife's motion to modify the judgment of dissolution and ordered the immediate sale of the family home based on a change of circumstances under Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.7.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband challenged a judgment granting respondent wife's motion to modify the judgment of dissolution and ordering the immediate sale of the family home based on a change of circumstances under Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.7. The dissolution of the parties' marriage resulted in an award to respondent of the family home as additional child support. The title was held by appellant and respondent as tenants in common, and was subject to the property being sold when respondent remarried or until the youngest child reached the age of majority. Appellant argued the modification and immediate sale of the home was an impairment of his vested property rights. The court affirmed, holding that appellant's title as a tenant in common was always subject to divestment or termination at the time the house was sold. The family home award was a temporary privilege subject to modification under Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.7 to effectuate the purpose of the award. The award was intended as additional child support and was modifiable, without an express reservation of jurisdiction, because the modification benefitted the children.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Respondent wife's motion to modify the judgment of dissolution so as to sell the family home was properly granted, because appellant husband's title in the family home as a tenant in common with respondent was subject to divestment or termination at the time the house was sold, and the family home award was modifiable, because it was intended as additional child support.

 

812 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Kelly


No. A021335


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


183 Cal. App. 3d 1235; 228 Cal. Rptr. 681; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1874



July 31, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 19, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 100403, Thomas J. Dandurand, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied appellant's motion for severance of trial and, instead, tried appellant with a codefendant who was tried under special circumstances allegations that allowed for the imposition of a death sentence. Appellant was convicted for murder, attempted robbery, robbery, and possession of a sawed off shotgun.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was tried with, and under the same charges as, a codefendant. The codefendant, however, was tried under circumstances that permitted imposition of a death penalty. Appellant's motion for severance of trial was denied, and appellant was convicted for murder, attempted robbery, robbery, and possession of a sawed off shotgun. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed, finding that appellant failed to fulfill his burden of proving, to a reasonable probability, that a separate trial would have resulted in a decision any more favorable to his case. There was little error in the trial court's exercise of discretion in denying his motion. Appellant also failed to show any prejudice from the fact he was tried to a "death qualified" jury, and there was no constitutional question involved in ordering a joint trial. Finally, a joint trial did not result in appellant being forced to testify against himself. His testimony was voluntary rather than compelled.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Appellant's criminal convictions were upheld, because appellant failed to prove, to a reasonable probability, that a separate trial would have resulted in a decision more favorable to him, he suffered no prejudice from being tried by a "death qualified" jury, and there was no constitutional issue involved in ordering a joint trial.

 

813 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Flood v. Wyeth Labs.


No. B007332


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


183 Cal. App. 3d 1272; 228 Cal. Rptr. 700; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1877; 57 A.L.R.4th 901; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P11,114



July 31, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified August 8, 1986. A petition for a rehearing was denied September 8, 1986, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 25, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SWC 58664, Frank Baffa, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Costs on appeal awarded to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor child sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed appellant's negligence and strict products liability suit against respondent vaccine manufacturer, finding respondent was immune from liability pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 429.36.


OVERVIEW: Appellant minor child's suit against respondent vaccine manufacturer for negligence and strict products liability in connection with brain damage caused by the vaccine was dismissed based on the exception for those who administer vaccines under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 429.36. The court reversed the judgment and held that respondent was not immune from liability under § 429.36. Cal. Health & Safety Code § 429.36 provided immunity for persons who administered vaccines. The history of the exemption focused on those who administered the vaccine to minors, and immunity was then extended to those who dispensed the vaccine to the community, but immunity was never extended to manufacturers of the vaccine. The legislature did not intent to exclude manufacturers from liability. Manufacturers were not included in the plain meaning of "one who administers a vaccine." The court held that there was no legislative intent to extend immunity to respondent as a manufacturer, and costs were awarded to appellant.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing appellant minor child's action was reversed because there was no legislative intent to exclude respondent vaccine manufacturer from liability for a problem with the vaccine administered to appellant, and respondent could not be defined as an administrator of the vaccine.

 

814 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Advanced Delivery Serv. v. Gates


No. B012061


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


183 Cal. App. 3d 967; 228 Cal. Rptr. 557; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1853



July 28, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 328824, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a messenger service and a client, sought review of the summary judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which was granted in favor of respondents, two county police chiefs, the horse racing board, and the horse racing park, in appellant's action to declare Cal. Penal Code § 337(a) was inapplicable, or in the alternative, unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, a messenger service and a client, challenged the summary judgment granted in favor of respondents, two county police chiefs, the horse racing board, and the horse racing park. Appellants had an agreement that the messenger service would pick up money and instructions to purchase pari-mutuel tickets at the horse racing park and return the same to client. Appellants argued that although their conduct violated Cal. Penal Code § 337(a), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 19594, 19595 allowed for an exception and that, under present day conditions, the prohibition against receiving, holding, or forwarding money to bet on horse races should be held invalid. The court affirmed the summary judgment enjoining appellants' activities and held that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 19593, 19594, 19595 clearly limited pari-mutuel wagering to persons within the horse racing park. The court held that § 19595 had continually and consistently been narrowly construed in light of the broad prohibition against bookmaking. The court held that irrespective of the more relaxed climate for gambling, § 337(a) was not unconstitutional, and wagering via a messenger service was still proscribed by the statute.


OUTCOME: The judgment enjoining illegal gambling activities was affirmed because appellant messenger service and clients' off-track betting scheme was prohibited by a statute that disallowed bookmaking. The statute was constitutional, and there were no applicable exceptions.

 

815 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Turner


No. A023450.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


183 Cal. App. 3d 896; 228 Cal. Rptr. 478; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1850



July 25, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] March 26, 1987 Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 27542, Ellen Sickles James, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which convicted him of first degree burglary and second degree burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 459 and attempted burglary under Cal. Penal Code §§ 459, 664.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was in custody, charged with one burglary, when an officer took him to the location of several burglaries and questioned him, without counsel. Defendant confessed to several crimes and said of the residence of the burglary for which he was charged, "Well I could have burglarized it." Defendant's statements were admitted at trial and he was convicted of first degree and second degree burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 459 and attempted burglary under Cal. Penal Code §§ 459, 664. Defendant appealed, arguing that his U.S. Const. amend. VI rights were violated, and the court affirmed. The court found that, while it was unconstitutional to interrogate a defendant without counsel about a charged crime, it was not necessarily a violation to question him regarding uncharged crimes. Since defendant was questioned about both, the court found that the crimes were not so related as to jeopardize effective representation on the charged crime. Thus, the confessions of uncharged crimes were properly admitted. The court found that admission of defendant's charged crime statement was error; however, it was not reversible error because the statement did not contribute to his conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, finding that the trial court did not err in admitting defendant's confessions obtained for crimes not yet charged at the time he was interrogated without counsel. The court found that the trial court did err in admitting his admission regarding a crime for which he was already charged, but that the error was not reversible.

 

816 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Rankin v. Curtis


No. D001838


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


183 Cal. App. 3d 939; 228 Cal. Rptr. 753; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1852



July 25, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 507389, Sheridan E. Reed, Judge.



DISPOSITION: (See fn. 13.) Accordingly, a writ will issue directing the superior court to vacate its order finding the settlement in good faith and to allow Curtis reasonable discovery before recalendaring the good faith hearing. 13 The parties shall bear their respective costs in this proceeding.



13 We wish to stress that our decision should not be construed as expressing any opinion on the adequacy of the settlement. We have issued the writ solely because of the procedural concerns which we have expressed. After discovery and a new good faith hearing, the court, guided by the Tech-Bilt criteria, is not precluded from deciding that the settlement here is in good faith. Struggling through this case we too have pondered the question whether section 877.6, subdivision (c), as applied is constitutional as to persons who are not given the opportunity to appear and participate in the good faith hearing. (See Singer Co. v. Superior Court, supra, 179 Cal.App.3d 875.) That issue, however, was neither briefed nor argued. Moreover, in light of our result which is consistent with Singer there is no need to explore the constitutional ramifications of the statute's application. Putting aside constitutional considerations, however, basic fairness requires that every defendant facing potential liability be allowed to participate in settlement negotiations and contest the alleged good faith of any settlement among other defendants.

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, camp counselor, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which approved a settlement of a personal injury action by camp operator and its insurer with respondent, injured party. Appellant claimed that insurer's alleged bad faith in failing to protect her interests during settlement negotiations rendered the entire settlement "in bad faith" for the purposes of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.6.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, injured party, filed a personal injury action against the owner, operator and employees of a summer camp and its insurer. Insurer took the position that appellant, camp counselor, was not an employee of the camp and therefore did not qualify as an "additional insured" under its liability policy. As a result, insurer made no attempt to represent appellant's interests in the settlement negotiations. Appellant's own homeowner's insurance company provided an attorney to represent them at the settlement hearing pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.6, but they requested a continuance in order to take discovery. The trial court denied the continuance and found the settlement to be in good faith. On appeal, the court found that appellant's participation in the hearing was pro forma at best, due to the timing problems. The court also held that appellant had a right to take discovery regarding the good faith of the settlement. Therefore, the court issued a writ directing the trial court to vacate its finding that the settlement was in good faith and to allow appellant reasonable discovery before recalendaring the good faith hearing.


OUTCOME: The court held that appellant, camp counselor, was entitled to take discovery before the trial court conducted a good-faith settlement hearing. Therefore, it issued a writ directing the trial court to vacate its order finding the settlement in good faith and to allow appellant reasonable discovery before recalendaring the hearing.

 

817 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Blew v. Horner


No. A028549.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


184 Cal. App. 3d 1505; 228 Cal. Rptr. 456; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1984



July 24, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing granted Aug. 25, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 123312, William B. Boone, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff worker challenged the award of summary judgment by the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), to defendant construction company in an action for personal injury.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff worker was injured while working for an independent contractor hired by defendant construction company. Plaintiff's employer was not licensed as required by law. The court noted that generally workers' compensation is an employee's exclusive remedy against an employer for injuries arising out of employment. The court held that, because a license was a condition of independent contractor status, plaintiff's employer was an employee of defendant and, consequently, plaintiff was also defendant's employee. As such, the court held that plaintiff's remedy for his injuries was limited to workers' compensation. The court found that the dual capacity doctrine did not apply to defendant's concurrent roles as employer and land owner so as to impose a separate legal obligation. Finally, the court concluded that, because plaintiff was not using the building as would an ultimate user of the product, he could not state a cause of action based on defendant's liability as a manufacturer of a defective product. The court affirmed the award of summary judgment to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of summary judgment to defendant construction company because plaintiff worker was defendant's employee and his recovery for personal injuries suffered while working was limited to workers' compensation benefits.

 

818 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cutter v. Brownbridge


No. A024456


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


183 Cal. App. 3d 836; 228 Cal. Rptr. 545; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1848



July 24, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 569838-5, Winton McKibben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patient sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), that dismissed with prejudice his complaint against respondent psychotherapist based upon respondent's disclosure of the details of appellant's therapy in violation of appellant's constitutional right of privacy and his common law right of privacy.


OVERVIEW: Appellant patient sought review of the decision of the lower court that dismissed his complaint against respondent psychotherapist when respondent argued that he was protected by judicial proceedings immunity pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 47(2) when he disclosed confidential information regarding appellant's treatment. The appellate court reversed finding that appellant's constitutional right to privacy outweighed the policies underlying the judicial proceedings immunity when private material was voluntarily published without first resorting to a prior judicial determination. The court concluded that respondent could not rely upon the protection of § 47(2) when he failed to first invoke the psychotherapist-patient privilege found in Cal. Evid. Code § 1014. The court reasoned that respondent, by volunteering information concerning appellant obtained in connection with their relationship, without seeking the protection of the privilege, did so at his own peril.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal concluding that respondent psychotherapist could not utilize the judicial proceedings immunity when he disclosed private material about appellant patient in violation of appellant's constitutional right of privacy, and the disclosure was made voluntarily and without first attempting to claim the psychotherapist-patient privilege permitted by law.

 

819 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McAllister v. S. Coast Air Quality Etc. Dist.


No. B013210


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


183 Cal. App. 3d 653; 228 Cal. Rptr. 351; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1834; 42 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P36,964



July 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified August 11, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 504390, Jack M. Newman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order striking plaintiff's claims for punitive damages is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which ordered that plaintiff's punitive damages claim against defendant public entity be stricken from her first amended complaint alleging discrimination on grounds that pursuant to the 1963 Government Tort Claims Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 818, punitive damages were not allowed against a public entity.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee sued defendant public entity for discrimination, retaliation in employment, breach of implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing, and intentional and negligent infliction of emotional distress. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrers with leave to amend and ordered stricken from the complaint all references to and requests for punitive damages. On appeal, plaintiff argued that the 1963 Government Tort Claims Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 818, which precluded punitive damages against a public entity, violated the equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. The court affirmed and held that punitive damages against a public entity were not allowed, absent a specific statute expressly allowing them. The court held that the equal protection clause did not prohibit legislative classification and imposition of statutory restraints on one class which were not imposed on another. The court held that the classification between governmental and nongovernmental tort victims had a fair relation to the object of the legislation and promoted a number of legitimate interests.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order striking plaintiff employee's reference and request for punitive damages in her first amended complaint and sustaining defendant public entity's demurrers with leave to amend. The court held that the Government Tort Claims Act precluded an award of punitive damages against defendant and that a large punitive award would not necessarily deter other governmental employees from discriminatory conduct.

 

820 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cross


No. A028622.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


183 Cal. App. 3d 483; 228 Cal. Rptr. 134; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1827



July 18, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review den. Oct. 2, 1986 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).
Bird, C.J. and Lucas, J., are of the view the opinion should remain published.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 77973, Donald P. McCullum, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted him of grand theft. Defendant contended that it was reversible error for the trial court to compel the discovery of a statement recorded by defendant's investigator after he interviewed the complaining witness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of his conviction for grand theft, alleging that the trial court erred in compelling the discovery, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1102.5, of a record made by an investigator from the public defender's office working on defendant's case after he spoke to the state's complaining witness. The court affirmed the conviction, although it ruled that the trial court erred in compelling the discovery of the record. After defendant's conviction, the state supreme court ruled § 1102.5 was unconstitutional. The court discussed a long line of cases approving of § 1102.5 and other discovery statutes like it, and expressed its respectful disapproval of the state supreme court's decision, but held that it was bound to apply it. The court, however, found that defendant's conviction was supported by substantial evidence in addition to the witness's statement, and held that the compelled discovery, although erroneous, was not reversible error because it was harmless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for grand theft, because although the trial court committed reversible error in relying on a discovery statute ruled unconstitutional after defendant was convicted, the error was harmless because defendant's conviction was supported by substantial evidence in addition to that contained in the document obtained by the state through discovery.

 

821 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Juarez


Crim. No. 14277


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


184 Cal. App. 3d 570; 229 Cal. Rptr. 145; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1927



July 18, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 1, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 53017, William H. Lally, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order imposed by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which extended defendant's commitment to a state hospital under Cal. Penal Code §1026.5(b), following a jury trial in which defendant was found to be suffering from a mental disease, defect, or disorder which caused him to represent a substantial danger of physical harm to others.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was committed to a state hospital after having been found not guilty by reason of insanity of assault with a deadly weapon. The trial court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the state's petition for an extension of his commitment and extended his commitment for two years. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the state was not required to prove that the underlying conduct posed a serious threat of bodily harm to another person because the issue was previously determined in a prior commitment proceeding. The court noted that Cal. Penal Code § 1026.5(b)(1) was amended to only require proof that defendant was previously committed under Cal. Penal Code § 1026 for a felony. The court found that the amendment to § 1026.5(b)(1) precluded defendant's statutory interpretation and equal protection arguments. The amendment to § 1026.5(b)(1) did not violate the ex post facto clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, or Cal. Const. art. I, § 9, because recommitment procedures cannot disadvantage defendant in a determination of criminal guilt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order extending defendant's commitment to a state hospital because his underlying offense, assault with a deadly weapon, fell within the commitment statute. Defendant's equal protection claim was without merit. The recommitment procedures did not violate the ex post facto clause because they did not disadvantage defendant in the determination of his criminal guilt.

 

822 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Phillips v. San Luis Obispo County Dep't of Animal Regulations


No. B015913


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


183 Cal. App. 3d 372; 228 Cal. Rptr. 101; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1815



July 15, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified July 18, 1986. A petition for a rehearing was denied August 8, 1986, and the opinion was modified on August 8 and 14, 1986, to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 60146, Barry Hammer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Petitioners are entitled to costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant dog owners sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which denied their petition for a writ of mandamus requesting that the trial court vacate respondents', departments of animal regulation, destruction order and declare several county ordinances unconstitutional because they failed to provide notice and a hearing before permitting a destruction order.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, departments of animal regulation, seized appellant dog owner's dog after she allegedly bit four children and ordered the dog destroyed. Appellants demanded a hearing. Respondents gave appellants a "courtesy" hearing. The hearing officer found the dog should be destroyed. Appellants sought a writ of mandamus to order respondents to vacate the destruction order and a finding that the ordinances unconstitutional because they failed to provide notice and hearing before permitting a destruction order. The writ was denied and appellants sought review. The court held that due process required that appellants have an opportunity to be heard prior to the destruction of their dog. The court held that the hearing requirement could not be implied from the county ordinances and reversed the trial court's judgment, holding that the ordinances were unconstitutional for failure to provide for notice and a hearing either before or after the seizure of an uncontrollable biting or vicious dog. The court remanded for a determination as to whether appellants were entitled to attorney fees under Cal. Civ. Code § 1021.5.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that denied appellant dog owners' writ of mandamus because the court determined that the county ordinances failed to provide for notice and hearing. The court held that an ordinance permitting the county to destroy a dog without a noticed hearing to the dog owner who requests one was constitutionally infirm. The court remanded for a determination as to whether appellants were entitled to attorney fees.

 

823 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Julian v. City of San Diego


No. D003408


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


183 Cal. App. 3d 169; 229 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1801



July 11, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 543152, Douglas R. Woodworth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured minor sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied appellant's petition filed pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 946.6, for relief from the tort claim filing requirements of Cal. Gov't Code § 945.4.


OVERVIEW: After appellant injured minor was hurt on property maintained by respondent city, and filed a claim, pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 945.4, with respondent for personal injuries alleging respondent's negligence. Appellant's claim was denied and he was notified he had six months to file a court action. After expiration of the six-month period, appellant filed an application with respondent to present a late claim for damages where he named employees of respondent who had been negligent. The application was not considered because a timely application on the claim had already been filed. Appellant petitioned the trial court for an order relieving him of the claim filing requirements of § 945.4. The petition was denied and appellant sought review. The court affirmed, holding where a separate claim was not required, appellant's petition was properly denied because under Cal. Gov't Code § 950.6, the six-month statute of limitations of § 945.6 barred any action for damages for personal injuries against respondent's employees. The court found § 945.6 required an action against an employee to be commenced within the time prescribed for bringing an action against the public entity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed denial of appellant injured minor's petition where appellant's proposed late claim against respondent city's employees had the same basis of potential liability as his claim against respondent which was barred by the six-month statute of limitations. The court held the later claim was no more than an attempt to amend appellant's original claim; thus, the six-month statute of limitations ran from the date of the original claim.

In re Marriage of McDonough


No. A029143


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


183 Cal. App. 3d 45; 227 Cal. Rptr. 872; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1784



July 1, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. MDR 13465, Robert A. O'Farrell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying wife's motion to modify the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of a postjudgment order of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which denied her motion to reopen a judgment of dissolution in order to divide the nondisability military benefits accrued by respondent husband during their marriage.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife filed a motion to reopen a judgment of dissolution in order to divide nondisability military retirement benefits accrued by respondent husband during their marriage. The trial court denied her motion, and appellant sought review. The court held that former Cal. Civ. Code § 5124 (repealed), which allowed the retroactive application of the Federal Uniformed Services Former Spouses Protection Act, 10 U.S.C.S. § 1408, was not an unconstitutional violation of the separation of powers doctrine, and that it gave the trial court the authority to divide the military benefits. The court reversed the trial court's order denying appellant's motion to modify the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment denying appellant wife's motion to modify a judgment of dissolution, holding that the trial court had personal jurisdiction over respondent husband and subject matter jurisdiction to divide his military benefits, and that the statute permitting the retroactive application of the federal law permitting the division of the benefits was constitutional.
