In re Marriage of Schoettgen


No. F007093


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


183 Cal. App. 3d 1; 227 Cal. Rptr. 748; 227 Cal. Rptr. 758; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1780



June 30, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tuolumne County, No. 21235, Richard E. Tuttle, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs on appeal are awarded to the prevailing party, in this case respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former husband sought review of an interlocutory judgment of the Superior Court of Tuolumne County (California), which found a particular piece of improved real property to be community property by transmutation, rather than the separate property, in the parties' divorce proceeding and found that appellant was not entitled to reimbursement for contributing the property to the community.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in the parties' divorce proceeding that appellant former husband's real property had been transmuted into community property because the parties had agreed to characterize it as a community asset. The court found that his offer of compromise, wherein he characterized the property as community property, was an admission of the property's community status and found that the evidence of the parties' conversations and conduct supported the trial court's determination that the property was a community asset. There was ample evidence to support the conclusion that the parties had expressly agreed to community ownership of the property and the court resolved the record's factual conflicts in support of the judgment. The court held that appellant was not entitled to reimbursement under the reimbursement provision of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 because the case arose before the provision was enacted and it did not apply retroactively.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent former wife that the property had been transmuted into community property based on the evidence that appellant former husband had characterized it as community property in a compromise offer and that the parties had agreed to treat it as community property. Appellant was not entitled to reimbursement because legislation allowing reimbursement could not be applied to the case retroactively.

 

826 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Epps


No. F004776


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


182 Cal. App. 3d 1102; 227 Cal. Rptr. 625; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1774



June 27, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 24, 1986, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 16, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 22191, Edward Kim, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction entered in the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) for the offenses of murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, residential robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 213.5, and burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459. Defendant challenged his convictions and the sentence imposed of life without parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction for murder, residential robbery, and burglary. The state laboratory had fully consumed the blood and urine samples that had been collected from defendant, notwithstanding defendant's request that they be provided for independent testing. The court determined that defendant was not denied due process of law by the state's failure to preserve potentially favorable evidence or by the trial court's failure to impose an appropriate sanction. The court ruled that whatever duty the Constitution imposed on the States to preserve evidence, that duty was limited to evidence that might be expected to play a significant role in defendant's defense. To be material, the evidence had to possess exculpatory value that was apparent before the evidence was destroyed, and be of such a nature that defendant would be unable to obtain comparable evidence by other available means. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and found no authority which supported defendant's proposition that life in prison without possibility of parole was disproportionate to the crime of first degree felony murder where the killing was intentional.


OUTCOME: The court found no denial of constitutionally guaranteed rights of defendant and no merit in defendant's contention that a sentence of life without parole was disproportionate to the crime of murder where the killing was intentional. The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and the sentence imposed.

 

827 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Festival Enters. v. City of Pleasant Hill


No. A030497


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


182 Cal. App. 3d 960; 227 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1763



June 26, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Defendants and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 25, 1986. Broussard, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 252307, David E. Pesonen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which granted plaintiff theatre owners' claim for declaratory and injunctive relief and held that defendant's "admissions tax" was an unconstitutional interference with plaintiffs' protected free speech. The trial court issued preliminary and permanent injunctions, concluding that the tax impermissibly burdened free speech.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff theatre owners operated the only theatres in defendant city when an ordinance was adopted levying a five percent tax on the admission price of sporting events, movie theatres, concerts, shows, museums, performances, displays, and exhibitions within defendant city. The tax was enacted primarily to generate revenue for needed street repairs. The financing plan for those repairs, later approved by the city council, included a tax increase and three new taxes, including the admissions tax. The projected yield for the first year of the tax was based solely on the estimated revenues from plaintiffs' movie theatres. Although worded broadly so as to apply to other forms of entertainment, plaintiffs' theatres were the only businesses currently affected by the tax, although the main interest asserted by the city in enacting the tax was the raising of revenue. The court affirmed the trial court's holding that the admissions tax was discriminatory as it applied to plaintiff. Even if the admissions tax, as written, could otherwise pass constitutional muster, the impact of the tax on plaintiffs' theatres was discriminatory and violated the Equal Protection Clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that defendant city's "admissions tax," as applied to plaintiff theatre owners, infringed their federal constitutional rights to free speech and equal protection. The court held that the tax imposed an impermissible differential burden on plaintiffs' businesses and was not necessary to achieve the goal of raising revenue.

 

828 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Arciga


Nos. F005404, F005843


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


182 Cal. App. 3d 991; 227 Cal. Rptr. 611; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1766



June 26, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kings County, No. 7418, Dennis L. Beck, Judge; Superior Court of Merced County, Nos. 11765, 12005, Donald R. Fretz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment in each case.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants Mexican nationals challenged decisions from the Superior Court of Kings County (California), which reinstated criminal proceedings and executed prison sentences upon the determinations made by the Director of Corrections that found defendants unfit for a narcotics abuse treatment program. The director rejected defendants because they were illegal aliens and Immigration and Naturalization Service "holds" had been placed on them.


OVERVIEW: Defendants Mexican nationals were convicted of narcotic offenses in separate proceedings. After both defendants were sent to the California Rehabilitation Center, the Director of Corrections found defendants were unfit for narcotics abuse treatment because the defendants were illegal aliens and Immigration and Naturalization Service had placed deportation "holds" on them. The trial court reinstated the criminal proceedings and executed prison sentences. Upon review, the court affirmed. The court held that the pendency of deportation proceedings was an adequate basis for finding defendants were unfit for a narcotics abuse program under Cal. Penal Code § 3053. The court reasoned that the purpose behind the narcotics abuse programs was to rehabilitate persons and that defendants' out patient treatments would likely be interrupted prior to their being cured. The court found that the pendency of deportation proceedings placed the state court in a secondary position in terms of continued control over defendants. The courts held defendants were entitled to a meaningful opportunity to respond to the exclusion but were not entitled to be present when the decisions were made.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments that found defendant Mexican nationals were unfit for narcotics abuse treatment program. The court held that deportation holds placed on defendants by Immigration and Naturalization Service was a sufficient basis for the Director of Corrections to have rejected defendants from the program where defendants were unlikely to complete the outpatient program.

 

829 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Loebner v. Franchise Tax Bd.


No. A030289.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


193 Cal. App. 3d 64; 228 Cal. Rptr. 262; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1920



June 23, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted Sept. 30, 1986 (S.F. 25067)
Review dismissed March 31, 1988 and remanded to Ct. App., 1st Dist., Div. 4 (see rule 976(d), Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 826409, Roy L. Wonder, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of June 23, 1986, previously reported at 184 Cal. App. 3d 497.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) sustaining defendant's demurrer and dismissing plaintiffs' complaint for a judicial determination that a personal income tax deficiency assessed by defendant was illegal.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed plaintiffs' action for a judicial determination that a personal income tax deficiency assessed by defendant was illegal. The issue was whether plaintiffs had the right to bring an action without first paying the disputed assessment. The court held that plaintiffs' complaint was premature and barred by Cal. Const., art. XIII, § 32 until they paid defendant's assessment. A taxpayer may not circumvent restraints on prepayment tax litigation by seeking only declaratory relief. Plaintiffs attempted to circumvent the statutory scheme in an effort to obtain adjudication of their claims before payment. The court stated that § 32 meant what it said. Nothing in the policy underlying § 32, its history, or the cases construing it, supported an exception. Therefore, the trial court acted properly in sustaining defendant's demurrer and dismissing the complaint.


OUTCOME: The judgment of dismissal was affirmed because plaintiffs had no right to a judicial determination that a personal income tax deficiency assessed by defendant was illegal without first paying the disputed tax. Plaintiffs' complaint was premature and barred by the California Constitution.

 

830 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Scheidel v. Lister


No. D002934


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


182 Cal. App. 3d 657; 227 Cal. Rptr. 510; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1735



June 19, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 521040, Sheridan E. Reed, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff easement holder appealed an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant property owners in an action seeking to establish a prescriptive easement over property that was sold in a final bankruptcy judgment, on the basis that the order of the bankruptcy court selling the property "free and clear" barred plaintiff's action under principles of res judicata.


OVERVIEW: After defendant property owners purchased the land beside plaintiff easement holder, blocking plaintiff's route to his garage, plaintiff filed an action seeking to establish a prescriptive easement over the property. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of defendants on the basis that the order of the bankruptcy court selling the property "free and clear" barred plaintiff's action under principles of res judicata. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that plaintiff could not be deemed to have consented to the sale, because plaintiff was only a casual or accidental bankruptcy proceeding participant and his easement claim was not asserted, recognized, or otherwise directly involved in the proceeding. Further, the court held that res judicata was inapplicable, because the rule that bankruptcy proceeding were in rem was not so broad as to encompass unlisted non-participants like plaintiff in a bankruptcy proceeding which did not consider his easement claim.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment to defendant property owners on the basis of res judicata, because the bankruptcy proceeding did not decide the easement issue and because plaintiff easement holder was only a casual or accidental participant.

 

831 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



White v. Church


No. A035090


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


185 Cal. App. 3d 627; 231 Cal. Rptr. 565; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2025



June 19, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] We are aware of no other instance in the history of this state where legislation has been enacted which has interfered with the election process once that process, as here, has been set in motion. We hold that action invalid under the California Constitution.

Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondents to count the ballots for the office of Sheriff of San Mateo County and to certify the results of the June 3, 1986, election for that office. In order to expedite further proceedings, this opinion is final forthwith. (Cal. Rules of Court, rule 24(c).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate successfully requested a stay from the supreme court in the scheduling of a new election under Cal. Elec. Code § 17113.5 enacted by Assembly Bill 2739, pending determination of the code section's constitutionality under Cal. Const. art. IV, § 16, which prohibited special legislation. The court was directed to determine the constitutionality of § 17113.5.


OVERVIEW:  Cal. Elec. Code § 17113.5, enacted by Assembly Bill No. 2739, halted the regular election for sheriff when the incumbent died and scheduled a new election. Petitioner candidate, running for sheriff against the incumbent, challenged the constitutionality of § 17113.5 against Cal. Const. art. IV, § 16, which prohibited special legislation. The California Supreme Court stayed the operation of § 17113.5, pending a determination of its constitutionality. The court found that § 17113.5 was specifically created to affect this particular election wherein the incumbent died before the regular election. The court found that Cal. Const. art. IV, § 16, provided that a local or special statute was invalid in any case if a general statute could be made applicable. The court concluded that Cal. Gov't Code § 25304.5 addressed the general procedures to follow when a candidate died before election and because that already existing, general statute was applicable, § 17113.5 was constitutionally invalid. The court issued a preemptory writ of mandate directing respondents, county clerk and county, to count the ballots from the regular election of sheriff and to certify the results.


OUTCOME: The court found that the election code section in question was invalid under the California Constitution because a general law existed that would have addressed the issues presented by the death of a candidate before the election. The court ordered that a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondents, county clerk and county, to count the ballots from the regular election for the office of sheriff and certify the results.

 

832 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Fendrich v. Van De Kamp


No. B007053


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


182 Cal. App. 3d 246; 227 Cal. Rptr. 262; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1702



June 11, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 8, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-504154, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We remand this matter to the trial court for modification of its order in light of the opinions expressed herein. In all other respects, the trial court's denial of appellant's petition for writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant limited partners sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a petition for writ of mandate in appellants' challenge to the constitutionality of regulations propounded by respondent attorney general under the Gaming Registration Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 19800 et seq., which affected the operation of appellants' card club.


OVERVIEW: Appellant limited partners operated a card club, and after 20 years of operation, the club became subject to the Gaming Registration Act (Act), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 19800 et seq. Appellants sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to rescind the regulations promulgated pursuant to the Act, challenging their constitutionality. The trial court denied the writ because it found the regulations were necessary and were not an abuse of discretion. The court affirmed the denial of writ in part; however, the court remanded for the trial court to modify its order. The court found that portions of the application seeking information about family or former family members were overly broad and intrusive, and found that a release form violated the California Right to Financial Privacy Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 7460 et seq., because it failed to provide a period of disclosure nor did it advise applicant of his right to revoke authorization. The court also found that the annual renewal fee was excessive. The court further held that, except as indicated, the regulations were reasonable, were well within the police powers of the state, and did not unconstitutionally discriminate.


OUTCOME: The court remanded to the trial court for modification because if found that certain portions of the application seeking information about family members and former spouses were overly broad and intrusive, that the release form violated the right to financial privacy, and that the fees levied for annual registration renewals exceeded the sum reasonably necessary. In all other respects, the court affirmed the denial of writ of mandate.

 

833 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Milse


Civ. No. 68540


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


182 Cal. App. 3d 203; 227 Cal. Rptr. 70; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1699



June 10, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SWD76226, George R. Perkovich, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs on appeal are awarded to the wife.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-husband sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found that the marital house which appellant and respondent ex-wife had resided in was respondent's separate property, and that a brass lantern and antique telescope were community property. Respondent sought review of the trial court's decision not to award her attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held in a divorce proceedings between appellant ex-husband and respondent ex-wife that the marital residence was respondent's separate property, a brass lantern and antique telescope were community property, and respondent was not entitled to attorney fees. Appellant sought review of the decisions regarding the house, lantern, and telescope, and respondent sought review of the denial of attorney fees. The court affirmed and held that the separate property status of the residence was based on an oral understanding between the parties during the marriage. The court further held that respondent had no warning that the understanding would subsequently be held worthless by the enactment of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1. Thus, application would be the clearest sort of due process denial. The court held that there was no error in the trial court resolving the conflict in the testimony regarding the separate property in respondent's favor. The court held that the denial of attorney fees was not in error because both parties had relatively equal income potential and approximately equal obligations of the parties to their respective counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the property distribution order of the trial court because the application of a statute enacted after an understanding between respondent ex-wife and appellant ex-husband that affected the distribution would have denied respondent due process, and there was no error in the court's decision not to grant respondent attorney fees because the parties had equal income and obligations to their attorneys.

 

834 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Elysian Heights Residents Ass'n v. City of L.A.


No. B017422


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


182 Cal. App. 3d 21; 227 Cal. Rptr. 226; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1688



June 6, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 2, 1986. Gates, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied July 31, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C543436, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed and the stay order is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents association sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for administrative mandamus against respondents, City of Los Angeles (City) and developer, that would have revoked a building permit issued by respondent City to respondent developer, who was also the real party in interest.


OVERVIEW: Respondent developer, who was also the real party in interest, was issued a building permit by respondent City of Los Angeles (City) for a parcel of land for construction of a 45-unit apartment building. Appellant residents association challenged the issuance of building and other permits to respondent developer by respondent City, and contended that the issuance of the permit was contrary to respondent City's general land use plan for the area. Appellant contended that respondent City's zoning ordinances were inconsistent with the land use plan as required by state law. After exhausting available administrative remedies which upheld the issuance of the permits to respondent developer, appellant filed a petition for administrative mandamus in the trial court. The trial court denied the petition and held that it would not be equitable to terminate the project. The court affirmed, and held that there was nothing in the State Planning and Zoning Law that precluded issuance of permits which might have been inconsistent with a community's general plan and that the building permit was issued in compliance with respondent City's ordinances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of a writ of administrative mandamus against respondent City of Los Angeles (City). The court held that the State Planning and Zoning Law did not preclude issuance of permits which might have been inconsistent with a community's general plan, and that the building and other permits were validly issued to respondent developer, real party in interest, in compliance with respondent City's ordinances.

 

835 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. McCowan


Crim. No. 12853


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


182 Cal. App. 3d 1; 227 Cal. Rptr. 23; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1687



June 5, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 64599, Steven H. Rodda, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded for a new trial solely on the question of insanity. The verdicts from the guilt phase of trial remain hereby undisturbed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) in which a jury found him guilty of first degree murder, second degree murder, and attempted murder and found true the special circumstance of multiple murders, personal use of a firearm in the commission of the offenses, and personal infliction of great bodily injury in the commission of attempted murder. The jury rejected defendant's plea of insanity.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of first degree murder, second degree murder, and attempted murder and found true various special circumstances. The jury rejected defendant's plea of not guilty by reason of insanity. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's convictions during the guilt phase of the trial, holding that defendant's due process rights were not violated when the trial court barred, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 25, 28, 29, expert testimony as to whether defendant formed the required mental state at the time of the crimes and that the trial court's instructions during the guilt phase were proper. However, the court reversed with respect to the insanity phase of the trial, holding that the trial court erred when it instructed the jury that defendant was insane only if he was incapable of knowing or understanding the nature and quality of his act and of distinguishing right from wrong at the time of the offenses. The court concluded that Cal. Penal Code § 25(b) required a disjunctive, not conjunctive test. The court remanded the case for a new trial solely on the question of insanity; the verdicts from the guilt phase of trial remained undisturbed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and left undisturbed defendant's convictions in the guilt phase of trial, but reversed with respect to the sanity phase of the trial. The court held that the trial court improperly instructed the jury regarding the insanity standard and remanded the case for a new hearing and determination of defendant's sanity at the time of the crimes, with directions that a new trial be had only on the issue of insanity.

 

836 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jacobs


No. A026678.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


190 Cal. App. 3d 1235; 226 Cal. Rptr. 786; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1663



May 30, 1986 


NOTICE:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


*  Certified for publication except as to parts III and IV. (Cal. Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1.)


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted Sept. 22, 1986 (Crim. 25774)
Trans. April 21, 1988 to Ct. App., 1st Dist., Div. 3, for reconsideration in light of People v. May (1988) 44 Cal. 3d 309.
Involuntary dismissal March 31, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 111943, Edward Stern, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of May 30, 1986, previously reported at 181 Cal. App. 3d 916.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction in the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) for robbery.


OVERVIEW: After appellant was arraigned for robbery and appointed counsel, a police officer advised appellant of his Miranda rights, which appellant waived, and asked some questions in the mistaken belief that appellant had not yet been arraigned. Appellant confessed to doing "a couple of these robberies." The trial court found that the statement did not violate Miranda, but violated his U.S. Const. amend. VI right to counsel. It was not admissible as evidence, but could be used to impeach appellant's testimony. Appellant did not testify, but argued that the ruling was error. The reviewing court upheld the conviction. Statements which are inadmissible as affirmative evidence because of a failure to comply with Miranda can nevertheless be used for impeachment purposes to attack the credibility of a defendant's trial testimony. The reviewing court extended this to violations of the right to counsel.


OUTCOME: Appellant's robbery conviction was affirmed because statements which are inadmissible as affirmative evidence because of a failure to comply with the right to counsel can nevertheless be used for impeachment purposes to attack the credibility of a defendant's trial testimony.

Baker v. City of Santa Monica


No. B015599


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


181 Cal. App. 3d 972; 226 Cal. Rptr. 755; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1668



May 30, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 26, 1986, and the Petition of Plaintiffs and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 19, 1986. Mosk, J., Lucas, J., and Panelli, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. WEC058763, WEC059251, Richard Allen Lavine, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons set forth at a, b, c, and d above, the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a city and its rent control board, and respondent taxpayers challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which upheld appellant board's standards for determining rent increases, but permanently enjoined appellants from regulating or prohibiting, directly or indirectly, demolitions of controlled rental housing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city added a rent control law to its charter. Respondent taxpayers challenged its validity, seeking an injunction. The trial court determined that appellant board's standards for determining rent increases, the net operating income approach, were constitutional and upheld them. The trial court found, however, that the rent control law's removal aspects were unconstitutional, and permanently enjoined appellants from regulating or prohibiting demolitions of controlled rental housing. Appellants contended that the trial court misapplied the law. Respondents argued that the rent control law was unconstitutional. The court found that the trial court did not err in finding that the rent control law and regulations were constitutional. It reversed the trial court's judgment, however, holding that the trial court erred in concluding that fair market value was determined a landlord's fair return, that a rent control ordinance guaranteed owners anticipated appreciation, that a municipal rent control ordinance must provide self-executing procedures for removal from controls via demolition, and that owners were protected from the combination of rent control and removal restrictions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment; the trial court's findings that fair market value determined a landlord's fair return, that a rent control ordinance guaranteed owners anticipated appreciation, that owners were protected from the combination of rent control and removal restrictions were in error.

 

838 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. McNiece


No. F004998


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


181 Cal. App. 3d 1048; 226 Cal. Rptr. 733; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1673



May 30, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 11, 1986. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 22291, David L. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment as to counts II and III is affirmed. The judgment as to count I is reversed. The matter is remanded for retrial and/or resentencing in accord with the opinions expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) found appellant guilty of vehicular manslaughter under Cal. Penal Code § 192(c)(3), and violations of Cal. Veh. Code § 23153(a) and Cal. Veh. Code § 23153(b). Appellant was sentenced to a six-year term on count I and an eight-month consecutive term on count II. The sentence on count III was stayed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 654. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who had run a stop sign and killed a person while driving drunk, was found guilty of vehicular manslaughter with gross negligence, Cal. Penal Code § 192(c)(3), and two other DUI crimes, Cal. Veh. Code § 23153(a) and Cal. Veh. Code § 23153(b). Appellant was sentenced to six years for vehicular homicide, and eight months for the § 23153(a) count. Appellant sought review, arguing that the jury was not instructed clearly that gross negligence may not be found solely because of driving under the influence of alcohol. The court agreed, reversing the judgment and remanding for retrial. The court also criticized the trial court's sentencing: the trial court had ignored factors favoring probation, and considered only criteria supporting a denial of probation. Further, the criteria were not applicable: the victim was not "vulnerable," and appellant's crime was not "especially contemptible." In imposing a consecutive term for the DUI causing injury, the trial court applied criteria improperly. The court rejected appellant's claim that the six-year term was disproportionate to the crime. The court reversed the sentence for the DUI causing injury as barred by Cal. Penal Code § 654.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's conviction for vehicular homicide with gross negligence and remanded, holding that the jury should have been clearly instructed that gross negligence may not be based solely on driving under the influence. The court reversed the sentence for DUI causing injury, as barred by Cal. Penal Code § 654. The court instructed the trial court to consider factors supporting probation, and not to consider certain other factors.

People v. Sassounian


No. B005918


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


182 Cal. App. 3d 361; 226 Cal. Rptr. 880; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1714



May 30, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 26, 1986. As Modified June 27, 1986. Johnson, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 11, 1986. Bird, C. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A375674, Henry P. Nelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction, including the sentence of life imprisonment without the possibility of parole, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which imposed a sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole after he was found guilty of murder in the first degree, Cal. Penal Code § 187, with a finding that the killing was because of the victim's nationality, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(16).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of killing his victim because of victim's nationality. The court held that eyewitness testimony, taken together with the evidence of the shooting itself, including the ballistics evidence, clearly justified the inference that defendant and his companion were waiting for the victim to stop at the corner on his way to work. The jury was justified in concluding that the defendant intended to kill his victim. The court found no prosecutorial misconduct in impeaching defense witnesses, in the destruction of a jail record, or in final argument. The court found no jury misconduct concerning an improper matter because there was no evidence that the jury had considered it in reaching its decision. Because defendant's intent was the subject of adequate instructions, there was no reversible error in the instructions which were given. The statute which included special circumstances based on the victim's nationality was not impermissibly vague. Thus, the decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision which convicted defendant of murdering his victim because of victim's nationality, because it found no prosecutorial misconduct, the jury did not consider improper evidence, the jury instructions on intent were proper, and there was sufficient evidence to convict.

Prison Law Office v. Koenig


No. A026985.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


181 Cal. App. 3d 737; 226 Cal. Rptr. 728; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1645



May 29, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted June 19, 1986; Review Denied December 31, 1986


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 794165, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prison law office sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, City and County of San Francisco (California), granting summary judgment to respondent chairman of the California Board of Prison Terms in appellant's action to enjoin implementation of Cal. Penal Code § 3003, which provided for the return of a parolee to the county of commitment when released on parole.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prison law office brought an action against respondent chairman of the California Board of Prison Terms to enjoin implementation of Cal. Penal Code § 3003, providing for return of a parolee to the county of his or her commitment when released on parole. The trial court granted summary judgment for respondent, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed, finding § 3003 was constitutional under either substantive due process or equal protection analysis pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. 1, § 7, because the goal of equal distribution of parolees throughout the state was a legitimate, reasonable state goal. The court found that the state did not relinquish its right to regulate the parolee's basic conduct during the remainder of the term of parole by allowing a parolee to serve the remainder of his sentence outside prison walls. Because the state was constitutionally permitted to restrict a prisoner's liberty by incarcerating him and to require a parolee to live in a particular place during the parole period, then § 3003, which specified in which county the parolee would spend his term of parole, also was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment for respondent chairman of the California Board of Prison Terms in appellant prison law office's action to enjoin implementation of provision for return of parolee to county of commitment because provision was constitutional on its face.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction in the Superior Court of Lassen County (California) for being a state prison inmate who assaulted the person of another with a deadly weapon or by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury under Cal. Penal Code § 4501 and for intentionally inflicting great bodily injury under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7 and made numerous contentions, including that § 12022.7 was unconstitutionally vague.

In re Lopez


No. E002492


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


181 Cal. App. 3d 836; 226 Cal. Rptr. 710; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1655



May 29, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus under Cal. Penal Code § 3057 regarding his confinement by the California Rehabilitation Center where petitioner was returned as a parole violator, after being arrested for using heroin.


OVERVIEW: While on parole from the California Rehabilitation Center (center), petitioner was arrested for using heroin. Thereafter, petitioner was returned to the center. Subsequently, petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus, alleging that under Cal. Penal Code § 3057, he was being wrongfully held in that confinement pursuant to a revocation of parole could not exceed 12 months and that because he had been confined longer than 12 months his equal protection rights had been violated. On review, the court denied the writ. The court ruled that petitioner was not wrongfully confined because he was not being held under the Penal Code but rather the Welfare and Institutions Code and that the Welfare and Institutions Code did not have the provision petitioner asserted. The court noted that petitioner's equal protection rights were not being violated because the state had a compelling interest to rehabilitate rather than punish civil addicts in order to prevent drug-related crime. Thus, the court found a compelling justification for the distinction between the confinement of a state prison inmate and that of a civil addict committee.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's writ of habeas corpus because petitioner's equal protection rights had not been violated under the Penal Code.

 

843 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



United States v. State Water Resources Control Bd.


Nos. A027690, A030014


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


182 Cal. App. 3d 82; 227 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1694



May 28, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a Rehearing were Denied June 25, 1986, and All Petitions for Review by the Supreme Court were Denied September 18, 1986.
Companion case at State Water Res. Control Bd. Cases, 2006 Cal. App. LEXIS 171 (Cal. App. 3d Dist., Feb. 9, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 277544, 277506, 277555; Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 759239, 759240, 759266, 759540, 759547, 759558, 759586, 759611; and Superior Court of Contra Costa County, Nos. 193377, 193342, 193298, 193313, 193368, Richard P. Figone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment granting a peremptory writ of mandate, remanding the proceedings to the Board and commanding the Board to set aside the Plan and Decision, is reversed with directions to enter judgment denying the writ. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal. The stay previously imposed shall remain in effect until this opinion becomes final.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Consolidated appeals of plaintiff water users and purchasers challenged the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which commanded defendant board to set aside its water quality plan and water rights decision on grounds that defendant exceeded its authority ordering curtailments to preserve water quality and failed to provide more stringent quality standards.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff water users and purchasers sought writs of mandate to invalidate defendant board's water quality plan and water rights decision on the grounds that defendant exceeded its authority ordering curtailments to preserve water quality and that it failed to provide more stringent water quality standards. The trial court granted the writ and remanded. The trial court did not make factual findings, and the court limited its review to the legal question of defendant's powers and duties and did not consider the factual question of reasonableness. The court reversed with directions to deny the writ. The court found that defendant's primary duty was to provide "reasonable protection" to beneficial uses, considering all the demands made upon the water under Cal. Water Code §§ 13000, 13241. Defendant's power to set permit terms and conditions and to modify permits included the authority to impose responsibility to maintain water quality upon permitted projects equally, to give higher priority to more preferred beneficial uses, to impose monitoring conditions, and to set standards to protect municipal or domestic supplies and fish and wildlife.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment with directions to enter judgment denying the writ because defendant board's jurisdiction over the issuance and modification of water permits included authority to reconsider allocations and to impose conditions to protect water quality for domestic supplies and to protect fish and wildlife.

 

844 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Doud


Civ. No. 24445


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


181 Cal. App. 3d 510; 226 Cal. Rptr. 423; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1629



May 23, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 17, 1986. Puglia, P. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 742618, Robert N. Zarick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order from which Robert appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-husband challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that granted respondent ex-wife's motion pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 5124 for modification of the award of appellant's military pension to be included as marital property in the parties' underlying divorce action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-husband and respondent ex-wife obtained a judgment that dissolved the parties' marriage. They stipulated to the division of their property but reserved the resolution of the status of appellant's military pension. Thereafter, respondent filed a motion for modification of the judgment of divorce pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 5124 to include appellant's military pension as marital property. The lower court granted respondent's motion and awarded her an interest in appellant's pension. Appellant challenged the lower court's order and the constitutionality of § 5124. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court because the settlement of the parties' divorce action occurred when federal law conflicted with state law and, therefore, § 5124 applied because it was enacted to redress the conflicting situation. The court held that § 5124 did not interfere with the parties' stipulation to the division of their marital property because the agreement contemplated pending legislation for the determination of the military pension and, therefore, was not a final judgment for purposes of the constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that granted respondent ex-wife an interest in appellant ex-husband's military pension because the enactment of state law that determined military pensions to be included as marital property did not violate the parties' agreement to suspend determination of the pension because it was not considered a final judgment for constitutional purposes.

Citizens Against Rent Control v. City of Berkeley


No. A022404


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


181 Cal. App. 3d 213; 226 Cal. Rptr. 265; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1608



May 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied July 30, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 493837-0, Winton McKibben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order awarding costs is modified to reflect a total award of $ 4,824.80 (representing $ 14,539.88 less the $ 9,715.08 erroneously awarded as costs for printing briefs for the United States Supreme Court proceedings) and, as modified, is affirmed. The order awarding attorney fees is affirmed. Respondents are entitled to further section 1021.5 attorney fees for their efforts in protecting the fee award ( Serrano IV, supra, 32 Cal.3d 621, 639; Slayton v. Pomona Unified School Dist., supra, 161 Cal.App.3d 538, 553). Accordingly, the cause is remanded to the superior court to determine and award a reasonable sum, consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. Because appellants were successful in challenging only one of the two orders from which they appealed, it is appropriate that each side bear its own costs on this appeal ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 26).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged an order awarding attorney fees and costs to respondent citizens group by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), upon remitter from the state supreme court. Respondents had prevailed on their appeal to the U.S. Supreme Court in which they challenged the legality of a city ordinance upheld by the state supreme court.


OVERVIEW: Respondent citizens group brought an action against appellant city seeking to invalidate a city ordinance limiting campaign contributions relating to municipal ballot measures. Respondents ultimately prevailed after they appealed to the United States Supreme from a decision of the state supreme court upholding the ordinance. Respondents then moved for an award of attorney fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5. The superior court, on remittitur, awarded respondents $ 209,514.50 in attorney fees and $ 14,539.88 in costs. Appellant challenged the orders. The court affirmed, as modified, striking the costs for printing briefs filed in the federal supreme court because such costs were non-recoverable under the supreme court's own rules. The award was proper because important rights had been enforced which benefited the public as a result of the action. The court noted that respondent's ability to financially bear the costs was irrelevant and the fact that the fee award would create a financial burden for taxpayers also did not constitute a special circumstance rendering the fee unjust. The court found no abuse of discretion and held that the award was not excessive on its face.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, as modified, the order awarding attorney fees and costs to respondent citizens group. The court struck that portion of the award attributed to the printing of briefs for the federal supreme court because those costs were non-recoverable. The award was not excessive on its face and fulfilled the statutory criteria because important rights had been enforced as a result of the action, which benefited the public.

 

846 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Planned Parenthood Affiliates v. Van De Kamp


No. A032610


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


181 Cal. App. 3d 245; 226 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1610



May 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a Rehearing were Denied June 16, 1986, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. The Petitions of all Parties for Review by the Supreme Court were Denied July 31, 1986. Panelli, J., was of the Opinion that the Petitions should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: Implementation of the Attorney General's opinion would result in reporting of voluntary behavior, a result both inconsistent with the intent of the Legislature and in violation of the California Constitution's privacy guarantees. We therefore grant the peremptory writ. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue restraining the Attorney General of the State of California, and all California district attorneys, from henceforth and forever enforcing, implementing and administering the child abuse reporting law insofar as that law applies to voluntary, consensual sexual behavior among minors under the age of 14, bearing no indicia of actual sexual or other abuse in the judgment of the reporting professional involved. Nothing in this opinion shall be interpreted as interfering with the reporting law's application to actual child abuse. The statewide stay heretofore imposed shall remain in effect until the finality of [***2] this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner planned parenthood filed a petition for writ of mandate challenging an opinion by respondent attorney general, which applied the child abuse reporting law to all sexual activity of minors under 14, without regard to whether the minor was the victim of child abuse or was engaging in voluntary sex. Petitioner asserted the law did not impose a requirement on a professional who had no knowledge of abuse.


OVERVIEW: Respondent attorney general issued an opinion which applied California's child abuse reporting law, Cal. Penal Code § 11165, to all sexual activity of minors under 14, without regard to whether the minor was the victim of child abuse or was engaging in voluntary sexual conduct. Petitioner planned parenthood filed a petition for writ of mandate challenging the opinion, and asserted that the law should not require a professional, who had no knowledge of abuse, to report a minor as a child abuse victim solely due to reported sexual activity. On review, the court observed that a fundamental part of the reporting law was to allow a trained professional to determine an abusive from a non-abusive situation. The court weighed privacy rights versus governmental interests and ruled that implementation of the opinion would have resulted in the reporting of voluntary behavior, a result both inconsistent with the intent of the Legislature and in violation of the California Constitution's privacy guarantees. Therefore, the court granted petitioner's peremptory writ of mandate and restrained the enforcement of the opinion.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner planned parenthood's peremptory writ of mandate because implementation of respondent attorney general's opinion would have resulted in the reporting of voluntary sexual behavior, a result both inconsistent with the intent of the Legislature and in violation of the California Constitution's privacy guarantees.

Blinder v. Tom


No. B009330


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


181 Cal. App. 3d 283; 226 Cal. Rptr. 339; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1611



May 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 11, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 528820, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied appellant's writ of mandate, which sought an order to allow appellant to take out-of-state depositions that respondent corporations commissioner had denied. Appellant contended that the procedure for obtaining compulsory process of out-of-state witnesses, under Cal. Gov't Code § 11511, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent corporations commissioner filed an accusation before the Department of Corporations and sought to revoke appellant company's broker-dealer certificate, pursuant to Cal. Corp. Code § 25212(c). Respondent denied appellant the opportunity to take depositions of out-of-state witnesses. Appellant filed a petition for a writ of mandate with the trial court and sought an order to allow appellant to take the depositions. The trial court denied the writ. The court affirmed, based on the principle of stare decisis, holding that the procedure for obtaining compulsory process for out-of-state witnesses embodied in Cal. Gov't Code § 11511 was not unconstitutional or a violation of due process. There was nothing in the record which suggested any actual bias of respondent, or that appellant could not receive a fair and impartial hearing. The court disagreed with appellant's contention that respondent's denial of the requested depositions was arbitrary and a denial of due process. There was substantial evidence to support the lower court's determination, which was neither erroneous nor an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court that denied appellant company's writ of mandate, holding that respondent corporations commissioner's denial of appellant's request to take depositions of out-of-state witnesses was proper. The court held that the procedure for obtaining compulsory process for out-of-state witnesses was not unconstitutional or a violation of due process. The court followed the principal of stare decisis.

People's Advocate, Inc. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 24645


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


181 Cal. App. 3d 316; 226 Cal. Rptr. 640; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1614



May 21, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] A peremptory writ shall issue directing the superior court to set aside its judgment declaring invalid the repeal of existing Government Code sections 9027, 9028, 9030, 9031 and 9131 on the First part and invalidating the sections 9926 through 9929.5 and 9936 through 9937 of the Government Code as added by the Second part of the Act and the policy provisions, severability clause and effective date necessarily linked to them. In all other respects the judgment declaring the invalidity of the "First" and "Second" parts of the Act is affirmed. The alternative writ is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner activist group filed a writ of mandate seeking to vacate the judgment of respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted a motion of real parties in interest, the Legislature, Senate, and Assembly of the State of California, and joint rules' committees, holding that the Legislative Reform Act of 1983, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 9027-9937, was unconstitutional and of no force or effect.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner activist group filed suit with respondent Superior Court of Sacramento against real parties in interest, the Legislature, Senate, and Assembly of the State of California, and joint rules' committees, seeking a declaration that the Legislative Reform Act of 1983 (Act), Cal. Gov't Code §§ 9027-9937, was valid and seeking an order compelling compliance. Real parties in interest moved for judgment, challenging the provisions of the Act, which regulated the internal rules, selection of officers, and selection and powers of the legislative committees, and they also challenged the remaining provisions as inseverable from the invalid provisions. Respondent granted the motion and entered judgment declaring the entire Act unconstitutional. Petitioners filed a writ of mandate seeking to vacate respondent's judgment. The court, stating that the electorate did not have the power to make internal rules, which were exclusively the province of the houses affected by them, denied relief as to those provisions found violative by the trial court but held that the remaining provisions were severable and directed the trial court to vacate its judgment declaring their invalidity.


OUTCOME: The court denied relief to petitioner activist group as to those provisions of the Legislative Reform Act of 1983 (Act) found by respondent trial court to be violative of the California Constitution, as alleged by real parties in interest legislative bodies. The court held the remaining provisions of the Act were severable and a peremptory writ was issued, directing respondent to vacate its judgment declaring their invalidity.

People v. McDonald


Crim. No. 13576.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


181 Cal. App. 3d 63; 226 Cal. Rptr. 621; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1595



May 16, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I of the majority opinion.


Review granted Aug. 21, 1986 (Crim. 25686); Dec. 12, 1986 Trans. to Ct. App., 3d Dist., for reconsideration in light of People v. Alfaro (1986) 42 Cal. 3d 627 and People v. Pieper (1986) 42 Cal. 3d 471.
Opn. filed February 4, 1987 not certified for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 67410, Mamoru Sakuma, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that convicted him of aggravated assault under Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), with a special finding that he intentionally inflicted great bodily injury upon the victim pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7. The trial court found that defendant had three prior serious felony convictions and enhanced his sentence pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of aggravated assault under Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), with a special finding that he intentionally inflicted great bodily injury upon the victim under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7. The trial court found that defendant had three prior serious felony convictions and sentenced him to 21 years of confinement. Defendant sought review, contending that the trial court erred in finding that he had three prior serious felony convictions and in enhancing his prison sentence for those convictions under Cal. Penal Code § 667. The court affirmed defendant's convictions. The court held that proof of a prior conviction established only the minimum elements of the crime, even if the charging document contained additional, superfluous allegations. The court noted that the prosecution could not go behind the record of the conviction and relitigate the circumstances of the offense to prove some fact that was not an element of the crime. The court held that the proof of defendant's prior convictions was consistent with those restrictions and that the enhancements were properly imposed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for aggravated assault, and the trial court's enhancement of his punishment based on the finding that defendant had three prior serious felony convictions. The court held that defendant's prior convictions were properly proved and that the enhancements were correctly imposed.

 

850 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Caswell)


No. H000940, No. H001026.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH Dist.


201 Cal. App. 3d 1111; 226 Cal. Rptr. 68; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1597



May 16, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted July 31, 1986 (See 46 Cal. 3d 381)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of extraordinary writs that the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) granted on the ground that Cal. Penal Code § 647(d), under which defendants were charged, was unconstitutional. Petitioner defendant was denied the writ by the superior court and petitioned for it again.


OVERVIEW: In municipal court, a group of defendants demurred to charges that they violated Cal. Penal Code § 647(d), which prohibited loitering in or about public toilets to engage in or solicit lewd or lascivious acts. The demurrers were overruled. Defendants then petitioned the superior court for writs of mandate or prohibition, which were granted for all defendants except one. Petitioners, the people and the denied defendant, sought review. The court rejected defendants' arguments that § 647(d) was unconstitutional on its face. The court held that the conduct described in the statute was validly defined as a crime, as it was neither entirely innocent nor passive. The court held that the statute was not void for vagueness, because it provided notice of the proscribed conduct, and because its lack of objective criteria to guide a police officer in deciding the criminality of conduct did not distinguish it from several others statutes keyed to specific intent. The court ordered the superior court to vacate its grant of defendants' petitions for the writ, and denied the petition of petitioner defendant.


OUTCOME: The superior court's grant of defendants' petitions for an extraordinary writ was ordered vacated, and the superior court was ordered to deny the petitions. The court upheld the denial of petitioner defendant's petition. The court held that defendants' charges of loitering about public toilets to engage in or solicit lewd or lascivious acts was not unconstitutional.

 

851 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Nolasco


No. A026987


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


181 Cal. App. 3d 39; 226 Cal. Rptr. 65; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1592



May 16, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 24, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, Nos. 117150, 117239, Peter Allen Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We accordingly conclude that subdivision (c) is not ex post facto in its application to respondents. 2 The judgment is reversed and the petition for writ of mandate is denied.



2 We express no opinion on the question of whether respondents can be incarcerated beyond the terms of their original sentences for subsequent misconduct in prison, that issue not having been presented to us in this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people challenged judgments from the Superior Court of Marin County (California), in actions of habeas corpus, where the trial court had ordered respondent prisoners released from prison on the ground that extension of their parole revocation release dates, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 3057(c), amounted to unconstitutional ex post facto punishment. U.S. Const. art. I, § 10; Cal. Const. art. I, § 9.


OVERVIEW: The people challenged judgments granting respondent prisoners' petitions for writs of habeas corpus ordering their release from prison on the ground that extension of their parole revocation release dates pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 3057(c) amounted to unconstitutional ex post facto punishment. U.S. Const. art. I, § 10; Cal. Const. art. I, § 9. The people also sought reversal of the trial court's order enjoining application of § 3057(c) to any similarly situated prisoners, that is, those whose commitment offenses occurred before the effective date of the statute, January 1, 1984. The court reversed, and held that § 3057(c) was not ex post facto in its application to respondents, and the petition for writ of mandate was denied. Respondents committed offenses before the effective date of § 3057(c). Then the parole of each respondent was revoked, leading to imposition of additional terms in custody pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 3057(a). While in custody after January 1, 1984, respondents committed acts of misconduct. Section 3057(c) was used to extend respondents' parole revocation terms for acts that occurred after the effective date and fair notice of the amendment to the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, and held that the extension of respondent prisoners' parole revocation date was not ex post facto in its application to respondents, and the petition for writ of mandate was denied. The acts in question were committed after the effective date of the extension statute.

Serrano v. Priest


No. B006438.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


200 Cal. App. 3d 897; 226 Cal. Rptr. 584; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1586



May 15, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted August 29, 1986 (L.A. 32235)
Trans. August 18, 1988 to Ct. App., 2d Dist., Div. 2, with directions to reconsider the issue of finding for county juvenile court schools in light of defendant Superintendent of Public Instruction Bill Honig's concession of the issue.
Dismissed October 27, 1989 pursuant to settlement agreement.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 379231; Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 938254, CA 000745, C 335103, Lester E. Olson, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of May 15, 1986, previously reported at 180 Cal. App. 3d 1187 and Reprinted at 189 Cal. App. 3d 539.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individuals appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), that held that the state's system of school financing was constitutional. Plaintiffs had asked the trial court to enforce a previous judgment ordering defendant state to improve the method of school finance.


OVERVIEW: In the suit brought by plaintiffs to enforce the trial court's original judgment, plaintiffs argued that there were still unacceptable disparities between school districts in money expended per pupil for public education. As a result of the original trial, defendant state was required to reduce disparities in per-pupil expenditures caused by differences in district property wealth. In the enforcement action, the lower court ruled that the original judgment should be read to require elimination of all but "insignificant differences" in per-pupil funding. In affirming the trial judge, the court agreed that the legislature had done all that was reasonably feasible to reduce disparities in per-pupil expenditures and that wealth-related differences in school spending had been reduced to insignificant levels. The court also agreed that while education is a fundamental interest, every statute that touched upon education need not be subject to strict scrutiny. The court found that the system of school finance satisfied all compelling state interests and satisfied the rational relationship test of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment in favor of defendant state and found that the legislature had done all that was reasonably feasible to reduce disparities in per-pupil expenditures to insignificant differences. The court ruled that the current system of school financing was equitable and that any remaining differences in per-pupil expenditures were both insignificant and justified by legitimate state interests.

People v. Buschbom


No. D002870.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


180 Cal. App. 3d 1134; 226 Cal. Rptr. 178; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1581



May 14, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted August 28, 1986 (Crim. 25702); As Modified on Denial of Rehearing June 12, 1986; Dec. 11, 1986 Trans. to Ct. App. 4th Dist, Div. 1, for reconsideration in light of People v. Alfaro (1986) 42 Cal. 3d 627.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. CR 67760, Cb 67757, J. Perry Langford, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his convictions for conspiracy to commit murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 182(1), 187, and attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 664 entered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his convictions for conspiracy to commit murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 182(1), 187, and attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 664. Defendant conspired to kill two victims and attempted to kill the second victim. The prosecution alleged one overall conspiracy, and various acts relating to the plan to kill the first victim were alleged as overt acts in the conspiracy to murder the second victim. Defendant alleged that the admission into evidence of these acts was improper. The court held that the evidence showed two conspiracies, and that the trial court erred in admitting evidence of the conspiracy to kill the first victim. However, the court held that the error was not reversible because it was unlikely that a more favorable result would have been reached in the absence of the error. The court held that the enhancement for great bodily injury under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7, could not be imposed in conjunction with defendant's conviction for conspiracy. Accordingly, the court struck the enhancement imposed pursuant to § 12022.7, and affirmed the judgment in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for conspiracy to commit murder and attempted murder. The court held that although evidence was improperly admitted, it did not constitute reversible error. With respect to the conspiracy count, the court struck the enhancement imposed for great bodily injury.

Stroh v. Midway Restaurant Systems, Inc.


No. D002491


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


180 Cal. App. 3d 1040; 226 Cal. Rptr. 153; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1572



May 13, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 29, 1986. Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied August 13, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 526333, G. Dennis Adams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lounge sought review of a preliminary injunction imposed against it by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) on the request of respondent department, under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 23053.1, for violations of rules occurring after revocation of appellant's liquor license and pending an administrative appeal.


OVERVIEW: After hearing, respondent department issued a decision revoking appellant lounge's liquor license based on violations of a rule prohibiting nude dancing. Appellant filed an administrative appeal, which automatically stayed the revocation. Based on violations which occurred after the revocation decision, respondent sought and obtained a preliminary injunction under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 23053.1, enjoining appellant from further violations. The court affirmed the injunction and held that § 23053.1 validly invoked injunctive relief where appellant continued to violate the same rules after the license was revoked following an administrative hearing concerning violation of such rules. Appellant's U.S. Const. amend. I rights were not violated because injunctive relief was confined to activities occurring in conjunction with the sale of alcohol. Equal protection rights were not violated because the state had a compelling state interest to enforce against violations in these circumstances and § 23053.1 was a reasonable means to achieve this goal. The remedy was not ex post facto because it was invoked exclusively based on violations which took place after the revocation decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order entered by the trial court which preliminarily enjoined appellant lounge from violations of rules relating to its liquor license, and the court validated the statute providing for such relief.

 

855 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Tires Unlimited v. Superior Court


No. H001555


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


180 Cal. App. 3d 974; 226 Cal. Rptr. 25; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 1567



May 12, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the superior court to vacate its order denying petitioners' motion to quash summons and to enter an order granting the motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, defendants in the underlying action, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California), to vacate its order denying their motion to dismiss for untimely return of summons and to enter a new order granting their motion. The real parties in interest, plaintiffs in the underlying action, had failed to return the summons within three years after filing their lawsuit.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, defendants in the underlying action, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent superior court to vacate its order denying their motion to dismiss for untimely return of summons. The summons and proof of service were filed more than three years, but less than 60 days, after the complaint was filed. The court rejected the argument of real parties in interest, plaintiffs below, that the one-year phase-in for a new provision, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 583.210, which allowed a three-year period for service and 60 days beyond that period to file proof of service, was confusing and vague. The phase-in provision, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 583.160, was plain on its face and could not be ignored. Thus, under the phase-in statute, the former Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581a applied, making the return untimely when filed after three years of the commencement of the action. A request for an extension of time to respond to the complaint did not extend the time because it was made after the expiration of the three-year period. The court found that dismissal of the action was mandated because the return was untimely.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a peremptory writ of mandate to issue directing respondent superior court to vacate its order denying petitioners' motion to quash summons and to enter an order granting the motion. Under the relevant statute, the return of summons was untimely because it was filed more than three years after commencement of the action.
