N. Cal. Psychiatric Soc'y v. City of Berkeley


No. A026125


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


178 Cal. App. 3d 90; 223 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2638



February 28, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Defendant and Appellant for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 22, 1986. Bird, C. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 566778-3, A026125, Winton McKibben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and coalition sought review of summary judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) granted to respondents, hospital and psychiatric associations and physician in respondents' suit seeking judicial declaration of unconstitutionality and to enjoin enforcement of Berkeley, Cal. Ordinance 5504 (1982) prohibiting electroconvulsive therapy.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of respondents, hospital and psychiatric associations and physician, declaring Berkeley, Cal. Ordinance 5504 (1982) unconstitutional and void and permanently enjoining its enforcement. Appellant city enacted an ordinance prohibiting electroconvulsive therapy (ECT). The trial court granted respondent's motion for summary judgment finding no triable issues of material fact which appellants challenged. The court affirmed judgment because it found numerous state statutes, including the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq., which manifested a clear legislative intent to wholly occupy the area of law regarding psychiatric care and services, preempting the enactment of a local ordinance that added extra requirements on existing legislation for the use of ECT. In addition, the ordinance conflicted with the intent that ECT be available and that the free choice of psychiatric patients to take or not take ECT be protected.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment on favor of respondents, hospital and psychiatric associations and physician, declaring the ordinance unconstitutional and void and enjoining appellant city from enforcement was affirmed on appeal because the ordinance conflicted with legislature's intent to wholly occupy the area of psychiatric treatment and services and to protect the free choice of psychiatric patients to take or not take electroconvulsive therapy.

 

885 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Stier


No. D002015


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


178 Cal. App. 3d 42; 223 Cal. Rptr. 599; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2633



February 27, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On March 24, 1986, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. The Petition of Appellant Husband for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied June 4, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. D 70821, Elizabeth N. Zumwalt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: That part of the order granting Darlene a community property interest in Larry's military retired pay is affirmed; the remainder of the order is reversed. The trial court is directed to conduct further proceedings consistent with this opinion and to compute Larry's arrears according to the formulae set out in section I. Further, the trial court shall determine and award attorney's fees under Civil Code section 4370.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former wife and defendant former husband both appealed the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which determined that plaintiff was entitled to a one-half community property interest in defendant's military retired pay, but applied the federal Uniformed Services Former Spouses' Protection Act, 10 U.S.C.S. § 1408, to limit plaintiff's interest.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff former wife filed suit to reduce the underpayment of her interest in defendant's military retired pay. The trial court awarded plaintiff a one-half community property interest but applied the Uniformed Services Former Spouses' Protection Act (FUSFSPA), 10 U.S.C.S. § 1408, to limit her interest. Plaintiff challenged the trial court's calculation, and defendant asserted that the trial court lacked jurisdiction under the FUSFSPA. On appeal, the court affirmed that part of the judgment related to plaintiff's interest and held that plaintiff had a community property interest in one-half of defendant's gross retirement benefits at the time of judgment and subsequent increases directly related thereto. However, those increases did not include defendant's increased benefits received from the postdissolution aggravation of his disability. As a result, the court reversed the trial court's method of calculating plaintiff's interest. The court also rejected defendant's jurisdictional challenge because there was no basis upon which to retroactively apply FUSFSA, and to do so would unconstitutionally destroy plaintiff's vested property right in defendant's benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part because plaintiff former wife had a community property interest in one-half of defendant former husband's military retired pay. The court reversed the trial court's calculation of plaintiff's benefits because plaintiff was not entitled to increases caused by the postdissolution aggravation of defendant's disability.

 

886 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Flores


No. F004174


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


178 Cal. App. 3d 74; 223 Cal. Rptr. 465; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2637



February 27, 1986 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III and V, VI, VII and VIII.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied June 20, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 304550-7, Dwayne Keyes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment as to all counts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), of two counts of second degree murder under Cal. Pen. Code § 187, and one count of attempted murder under Cal. Pen. Code §§ 187, 664, raising questions as to jury instructions regarding malice, procedural and substantive due process, equal protection, and cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his conviction of two counts of second degree murder under Cal. Pen. Code § 187, and one count of attempted murder under Cal. Pen. Code §§ 187, 664. The court found no error in the jury instructions because the jury knew it was necessary to find that appellant acted with a subjective appreciation of the risk his conduct created, and that express malice was necessary. Proposition 7 did not violate appellant's procedural due process rights by punishing all degrees of attempted murder in the same way because its title fairly and sufficiently described the initiative and did not mislead the public. Appellant's substantive due process rights were not violated because § 664 punishing all attempted murders equally was rationally related to a proper legislative goal, nor were his equal protection rights violated because his fundamental interest in liberty was not involved, the strict scrutiny test did not apply, and a rational basis existed to punish all attempted murders equally. Identical punishment for both degrees of attempted murder was not cruel and unusual punishment because the punishment was not out of all proportion or clearly an extraordinary penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting appellant as to second degree murder and attempted murder because the court found the jury instructions regarding malice were correct, appellant's procedural and substantive due process, and equal protection rights were not violated, and identical punishment for both first and second degree attempted murder was not cruel and unusual punishment.

 

887 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Ewap, Inc. v. City of Ont.


No. E001021


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


177 Cal. App. 3d 1108; 223 Cal. Rptr. 422; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2621



February 25, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 14, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. OCV 26693, Charles Bierschbach, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying plaintiff attorneys' fees is reversed. The matter is remanded to the superior court for determination of the amount of the fee award both in the action below and on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff adult bookstore sought review of the portion of a summary judgment decision of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which denied attorney's fees pursuant to the Civil Rights Attorney's Fee Awards Act of 1976, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988, in favor of defendant city. Defendant cross-appealed and alleged that a special circumstance justified the denial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city enacted zoning and permit ordinances which dictated that all preexisting adult businesses, including plaintiff adult bookstore, would cease operation within a five year period. Late in the lower court action, defendant conceded that the use permit was unconstitutional and agreed not to enforce the zoning requirements pending trial. Plaintiff claimed its civil rights were violated pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and that it was the prevailing party for purposes of the Civil Rights Attorney's Fee Awards Act of 1976, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. The court reversed the lower court's denial of an attorney's fees award because plaintiff was a prevailing party when it obtained the essential relief it sought in its complaint. The court held that plaintiff's civil rights were violated because the nonspeculative threat of the enforcement of unconstitutional zoning laws constituted a deprivation of civil rights sufficient to invoke § 1983. The court rejected defendant's claim that because petitioner had never been denied its constitutional rights due to defendant's concession and lack of enforcement, a special circumstance existed that justified the denial of attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's denial of an attorney's fees award, in favor of plaintiff adult bookstore, because plaintiff became the prevailing party when it obtained the essential relief sought in its complaint. The court determined that defendant city's concession that the zoning ordinances were unconstitutional did not constitute a special circumstance that justified the denial of attorney's fees.

Terminal Plaza Corp. v. City


No. A021611


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


177 Cal. App. 3d 892; 223 Cal. Rptr. 379; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2605



February 24, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 786779, Frank W. Shaw, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: That part of the judgment declaring the ordinance invalid due to the failure of appellant to submit the proposal to the Planning Commission is reversed. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which ruled that appellant violated San Francisco City Charter § 7.501 when it created the Residential Hotel Unit Conversion and Demolition Ordinance, Ord. 15-81, ch. 41, San Francisco Admin. Code, without prior submission to the planning commission or an environmental evaluation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city enacted an ordinance that created a moratorium on the demolition or conversion of residential hotel units. Respondent hotel owner filed an action challenging the validity of the ordinance on several grounds. The lower court ruled that the ordinance was invalid because appellant violated San Francisco City Charter § 7.501 and the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq., without prior submission of the proposal to the planning commission or an adequate environmental evaluation.. The court reversed the judgment that declared the ordinance invalid due to appellant's failure to submit the proposal to the planning commission. The court held that the ordinance was of general application and merely regulated existing uses; thus, the planning commission approval was not necessarily required. The court affirmed the judgment insofar as the ordinance violated CEQA for appellant's failure to prepare an environmental impact report. The court affirmed the award of attorney fees to appellant, as it appeared that respondent brought its action primarily to further a significant public purpose, rather than to protect its own rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the part of the lower court's judgment that declared the ordinance invalid due to the failure of appellant city to submit the proposal to the planning commission. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects because appellant failed to prepare an environmental impact report, which was required prior to the ordinance's approval.

O'Connor v. Superior Court


No. F006105, Civ. No. F006119


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District

February 24, 1986

DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue to the Superior Court of the County of Kern in action No. 190712 to vacate its order overruling petitioners' demurrers to the first and third causes of action of plaintiff's complaint and to enter a new order sustaining said demurrers without leave to amend. Each party will bear his or her own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, campaign consulting firm and political candidate, sought a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of Kern County (California) to vacate its order overruling petitioners' demurrers to the first and third causes of action of real party in interest political incumbent's unfair business practice and misrepresentation complaint and to enter a new order sustaining said demurrers without leave to amend.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest political incumbent alleged that petitioners, campaign consulting firm and political candidate, engaged in the unfair practice of promoting candidates by unauthorized use of names of public figures and false attribution to such figures of endorsements and purported quotations. Respondent superior court overruled petitioners' demurrers to the first and third causes of action of real party in interest's complaint and petitioners sought a peremptory writ of mandate to respondent to vacate its order overruling its demurrers, and to enter a new order sustaining them without leave to amend. The court held that, because real party in interest's cause of action arising from election campaigning was not authorized in the Cal. Elec. Code, the legislature recognized that any attempt at wholesale regulation of, or government intrusion into, the content of political literature was constitutionally impermissible.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate to respondent superior court to vacate its order overruling petitioners', campaign consulting firm and political candidate, demurrers in real party in interest political incumbent's complaint and to enter a new order sustaining said demurrers without leave to amend because the consumer protection statutes were not applicable to political election campaigning.

Paoli v. Cal. Coastal Com


No. A028421


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


178 Cal. App. 3d 544; 223 Cal. Rptr. 792; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2677



February 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 14, 1986, as Modified March 18, 1986 and Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 7, 1986. Lucas, J., and Panelli, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 47849, Arthur B. Broaddus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the trial court is directed to deny the petition for writ of administrative mandamus. 8



8 The State Commission urges this court to render an opinion on whether the commission should have accepted respondent's offer in the spring of 1983 to deed an open-space easement subject to certain enumerated conditions. The State Commission also makes an argument against respondent's receipt of attorney's fees, should the trial court attempt to order them in the future. We decline to discuss the merits of either issue. "An appellate court will not review questions which are moot and which are only of academic importance. It will not undertake to determine abstract questions of law at the request of a party who shows that no substantial rights can be affected by the decision either way." ( Keefer v. Keefer (1939) 31 Cal.App.2d 335, 337 [87 P.2d 856].)

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, California Coastal Commission, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which granted a peremptory writ of administrative mandamus pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5, in favor of respondent developer, and ordered appellant to issue a coastal development plant free of all open-space easement conditions.


OVERVIEW: Respondent developer sought permission to construct an inn complex within a critical view corridor. A permit was issued that included a doughnut-shaped open-space easement around the inn. Respondent challenged the easement condition and subsequently, appellant, California Coastal Commission, moved the easement to an access road area. Respondent challenged the easement shift and claimed that the two easements were intended for different purposes. The court reversed the lower court's grant of an administrative mandamus order that ordered appellant to strike the easement condition. The court reasoned that the easement could be moved because the same purpose would be served when the project as a whole could be conditioned to provide scenic protection as required by Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30251. The court held that the open-space easement permit condition was constitutional because it was reasonably related to one of the Coastal Act's principle objectives of preserving the scenic and visual qualities of coastal areas and was supported by evidence in the record.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's grant of a peremptory writ of administrative mandamus, in favor of appellant, California Coastal Commission, because the open-space easement condition of respondent developer's permit would provide scenic protection as required by the Coastal Act. The court held that the open-space easement condition was constitutional because it was reasonably related to a principal objective of the Coastal Act.

Women's Internat. League Etc. Freedom v. City of Fresno


Civ. No. F003074


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


186 Cal. App. 3d 30; 237 Cal. Rptr. 577; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2592



February 18, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review granted May 22, 1986. Review dismissed and opinion ordered published on May 14, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, Nos. 265854-0, F 003074, Frank J. Creede, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: To the extent the trial court declared section 2-2809(c) unconstitutional and enjoined City from enforcing it, the judgment is reversed. The appeal by the League is dismissed. ( Turner v. Markham (1909) 156 Cal. 68 [103 P. 319].) Neither party is entitled to attorney fees. Each party to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which declared Fresno, Cal., Municipal Code § 2-2809(c), which prohibited political advertising on municipally owned and operated buses, unconstitutional and enjoined defendant from enforcing it against plaintiff women's league.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff women's league contracted with defendant city to display signs containing a message regarding registration for the draft inside transit buses. Defendant caused the transit to remove the signs pursuant to Fresno, Cal., Municipal Code § 2-2809(c), which prohibited political advertising on municipally owned and operated buses. The trial court held that § 2-2809(c) was unconstitutional and enjoined defendant city from enforcing it. On appeal, the court reversed holding that § 2-2809(c) as it applied to advertising within buses was constitutional because the privacy rights of the captive audience justified the exclusion within transit buses of forms of expression which could not otherwise be restricted. Therefore, the court held that the area within a transit bus was not a public forum worthy of constitutional protection under Cal. Const.


OUTCOME: On appeal, the court reversed the ruling that the municipal ordinance which prohibited political advertising on municipally owned and operated buses was unconstitutional holding that the privacy rights of the captive audience justified the exclusion within transit buses of forms of expression which could not otherwise be restricted.

People v. Rios


No. A020984


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


177 Cal. App. 3d 445; 222 Cal. Rptr. 913; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2564



February 10, 1986 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, the portion of the opinion certified for publication follows.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 21, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 72439, Mark L. Eaton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted him of one count of concealment or detention of a child in violation of a custody order under Cal. Penal Code § 278.5 and one count of felonious false imprisonment under Cal. Penal Code §§ 236, 237.


OVERVIEW: Appellant picked up his daughter for a weekend visit under a child custody order and then filed a false missing person's report on his daughter with the police. Appellant later drove his daughter to Mexico, left her with his cousin for the next four years, and returned to California. The child had no knowledge of her real parents because it was concealed from her. Appellant was subsequently convicted of count one for concealment or detention of a child in violation of a custody order under Cal. Penal Code § 278.5 and count two for felonious false imprisonment under Cal. Penal Code §§ 236, 237. The lower court imposed the aggravated term of three years in state prison to the conviction in count two and ordered stayed the imposition of sentence as to count one. The court affirmed the lower court's conviction and sentencing and held that under §§ 236 and 237, when the victim was a child of tender years, it was sufficient if the crime was effected by violence, menace, fraud, or deceit directed to the custodial parent rather than to the child. The court noted that the fact that appellant was the father and not a stranger committing the crime did not demand a separate penal response.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's conviction and sentencing of defendant for one count of concealment or detention of a child in violation of a custody order and one count of felonious false imprisonment. The court held that under the statutory requirement, it was sufficient, when the victim was a child of tender years, that the crime be effected by violence, menace, fraud, or deceit directed to the custodial parent rather than the child.

 

893 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Fresno Fire Fighters v. Jernagan


No. F004993


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


177 Cal. App. 3d 403; 222 Cal. Rptr. 886; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2559



February 7, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a Rehearing were Denied March 5, 1986, and Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 21, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 269701-9, Hollis G. Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed insofar as Fire Fighters' claim to interest earned on cash deposits in civil actions. The judgment is reversed insofar as Fire Fighters' claim to a return of interest earned on cash bail deposits in criminal actions and the award of attorney's fees to Fire Fighters under the private attorney general doctrine. The matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to reevaluate Fire Fighters' right to attorney's fees according to Code of Civil Procedure section 1021.5.

Each party to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant controller sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which awarded respondent union interest earned on their monies deposited in civil and criminal actions under state law.


OVERVIEW: Respondent union, who represented all persons with cash undertakings, cash bail and other category cash depositors, filed a class action suit to obtain interest on all cash funds deposited with the California court system in civil and criminal cases. The trial court awarded interest be paid and awarded respondents' attorney's fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §1021.5. On appeal, the court affirmed the payment of interest on all cash deposits in civil cases because Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 573 mandated that the funds be placed in a special interest-bearing account for the benefit of the owner, and that the court was in control of the deposited funds until returned, with interest, to the owner. The court reversed the trial court's decision which awarded respondents interest on bail money in criminal cases because the legislature had provided a statutory scheme which required, at the direction of the board of supervisors, that the money be used to support the courts in the county where collected. The court remanded back to the trial court for the issue of attorney's fees with instructions that they reevaluate the award according to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the judgment from the trial court for respondent unions, which awarded interest earned on cash deposits in civil actions because statute provided that all monies in civil matters be placed in special interest-bearing account for the benefit of the owner. The court reversed for interest awarded on bail deposits in criminal actions because there was a statutory scheme for the allocation of the interest on bail money.

 

894 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Franco


No. F003458


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


177 Cal. App. 3d 422; 222 Cal. Rptr. 903; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2561



February 7, 1986 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of the "Guilt Phase" portion of the Statement of Facts, and part II.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 300649-1, Mario G. Olmos and Hollis G. Best, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which convicted him of false imprisonment and assault with a deadly weapon with force likely to produce great bodily injury in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 236, 245(a)(1), finding that defendant was legally sane at the time of the commission of the offenses under a conjunctive interpretation of the insanity test provided by Cal. Penal Code § 25(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of false imprisonment and assault with a deadly weapon other than a firearm with force likely to produce great bodily injury in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 236, 245(a)(1). The trial court found that defendant was sane at the time of the commission of the offenses, holding that he failed to establish both prongs of the insanity test provided in Cal. Penal Code § 25(b). On appeal, the court held that defendant was required only to prove either that he was incapable of knowing or understanding the nature and quality of his act or that he was incapable of distinguishing right from wrong at the time of the commission of the offenses under § 25(b). However, the court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that the trial court's error in requiring defendant to satisfy both prongs of the § 25(b) test was harmless because the only evidence of record was that defendant was sane under either and both prongs of § 25(b) based upon witness testimony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for false imprisonment and assault with a deadly weapon, holding that although the trial court erred in requiring defendant to show both that he was incapable of knowing or understanding the nature and quality of his act and of distinguishing right from wrong, the error was harmless because the only evidence was that defendant was sane under either and both prongs of the insanity test.

Cunningham v. Superior Court


No. B004588


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


177 Cal. App. 3d 336; 222 Cal. Rptr. 854; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2554



February 6, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 5, 1986, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. The Petition of Real Party in Interest County of Ventura for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 8, 1986.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of certiorari issue compelling respondeat superior court to vacate its order of February 3, 1984, appointing Cunningham as counsel for Martinez and to vacate its order of March 28, 1984, finding petitioner to be in contempt of court. Let said writ further provide that respondent court is ordered to appoint qualified counsel who is willing to represent Martinez without compensation within 30 days after the finality of this opinion. If the court is unsuccessful in so appointing counsel, the court may not proceed with the action unless the county or state provides reasonable compensation for appointed counsel for Martinez.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney challenged an order of respondent Superior Court of Ventura County (California), ordering him to represent real parties in interest, county and indigent defendant in a paternity suit, and an order holding him in contempt.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney, whose practice was limited to personal injury cases, was held in contempt by respondent, the Superior Court of Ventura County, for refusing to represent real parties in interest, the county and an indigent defendant, without compensation. Petitioner argued that he was denied his right to equal protection of the law. On appeal, the court issued a writ ordering respondent to vacate its orders appointing petitioner as counsel and holding him in contempt. The court held that petitioner established the first prerequisite to a meritorious claim under the equal protection clause: a showing that the state adopted a classification that affected two or more similarly situated groups in an unequal manner. The right to pursue one's chosen profession was not a fundamental right, therefore, the court applied a rational relationship test to the trial court's appointment. Not all attorneys were required to participate in the pro bono work, and other groups such as grocers, were not required to provide food to the poor. Although the state had a legitimate function assisting the poor, that goal could not be accomplished at petitioner's expense.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ compelling respondent Superior Court of Ventura County (California), to vacate its orders appointing petitioner attorney as counsel for real parties in interest, the county and indigent defendant, and finding him in contempt of court. Respondent was to appoint qualified counsel willing to accept no fee, and if unable to do so, the trial court could not proceed unless the county or state paid reasonable compensation.

Cohen v. Board of Supervisors


No. A015565.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


177 Cal. App. 3d 141; 222 Cal. Rptr. 795; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2535



February 4, 1986.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Reprinted as mod. 178 Cal. App. 3d 447.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco, No. 784036, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, resident taxpayer and attorney, challenged an order of the Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco (California), that denied their request for a preliminary injunction against the enforcement of an ordinance regulating escort services, S.F. Cal. Municipal Police Code §§ 1074.1-1074.30, by respondents, Board of Supervisors of the City and County of San Francisco.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, resident taxpayer and attorney, brought an action that requested a preliminary injunction against enforcement of an ordinance regulating escort services, pursuant to S.F. Cal. Municipal Police Code §§ 1074.1-1074.30, by respondent board. On review, the court affirmed the order in favor of respondents. The court concluded that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in implicitly ruling against appellants on the interim harm factor. The court found that appellants showed no harm that resulted from enforcement of the ordinance. Appellant resident taxpayer failed to assert any threat or injury to himself personally from enforcement of the ordinance. Moreover, appellant attorney's claim that enforcement of the ordinance would result in prosecutions of his law practice was without merit. Respondents proffered testimony by a police officer that indicated the need to regulate escort services. The court found apparent harm in the form of criminal activity such as prostitution that would impact respondents if enforcement of the ordinance was enjoined. Therefore, the court did not address the merits of appellants' constitutional arguments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied the request of appellants, resident taxpayer and attorney, for a preliminary injunction against enforcement of an ordinance regulating escort services by respondent board. The court concluded that there was no abuse of discretion because appellants showed no interim harm and respondents would incur harm if the ordinance's enforcement was enjoined.

 

897 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cohen v. Bd. of Supervisors


No. A015565


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


178 Cal. App. 3d 447; 225 Cal. Rptr. 114; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2668



February 4, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 6, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco, No. 784036, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of September 18, 1981, denying the application for preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a resident taxpayer and an attorney, sought review of the denial by the Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco (California), of a preliminary injunction against enforcement of an ordinance regulating escort services in an action against respondent city board of supervisors.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, a resident taxpayer and an attorney, sought a preliminary injunction against respondent city board of supervisors and against enforcement of the City of San Francisco, Cal., Municipal Police Code §§ 1074.1 - 1074.30, an ordinance which imposed a permit requirement upon those engaged in the operation of an escort service. Appellant resident taxpayer sought to enjoin the illegal expenditure of public funds, while appellant attorney sought to protect himself from violating the ordinance as a practicing attorney. The cause had been transferred with directions to determine whether the lower court had abused its discretion by failing to satisfy the "interim harm" factor necessary for issuing injunctions. The court affirmed the order denying the preliminary injunction and concluded that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in finding impliedly against appellants on the interim harm factor. In determining whether a preliminary injunction was to be issued, the court compared the interim harm that appellants would have sustained if the injunction were denied to the harm respondent would have suffered if the preliminary injunction were granted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of a preliminary injunction against enforcement of an escort service ordinance in action by appellants, a resident taxpayer and an attorney, against respondent city board of supervisors because the trial court had impliedly found that the interim harm to appellants caused by denying the injunction was less than the harm to the city and the county if the ordinance were not enforced.

Lewis v. City of Hayward


No. A028473


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


177 Cal. App. 3d 103; 222 Cal. Rptr. 781; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2531



February 3, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petitions of Defendant and Respondent and Real Parties in Interest and Respondents for Review by the Supreme Court were Denied May 29, 1986. Broussard, J., Reynoso, J., and Grodin, J., were of the Opinion that the Petitions should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H-87801-4, Raymond L. Marsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reason the judgment is reversed. This determination disposes of all claims as a matter of law; thus the trial court is directed to enter judgment for appellants consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a resident and nonprofit corporation, appealed from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of defendants, city and landowners, in a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief challenging the facial constitutionality of the window cancellation provision, Cal. Gov. Code § 51282.1, of the Williamson Act, Cal. Gov. Code § 51200 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a resident and nonprofit corporation, filed a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief challenging the facial constitutionality of the window cancellation provision against defendants, city and landowners. The trial court entered summary judgment in favor of defendants and held that the window provision, Cal. Gov. Code § 51282.1, of the Williamson Act (Act), Cal. Gov. Code § 51200 et seq., did not violate Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 8. The court reversed the trial court. The court found no indication that the constitutional provision permitted the legislature to define enforceable restrictions at all, much less to define restrictions which did not actually serve to restrict the use of the land at issue and that the constitution merely allowed the legislature to specify the manner in which land was enforceably restricted. The court held that the window cancellation provision provided a windfall rather than equitable relief from a change in Supreme Court doctrine. The court concluded that the specific provisions of the Act itself supported the conclusion that the statute was intended to operate without the window provision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the award of summary judgment in favor of defendants, city and landowner, and held that Williamson Act was intended to operate without the window cancellation provision after 1982 and that the application of the provision was contrary to the California Constitution.

In re Marriage of Downes


No. A028705


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


177 Cal. App. 3d 205; 222 Cal. Rptr. 776; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2541



February 3, 1986 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Part I is not published because it does not meet the standards for publication contained in California Rules of Court, rule 976(b).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 477032, Mark E. Thomas, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded with directions to the trial court to conduct an evidentiary hearing and thereafter rule on Norma's motion under section 5124. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-husband sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted respondent ex-wife's motion for modification of the parties' divorce decree under Cal. Civ. Code § 5124, and awarded respondent a community interest in appellant's military pension.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-husband and respondent ex-wife were divorced pursuant to a dissolution decree, which awarded appellant's military retirement to him as his separate property. At the time of the dissolution, the authority of courts to divide community interests in military pensions was in flux. Interests in military pensions had not been treated as marital property. However, the Federal Uniform Services Former Spouses' Protection Act, 10 U.S.C.S. § 1408, authorized courts to treat interests in military pensions as community property. Respondents' motion for modification ensued, and the trial court granted the motion and awarded respondent a community interest in appellants' military pension. On appeal, the court reversed because Cal. Civ. Code § 5124 granted relief only to parties who mistakenly treated military pensions as separate property due to the previous treatment of military pensions as separate property. The court remanded for the trial court to make a factual finding as to whether the parties treated appellant's military pension as separate property because of bargaining or because of the prior treatment of military pensions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the award in favor of respondent ex-wife of a community interest in appellant ex-husband's military pension, and remanded with directions for the trial court to conduct an evidentiary hearing, because a factual finding was required to determine whether the parties' original dissolution decree resulted from bargaining or the mistaken treatment of military pensions as separate property.

County of Contra Costa v. State of California


Civ. No. 24357


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


177 Cal. App. 3d 62; 222 Cal. Rptr. 750; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2528



January 31, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 13, 1986. Respondents' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied April 23, 1986. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 300784, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the State of California, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) granting judgment for plaintiff county and declaring certain bills void under Cal. Const. art. XIIIB, § 6.


OVERVIEW: The trial court entered a judgment declaring that certain bills enacted during defendant state's legislative session were void and that certain challenged bills were unenforceable. The trial court reasoned that defendant state had failed to provide a subvention for reimbursement of the costs imposed on local governments as required by Cal. Const. art. XIIIB, § 6. Defendant appealed arguing that plaintiff county failed to exhaust its administrative remedies and that the contested statutes did not constitute reimbursable mandates under the constitution. The court agreed and held that a judicial action before the legislative process had been completed was premature and that the trial court was without jurisdiction until administrative remedies had been exhausted. Moreover, the court reasoned that nothing in Cal. Const. art. XIIIB rendered the statutory administrative procedure for hearing and determining claims void. Also, the court held that plaintiff failed to present evidence showing that it would be futile to submit the claims to the administrative procedure.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court granting judgment for plaintiff. The court reasoned that plaintiff failed to exhaust its administrative remedies.

California Coastal Com. v. Alves


No. A024184.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


176 Cal. App. 3d 952; 222 Cal. Rptr. 572; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2497



January 23, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing February 20, 1986; Review Denied April 24, 1986


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 255969, Thomas M. Jenkins, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant coastal commission sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which denied its claim for fines and injunctive relief against respondents, owners, developers and subdividers of land, in an action brought pursuant to the California Coastal Act of 1976, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant coastal commission challenged an order denying injunctive relief and fines against respondents, owners, developers, and subdividers of land, in an action brought pursuant to the California Coastal Act of 1976 (Act), Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30000 et seq. Appellant brought the action, alleging that respondents had unlawfully subdivided and constructed a house and other improvements on land protected by the Act. The trial court opined that respondents acquired a vested right to undertake and complete development of the subject property as was contemplated and implicitly sanctioned by the original four-lot subdivision without the need for further subdivision of the property. The court affirmed in part, holding that the doctrine of vested rights protected property owners from changes in land use regulations which occurred before completion of the owner's development project by permitting completion of the development notwithstanding an intervening change in the law that would otherwise preclude it. However, the court reversed in part and remanded the denial of injunctive relief because a violation was found to exist and could be remedied by injunction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded the judgment of the trial court, which denied appellant coastal commission's request for injunctive relief and assessment of fines against respondents, owners, developers, and subdividers of land, because the doctrine of vested rights protected respondents from changes in land use regulations as reflected in the California Coastal Act of 1976.

City of L.A. v. Superior Court


No. B005694


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


176 Cal. App. 3d 856; 222 Cal. Rptr. 562; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2486



January 21, 1986  
January 21, 1986



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue compelling respondent court to set aside its decision of May 7, 1984, in which it found the subject settlement to be in good faith, and thereafter to conduct a new hearing, on proper notice to all parties and giving each litigant an opportunity to present its factual and legal position, to determine whether the subject settlement agreement comports with the requirements of Tech-Bilt.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review of sliding scale settlement agreement between respondent plaintiff and defendants approved by respondent trial judge. Petitioner claimed that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.5, was unconstitutional. The court originally denied petitioner's requested mandate and after appeal the California Supreme Court remanded the case to the court to reconsider if the agreement was in good faith.


OVERVIEW: Respondents plaintiff and defendants entered into a sliding scale settlement agreement of medical malpractice claims in which petitioner city did not participate. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to set aside agreement, claiming that respondent trial court violated equal protection and due process and that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.5, was unconstitutional. After the court denied the writ the California Supreme Court remanded to the court to reconsider if the agreement was in good faith based on factors established by the California Supreme Court. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate to respondent trial court to set aside the order approving the settlement and remanded for respondent trial court to conduct a factual hearing to allow all litigants to present facts and law to support or challenge the agreement. The court held that Cal. Civ. Proc. OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate to respondent trial judge to vacate approval of respondent plaintiff and defendants' sliding scale settlement agreement and remanded the case to the trial court to conduct a hearing on good faith of agreement, because, although the agreement did not violate due process or equal protection, a hearing was necessary on petitioner defendants' claims that the payments were a loan.

In re Dolly A.


No. A023260


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


177 Cal. App. 3d 195; 222 Cal. Rptr. 741; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2540



January 16, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 59177, Daniel E. Creed, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders are reversed and the matter remanded to the juvenile court for a new jurisdictional hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant divorced father challenged the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), favoring respondent probation officer, declaring appellant's daughter a dependent child, and placing her in her mother's custody, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 300, in a juvenile dependency proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, divorced father of minor child made the subject of a dependency proceeding, was charged with both criminal and health and welfare violations for allegedly sexually abusing his daughter. On three occasions the jurisdictional hearing in the dependency matter was continued at appellant's request. Appellant sought review from the order granted in favor of respondent probation officer that declared appellant's daughter a dependent child of the court, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 300, and placed her in her mother's custody. The court reversed the order, holding that the trial court abused its discretion in denying appellant's continuance request where denial of a continuance forced defendant to elect between giving up his right not to be deposed as a criminal defendant and his right to testify on his own behalf in the proceeding to deprive him of custody of his daughter.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, holding that the trial court abused its discretion by denying a continuance request, where denial made appellant divorced father elect between giving up his right not to be deposed as criminal defendant and his right to testify on his own behalf in a dependency proceeding over his daughter.

 

904 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Delgado


No. A024304


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


176 Cal. App. 3d 666; 222 Cal. Rptr. 119; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2467



January 15, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 14, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 767284, Leland J. Lazarus, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which characterized certain real property as the separate property of respondent wife in a postjudgment order following divorce. Appellant contended that Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1, enacted while the appeal was pending, created a presumption that property held in joint tenancy was community property.


OVERVIEW: Respondent wife's mother testified that she wanted to give her real property to her married daughter alone, but, on the advice of her attorney, executed a joint tenancy deed to respondent and appellant husband. When the marriage ended in divorce, the trial court at first found that the residence was community property, but upon reconsideration, characterized it as respondent's separate property. On review, appellant challenged the finding on grounds that the mother's testimony was not weighty enough to establish an agreement and that Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1, enacted while the appeal was pending, now compelled a characterization of the residence as community property. The court held that it lacked the authority to reweigh the evidence presented in the mother's testimony. The court found that a recent state supreme court decision on when § 4800.1 became operative had created confusion. The court decided not to apply § 4800.1 retroactively and affirmed the trial court's judgment that the residence was respondent's separate property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's finding that the residence given to respondent wife by her mother was respondent's separate property even though the deed conveying the property created a joint tenancy in respondent and her former spouse.

People v. Conner


No. F003794


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


176 Cal. App. 3d 716; 222 Cal. Rptr. 311; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2472



January 15, 1986 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II, III, V, VI and VII.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified January 17, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 289344-4, Dwayne Keyes and Mario G. Olmos, Judges. +


+ Judge Olmos heard the Hitch motion [People v. Hitch (1974) 12 Cal.3d 641 (117 Cal.Rptr. 9, 527 P.2d 361)]; Judge Keyes was the trial judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, as corrected, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his convictions by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), for two manslaughter convictions and two felony drunk driving convictions. The trial court stayed the sentences on the manslaughter convictions and on one felony drunk driving conviction. Appellant alleged that the stay imposed on the felony drunk driving conviction should reflect that the conviction could not be used for penal or administrative purposes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced to state prison for three years each on two manslaughter convictions and four years each for two felony drunk driving convictions. Cal. Veh. Code § 23153(a), (b). The trial court stayed the sentences on the two manslaughter convictions and one felony drunk driving conviction, § 23153(a). Appellant alleged that the stay imposed by the trial court on § 23153(a), should reflect that the conviction could not be used for penal or administrative purposes. On appeal, the court agreed and affirmed his convictions as corrected. The court held that the stay of the sentence became permanent at the time appellant completed service of sentence, and the stay of the use of the conviction for penal and administrative purposes remained conditional upon the continued validity of the conviction of the "unstayed" term.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions and held that the judgment as to the felony drunk driving conviction that was stayed was corrected to stay the use of the conviction for sentencing and administrative use conditionally upon the continued validity of the conviction of the "unstayed" term.

Hayman v. Block


No. B014508


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


176 Cal. App. 3d 629; 222 Cal. Rptr. 293; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2465



January 14, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 13, 1986. Thompson, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 8, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 452482, Norman L. Epstein, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a summary judgment entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in favor of respondent sheriff. Appellants' complaint claimed that respondent violated his oath and duties through discriminatory and unconstitutional enforcement of Cal. Penal Code §§ 370,372, public nuisance statutes, to harass prostitutes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers filed an action against respondent sheriff, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 56(a), 1060 and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 in which they sought injunctive and declaratory relief. Appellants claimed that respondent violated his oath and duties through a practice and policy of discriminatory and unconstitutional enforcement of Cal. Penal Code §§ 370, 372, public nuisance statutes, and through the use of discriminatory arrest and bail procedures. Appellants claimed that the practices were part of an effort to suppress streetwalking prostitution. The trial court entered a summary judgment in favor of respondent. On review, the court affirmed, reasoning that the trial court was bound to consider the competency of the evidence presented. The court held that appellants presented no competent evidence to contradict evidence presented by respondent and the facts that appellants stipulated were true. The court agreed with the trial court that appellants' declarations or depositions did not raise any issues of fact about whether respondent was abusing Cal. Penal Code §§ 370, 372 to harass prostitutes.


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court's summary judgment in favor of respondent sheriff in appellant taxpayers' action was properly granted. The court held that appellants produced no credible evidence sufficient to raise triable issues of fact about whether respondent used public nuisance statutes to harass prostitutes.

 

907 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Lopez


No. F004417


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


176 Cal. App. 3d 545; 222 Cal. Rptr. 101; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2458



January 10, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, No. 27008, Len M. McGillivray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which convicted defendant of mayhem pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 203, and found that he used a deadly and dangerous weapon within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b).


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of mayhem pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 203, and found that he used a deadly and dangerous weapon within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b). Defendant challenged the conviction because of error in the jury instructions. On appeal, the court disagreed with defendant's argument that the 1979 revision of Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 9.30 impermissibly broadened the statutory definition of mayhem by stating that a malicious intent could be an intent to vex or annoy. The court found that the inclusion of "vex" or "annoy" in the jury instructions was statutorily authorized by Cal. Penal Code § 7(4) definition of "maliciously." The court overruled defendant's contention that the jury instruction violated the doctrine of stare decisis because the cases defendant cited did not consider whether the intent to vex or annoy was a sufficient intent for sustaining a mayhem conviction. The court also rejected defendant's argument that the terms "vex" or "annoy" were vague and uncertain that they violated constitutional principles of due process. The court affirmed appellant's conviction because there was no error in the jury instructions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for mayhem, and affirmed the trial court's finding that he used a deadly and dangerous weapon. It found that the use of jury instructions that included the terms "vex" and "annoy" did not impermissibly broaden the statutory definition of mayhem, finding that the inclusion of such terms was statutorily authorized.

Cunningham v. Superior Court


No. B004588.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


176 Cal. App. 3d 349; 222 Cal. Rptr. 44; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2443



January 3, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Jan. 30, 1986 (See 177 Cal. App. 3d 336)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought review of an order of respondent Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which held petitioner in contempt when he refused to represent an indigent defendant in a paternity action instituted by real party in interest county.


OVERVIEW: Respondent trial court held petitioner attorney in contempt for refusing to represent an indigent defendant in a paternity case. Petitioner argued that he had never handled a paternity case, and that forcing him to represent the defendant was an unconstitutional denial of his right to equal protection of the law. The court granted certiorari and ordered the trial court to vacate its orders of appointment and contempt. The court reviewed the appointment under the rational relationship test because the right to pursue a profession was not a fundamental right for the purpose of invoking the strict scrutiny test. The court held that it was a denial of equal protection when the government charged the cost of a state function, conducted for the benefit of the public, to a particular class of persons. The court held that when the state prosecuted a paternity action against an indigent, it must bear the expenses necessary to comply with the provisions of the state and federal constitutions, which included the cost of counsel for the indigent defendant. Compelled representation without compensation could deprive the indigent of effective assistance of counsel.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of mandate in favor of petitioner attorney and ordered respondent trial court to vacate its orders appointing petitioner as counsel and finding petitioner to be in contempt of court. The court held that to require petitioner to provide legal services without compensation was a violation of petitioner's right to equal protection of the law.

 

909 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Sagaser v. McCarthy


No. F005551


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


176 Cal. App. 3d 288; 221 Cal. Rptr. 746; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2439



January 2, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kings County, No. 39666, Eugene W. Krum, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The motion to dismiss the appeal as moot is granted. The matter is remanded to the trial court for a factual hearing and a determination of what attorneys' fees, if any, are allowable. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal. ( Youngblood v. Board of Supervisors (1978) 22 Cal.3d 644, 657, fn. 13 [150 Cal.Rptr. 242, 586 P.2d 556]; Bell v. Board of Supervisors (1976) 55 Cal.App.3d 629, 637 [127 Cal.Rptr. 757].) "[An] appellate court has inherent power to make any award of costs which it deems proper when the interests of justice so require." (Ibid.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, class of property owners, challenged a judgment in favor of respondents, public officials, from the Superior Court of Kings County (California), which denied appellants' writ of mandate for violations associated with the failure to furnish information from public records and a threatened waste of public funds.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, class of property owners, filed a writ of mandate and complaint against respondents, public officials, after appellants unsuccessfully challenged the adequacy of an environmental impact report, regarding consideration of possible sites for a new prison. The trial court ruled in favor of respondents. While appellants sought judicial relief through the appellate process, the state legislature passed a prison funding bill which exempted the proposed prison in question from compliance with the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA). The bill carried an urgency clause and became effective immediately after the governor's signature. Respondents filed a motion to dismiss the appeal as moot. The court held that the bill in question was constitutional and valid legislation, and it dismissed the appeal as moot. Because the immediate expansion of the prison system was necessary to protect public health and safety, and the means employed by the legislature were rationally related to the desired objective, retroactive application of the bill and elimination of CEQA compliance did not violate the due process and equal protection clauses under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court granted respondents' motion to dismiss the appeal because the passage of a bill in the legislature, which the court found was constitutional, rendered the appeal moot. The matter was remanded to the trial court for determination of whether appellants, a class of property owners, were entitled to attorneys' fees.

In re Leday


No. A027744


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


177 Cal. App. 3d 461; 221 Cal. Rptr. 398; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2958



December 27, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied March 13, 1986.



DISPOSITION: Based upon Ramirez the petition herein is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the supreme court the Court of Appeals of California, First Appellate Division, Division Five was to reconsider defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus to determine if the extension of his recommitment to prison as a parole violator pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 3057(c), was an invalid ex post facto punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought a writ of habeas corpus and contended that the extension of his recommitment to prison as a parole violator under Cal. Penal Code § 3057(c), was an invalid ex post facto punishment. The court held that there were two elements for a criminal or penal law to be ex post facto: it must be applicable to the event that occurred before its enactment, and it must disadvantage the offender affected by it. The court found that petitioner was imprisoned for a crime that occurred prior to the statutory change that required additional custodial time for conduct that occurred subsequent to the initial incarceration. The court held that because petitioner's improper conduct occurred after the change in the law and because he was on notice that a future misbehavior could result in extra custodial time, the law was not an ex post facto punishment and denied his writ.


OUTCOME: defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied. The extension of petitioner's recommitment to prison as a parole violator was not an ex post facto punishment because his improper conduct occurred after the enactment of the law and he had notice that a future misbehavior would result in extra time.

 

911 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Pacheco


No. A029074


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


176 Cal. App. 3d 100; 221 Cal. Rptr. 369; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2925



December 23, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H-6459, Stanley P. Golde, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The record is not clear whether Pacheco had a reasonable expectation that, if he pleaded guilty, the trial judge would strike the prior convictions and grant him probation. Accordingly, we remand with instructions that the trial court conduct a hearing to determine whether Pacheco's guilty plea was based upon such a reasonable expectation. If, after the hearing, the trial court determines that Pacheco's guilty plea was so based, then he must be given an opportunity to withdraw his guilty plea and further proceedings may then be had as appropriate. In any event, Pacheco must be advised of his rights concerning the prior convictions before he may admit them. If, after admonishment, proper waivers are obtained, then Pacheco must be sentenced according to law. The cause is reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which struck defendant's two prior conviction allegations pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385 and granted defendant probation. Defendant had pled guilty to two counts of sale of heroin, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352, and admitted that he had been convicted twice before of selling controlled substances.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to two counts of sale of heroin, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352, and admitted allegations that he was convicted twice before of selling controlled substances. The trial court, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385 and over the people's objection, struck the prior convictions and granted defendant probation. The court reversed this judgment because it found that the legislature intended the language of Cal. Penal Code § 1203.07 to be mandatory. Cal. Penal Code § 1203.07(a)(3) prohibited the granting of probation to any person convicted of selling heroin who had previously been convicted of selling a controlled substance. Thus, the court held that § 1203.07 was intended to eliminate a trial court's § 1385 discretion to strike allegations or findings of a defendant's ineligibility for probation. The court remanded the matter because it found that the trial court failed to adequately admonish and obtain waivers in proper form from defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court striking defendant's two prior conviction allegations and granting him probation because it found that California law prohibited a trial court's use of discretion to strike allegations or findings of a defendant's ineligibility for probation. The court remanded the matter to allow the trial court to admonish defendant and obtain proper waivers.
