Collier v. Menzel


No. B009565


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


176 Cal. App. 3d 24; 221 Cal. Rptr. 110; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2919



December 20, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 152904, Ronald C. Stevens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment appealed from is reversed and remanded to superior court for the issuance of a writ and the determination of costs at trial and on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandate seeking to compel respondent county clerk-recorder to allow appellants to register to vote.


OVERVIEW: Appellant citizens, a group of homeless individuals, listed as their residence on affidavits of registration to vote the address of a public park. When respondent county clerk-recorder refused to process the applications on the basis of an insufficient residence address, appellants sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to accept their applications. The trial court denied their petition. The court reversed this denial and held that appellants complied with applicable state registration requirements because the designation of a public park as a residence for voting purposes qualified as a place of fixed habitation, and so long as appellants actually resided there, they could register to vote in that precinct. The court also held that respondent's refusal to register appellants violated appellants' constitutional rights to equal protection. The court noted that the right to vote was a fundamental right and classifications denying it deserved strict scrutiny. Finding that respondent had not asserted sufficient grounds to justify its refusal to register persons establishing their home as the park at issue, the court remanded the matter so that the requested writ could be issued.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant citizens' petition for writ of mandate which sought to compel respondent county clerk-recorder to allow them to register to vote because it found that such denial violated appellants' constitutional rights to equal protection. The court remanded to the trial court for issuance of the writ.

 

913 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Ingersoll v. Palmer


No. A029680.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


193 Cal. App. 3d 617; 221 Cal. Rptr. 659; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2897



December 19, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted April 3, 1986 (See 43 Cal. 3d 1321)


PRIOR HISTORY: This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of December 19, 1985, previously reported at 175 Cal. App. 3d 1028 and Reprinted at 184 Cal. App. 3d 1198.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers challenged the establishment of roadblock sobriety checkpoints by respondents police department, through a writ of mandate (California), and petitioners sought to stay their operation under both the federal and the California Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: This petition was brought by petitioner taxpayers to challenge the establishment of roadblock sobriety checkpoints in several California counties, set up by respondent police department. At the time the petition was filed, most such roadblocks were only threatened, and petitioners sought to stay their operation. To determine whether such checkpoints were constitutionally reasonable the test was similar under both the federal and the California Constitutions. The court held that it had to balance the gravity of the governmental interest or public concerns served by the roadblocks, the degree to which the roadblocks advanced these concerns, and the severity of the interference with individual liberty resulting from the roadblocks. Though intrusive and burdensome to the public even when properly conducted, the degree of intrusion was justified by the magnitude of the drunk driving hazard and the potential for deterrence and detection. Petitioners having presented no showing that the checkpoints in issue violated these principles, the alternative writ was discharged and the petition for peremptory writ was denied.


OUTCOME: The peremptory writ of mandate sought by petitioner taxpayers to stay the operation of roadblock sobriety checkpoints by respondent police department was denied. The court held that the checkpoints were constitutional when conducted within specific guidelines delineated by the court and other authorities, and were a valid deterrent for drunk drivers.

 

914 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Deluxe Theater & Bookstore v. City of San Diego


No. D002564


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


175 Cal. App. 3d 980; 221 Cal. Rptr. 100; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2893



December 18, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 528086, Sheridan E. Reed, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying a preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adult theater sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied a preliminary injunction on the enforcement of respondent city's ordinance prohibiting partially or fully enclosed peep show booths showing sexually explicit films.


OVERVIEW: Appellant adult theater had 30 partially enclosed private peep show booths and challenged the constitutionality of an ordinance enacted by respondent city which prohibited partially or fully enclosed peep show booths. The trial court refused to issue a preliminary injunction against respondent. The court affirmed and held that the ordinance was an acceptable time, place, and manner regulation of peep show businesses because the governmental interest in regulating appellant for purposes of health, safety, and public welfare was unrelated to the suppression of free expression. The court held that the requirement of the ordinance that the interior of the booths not be partially or fully enclosed did not restrict the freedoms under U.S. Const. amend. I because there was no restriction on either the content of the pictures or their dissemination. The court rejected appellant's contention that the ordinance infringed on the right of their customers to engage in sexual conduct in partially or fully enclosed booths, under a constitutional right of privacy, because the government had a legitimate interest in seeking to prevent masturbation in locales outside of private homes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court denying appellant adult theater's request for preliminary injunction on the enforcement of respondent city's ordinance that prohibited partially or fully enclosed peep show booths. The court held that the ordinance was an acceptable time, place, and manner regulation because the governmental interest in health, safety, and welfare was unrelated to suppression of free expression.

 

915 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Committee of Seven Thousand v. Superior Court


No. G003323.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


194 Cal. App. 3d 1076; 221 Cal. Rptr. 616; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2943



December 13, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted April 17, 1986 (See 45 Cal. 3d 491)


PRIOR HISTORY: This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of December 13, 1985, previously reported at 176 Cal. App. 3d 275 and Reprinted at 185 Cal. App. 3d 253.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner electorate committee sought a peremptory writ against respondent Court of Orange County's order barring an election on a municipal freeway development financing fee issue before real party in interest city under Cal. Gov't Code § 6648.3.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner electorate committee sought a preemptory writ against respondent court's order barring a referendum election on a municipal development freeway financing fee issue before real party in interest city under Cal. Gov't Code under § 6648.3. The court denied the peremptory writ, explaining that the matter of major freeway financing was a subject of statewide concern to which the ordinary initiative and referendum processes did not apply. The court noted that it was only in matters of statewide concern that the legislature could be held to delegate a particular authority to a governing board to prevent application of the referendum process to disenfranchise the electorate. The court concluded that such circumstances existed in the present action because the electorate of real party in interest lacked power to enact the proposed ordinance when the freeway funding initiative was a matter of public concern, which was specifically entrusted to the authority of the city council under the statute. The court reasoned that the three large freeway corridors trisecting the state's second largest county was obviously a matter of statewide concern.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner electorate committee's motion for a peremptory writ against respondent's court order barring a referendum election on real party in interest city's decision on a freeway financing issue. The court held that the electorate lacked authority to enact such initiatives that affected a matter of statewide concern, and which the legislature specifically delegated to the exclusive authority of the city council.

 

916 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Baumann


No. D001515


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


176 Cal. App. 3d 67; 222 Cal. Rptr. 32; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2923



December 13, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 31, 1985, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied March 26, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRV 8781, Michael B. Harris, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is in all respects affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of restitution entered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which was entered pursuant to defendant pleading guilty to one count of embezzlement, and as a precursor to probation. Defendant contended the order was excessive in that it was based not only on the count to which she pleaded guilty or on the dismissed counts alleged in the information, but also on uncharged offenses as well.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a bookkeeper with a firm owned by the victim. Defendant was responsible for issuing company checks. She pleaded guilty to one count of embezzlement. As a condition of her probation, she was ordered to pay restitution to the victim. The victim produced 75 unauthorized checks made payable to defendant's husband's business. The trial court found that $ 20, 419 constituted the substantiated loss of the victim. Defendant contended that the restitution order made as a condition of probation was not authorized by her "Harvey" waiver. The court disagreed and found that the trial court specifically advised defendant and defendant specifically agreed that unfiled charges related to the case could be considered in determining whether to grant probation or impose sentence. Defendant also alleged that the restitution order, as a condition of probation, was unconstitutional in a variety of ways. The court disagreed. There was no denial of due process. Defendant then alleged that the restitution order was invalid because the trial court failed to make express findings that it was primarily intended to rehabilitate. The court disagreed and affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and condition of probation ordering restitution to the victim, finding no merit to defendant's arguments. Defendant specifically agreed that unfiled charges related to the case could be considered in determining probation. In addition, defendant was not denied due process of law in the restitution proceeding.

 

917 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Gatewood v. Bd. of Ret.


No. D002365


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


175 Cal. App. 3d 311; 220 Cal. Rptr. 724; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2836



December 5, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 522277, Milton Milkes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed with directions to grant the petition for writ of mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged the denial of a writ of mandate by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), regarding an underlying action that arose when petitioner applied for, and was denied, a service-connected disability retirement under Cal. Gov't Code § 31720 by respondent county employees board of retirement. Appellant contended that changes in the law impaired his contract rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee applied for and was denied a service-connected disability retirement under Cal. Gov't Code § 31720 by respondent county employees board of retirement. The trial court denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate. On appeal, the court determined that respondent and the trial court had properly applied the amendment to Cal. Gov't Code § 31720 when they tested for service-connected disability; the amended version of § 31720 had semantic, not substantive, changes; and there was nothing in the record to indicate the trial court had not independently judged the evidence. The evidence, however, did not substantially support the superior court's findings that appellant's disability was not service-connected. Two of the three psychiatrists who were expert witnesses unequivocally stated that appellant's paranoid psychological condition was work-related. The third witness, notwithstanding a contrary report, provided testimony that clearly showed that appellant's condition arose out of and was substantially contributed to by his employment. The trial court judgment was reversed with directions to grant the petition for writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: Judgment was reversed with directions to grant the petition for writ of mandate. The court found that the appropriate test for disability was applied by the trial court and respondent county employees board of retirement, and the statutory changes had no effect on appellant employee's case. The evidence, however, did not substantially support the superior court's findings that appellant's disability was not service-connected.

L.A. Country Club v. Pope


No. B009247


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


175 Cal. App. 3d 278; 220 Cal. Rptr. 584; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2834



December 5, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 26, 1985, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 20, 1986. Mosk, J., and Lucas, J., did not participate therein. Reynoso, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C434778, Bruce R. Geernaert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tax assessor challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent taxpayers' motion for summary judgment in respondents' declaratory action regarding the proper taxable value for which appellant should assess respondents' tax rates.


OVERVIEW: Respondent taxpayers sought declaratory relief from an assessment procedure adopted by appellant tax assessor, in which he sought to reassess respondents' taxes at current values in accordance with Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 1 and Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 52. Appellant contended Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 10 removed nonprofit golf courses, such as respondents, from application of Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 2, thus the valuation rollback provision no longer applied. Additionally, appellant contended § 52 intended to impact the assessment of golf courses. Respondents filed a complaint for declaratory relief. Both parties moved for summary judgment and the trial court granted respondents' motion. Appellant challenged the trial court's judgment. The court affirmed the trial court's decision and held that no conflict existed between the two constitutional provisions and therefore, appellant should have assessed respondents' taxes as he did in the past. The court reasoned that it agreed with appellant's contention that the intent of the legislature by enacting § 52 was to impact the assessment of taxes, however, such intent was not achieved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed trial court's judgment granting respondent taxpayers' motion for summary judgment in its declaratory action regarding the proper determination for the taxable rate. The court held that appellant tax assessor used an improper value because the statutory amendments and provisions did not remove respondents from the application of the valuation rollback provision therefore, summary judgment was properly granted to respondents.

Troensegaard v. Silvercrest Indus.


No. A028937


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


175 Cal. App. 3d 218; 220 Cal. Rptr. 712; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2826; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P10,859



December 4, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 3, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 86575, Christopher C. Cottle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment will be modified by striking the $ 55,000 punitive damage award and by giving defendant Silvercrest Industries, Inc., credit for the $ 6,000 paid by other defendants. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed. The parties will stand their respective costs of the appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant manufacturer appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which entered a judgment against it for compensatory damages, punitive damages, and a certain amount for a civil penalty, in plaintiff's personal injury action for damages based on breach of express warranty, products liability, and fraudulent concealment in relation to a mobile home she purchased.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff purchased a mobilehome manufactured by defendant; after moving into it, plaintiff noticed an unpleasant odor, and began to develop headaches, and eye, nose, and throat irritation. A test found that formaldehyde was present in the mobilehome above the recommended levels. Plaintiff eventually filed suit, alleging breach of express warranty, product liability, and fraudulent concealment. The trial court rendered judgment against defendant for compensatory damages, punitive damages, and a civil penalty; plaintiff was also awarded attorney's fees and costs. On appeal, the court found that a punitive damage award was proper, in light of defendant's intentional concealment from plaintiff of the high level of formaldehyde fumes found in the mobilehome, amongst other findings. The court also held that there was substantial evidence of defendant's willfulness to justify the imposition of a civil penalty under Cal. Civ. Code § 1794, but also noted that plaintiff had in effect elected to waive punitive damages under Cal. Civ. Code § 3294, by seeking the civil penalty. As plaintiff received both, she received a double recovery; the punitive damages award was ordered as stricken.


OUTCOME: Award of punitive damages in plaintiff's personal injury action based on breach of express warranty, products liability, and fraudulent concealment as to a mobile home by defendant manufacturer, was ordered stricken as plaintiff had effectively elected to waive punitive damages by seeking a civil penalty, which was justified by substantial evidence. As so modified, the order awarding compensatory costs, attorney's fees, and costs was affirmed.

 

920 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Swedberg v. Christiana Cmty. Builders


No. D001136


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


175 Cal. App. 3d 138; 220 Cal. Rptr. 544; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2816



November 27, 1985 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976.1 and 976(b), this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of sections II B through G.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied December 16, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 446378, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff home buyers appealed a ruling from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which awarded summary judgment to defendants, real estate company and its parent, on plaintiffs' action for damages for misrepresentations by a realtor. The trial court found that plaintiffs' failure to respond to requests for admission resulted in deemed admissions under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 2033(a) that could be used by both defendants.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff home buyers brought suit against defendants, real estate company and its parent, claiming damages for misrepresentations by a realtor who allegedly failed to tell plaintiffs various details of the assumable financing on a home. Defendant real estate company served a request for admissions on plaintiffs, who failed to respond, and then a second request, to which plaintiffs responded late. Defendant real estate company successfully moved for the admissions to be deemed admitted under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 2033(a), and both defendants moved for summary judgment, which the trial court granted. Plaintiffs appealed but the court affirmed, finding that there was no exception under § 2033(a) barring the use of deemed admissions by a non-requesting party, in this case defendant real estate company. The court further found that if the legislature had intended for admissions not to be used by all parties to a single action, it would have included that limitation in § 2033(c). The court held that the absence of such a limitation reflected legislative intent that the deemed admissions could be used by both defendants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment awarded to defendants, real estate company and its parent, in plaintiff home buyers' action for damages. The court found that the legislature intended that all parties to a single action could make use of deemed admissions.

 

921 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Peery v. Superior Court


No. D003580


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


174 Cal. App. 3d 1085; 219 Cal. Rptr. 882; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2803



November 25, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The alternative writ is discharged, and the petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father sought a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent, the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), to relinquish jurisdiction, enforce a Louisiana custody decree, and order the immediate return of a minor child from the mother, the real party in interest, to petitioner on the grounds that the prior order of the lower court was invalid for lack of jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: A mother, the real party in interest, filed a dissolution action in California and a stipulated interlocutory judgment was entered awarding her custody. Petitioner father later filed a dissolution action in Louisiana and obtained an order of custody in that state. The mother applied to respondent California court seeking a show cause order and respondent found that it had jurisdiction. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate seeking to overturn that finding. The court denied the petition for peremptory writ of mandate, holding that the Louisiana court lacked jurisdiction to redetermine custody under the Uniform Child Custody Jurisdiction Act (UCCJA), because of the pendency of the custody proceeding here. The Louisiana decree was not entitled to full faith and credit under Cal. Civ. Code § 5162, because it was not rendered under factual circumstances meeting the jurisdictional standards of the title nor did the rendering court have UCCJA jurisdiction, under Cal. Civ. Code § 5163. The mother's failure to appeal the Louisiana decision was immaterial.


OUTCOME: The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate was denied, because the California court had subject matter jurisdiction over the custody of the child as to petitioner father and mother, the real party in interest, who participated in the proceeding and consented to the judgment. The Louisiana court had no power to permit a collateral attack on that judgment and its decree was not entitled to full faith and credit.

Int'l Ass'n of Fire Fighters v. City of Oakland


Nos. A013812, A013813


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


174 Cal. App. 3d 687; 220 Cal. Rptr. 256; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2774; 121 L.R.R.M. 3426



November 21, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 478487-4 and 478557-6, Gordon L. Minder, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff union challenged an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which dismissed plaintiff's complaints against defendants, local government units, to invalidate propositions approved by the voters that limited membership in the police and fire retirement system and that repealed a city charter that provided police and fire fighters with automatic salary adjustments.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, local governments, placed propositions before the voters to limit membership in the police and fire retirement system and to repeal a city charter that provided automatic salary adjustments to police officers and fire fighters. The proposition was approved by the voters and plaintiff, union, filed an action to have the amended charters declared unlawful because they violated the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act. Cal. Gov't Code § 3505 (Act). The trial court dismissed the complaint because the relief sought by the plaintiffs, to invalidate charter amendments, could only be sought by the Attorney General through the initiation of quo warranto proceedings pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 804. Plaintiffs claimed on appeal that the amended charters violated the Act and that the attorney general could not represent its interests. During the pendency of the appeal the California Supreme Court ruled a city council must comply with the requirements of the Act before it proposes amending its charter to affect the terms and conditions of public employment. The court nevertheless rejected plaintiff's claim on appeal because only the Attorney General could bring a quo warranto action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court which dismissed plaintiff union's complaint challenging the validity of an amendment to the city's charter without compliance with the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act because only the Attorney General is authorized to institute a quo warranto proceeding the challenge city's unlawful amendment to its charter.

 

923 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Lachenmyer


No. D001582


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


174 Cal. App. 3d 558; 220 Cal. Rptr. 76; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2762



November 18, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. D173041, Earl H. Maas, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which awarded to respondent husband the parties' condominium pursuant to a retroactive application of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2, in the division of community property following an interlocutory decree of dissolution.


OVERVIEW: Following an interlocutory decree of divorce between appellant wife and respondent husband, the trial court awarded the parties' condominium to respondent pursuant to a retroactive application of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2, which provided for reimbursement of separate property contributions to the acquisition of community property in the absence of a written waiver of that right. The condominium had been respondent's separate property before he executed a quitclaim deed, during the marriage, transferring the property to appellant and respondent as joint tenants, and, thus, appellant had a vested property right in the condominium as community property. Appellant sought review and the appellate court reversed, holding that the retroactive application of § 4800.2 constituted an unconstitutional deprivation of vested property rights without due process of law. Under the former law, a spouse presumptively received no reimbursement for separate property contributions, which were viewed as gifts to the community; whereas § 4800.2 now required a written agreement. The old scheme was not unjust and plaintiff had no way to protect her interest upon retroactive application of § 4800.2.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the judgment awarding a condominium to respondent husband, in a division of community property. The trial court based its award by retroactively applying a statute providing for a reimbursement of separate property contributions to the community. However, this retroactive application unconstitutionally impaired appellant wife's vested community property interest in the condominium without due process of law.

Chodur v. Edmonds


No. D002256


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


174 Cal. App. 3d 565; 220 Cal. Rptr. 80; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2763



November 18, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 516737, Sheridan E. Reed, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant real estate agent sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his petition for peremptory writ of mandate directed at respondent real estate commissioner, which sought to set aside respondent's order revoking appellant's real estate license subject to his right to apply for a restricted license.


OVERVIEW: Respondent real estate commissioner adopted the administrative law judge's recommendation to revoke appellant real estate agent's license for dishonest dealing, but respondent modified the order to provide for the issuance of a restricted broker's license upon application. Appellant filed a petition for writ of mandate and an application of stay of administrative decision. The trial court found a sufficient showing of misconduct and denied the writ. The court affirmed the decision and held that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10177(j) use of the term "dishonest dealing" was not unconstitutionally vague. The court held that there was substantial evidence that appellant had become a fiduciary when he agreed to accept and make the payments on behalf of a client as his agent. The court declined to require a finding of fraud or deception in order to sustain dishonest dealing in violation of § 10177(j). The court held that respondent's decision revoking the license, but allowing a restricted license was not arbitrary, capricious, or patently abusive exercise of discretion.


OUTCOME: The trial court's denial of appellant real estate agent's request for a writ of mandate directing respondent real estate commissioner to set aside the revocation of appellant's real estate license as there was a sufficient showing of misconduct for dishonest dealing as appellant had a fiduciary relationship with his client, and the decision was not an arbitrary, capricious, or patently abusive exercise of discretion by respondent.

City of Oakland v. Oakland Raiders


No. A029031


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


174 Cal. App. 3d 414; 220 Cal. Rptr. 153; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2751



November 15, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 12, 1985, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 27, 1986. Broussard, J., and Panelli, J., did not Participate therein. Reynoso, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 76044, Nat A. Agliano, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), that entered a judgment in favor of defendants, football franchise and league, and found that plaintiff's proposed exercise of eminent domain to acquire defendant football franchise violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3.


OVERVIEW: In eminent domain proceedings, plaintiff city sought to acquire defendant football franchise, a franchise of defendant league. The trial court entered judgment in favor of defendants and found the plaintiff's proposed exercise of eminent domain violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. Plaintiff sought review and argued that its exercise of eminent domain power was exempt from scrutiny under the commerce clause. Defendants argued that professional football was so completely involved in interstate commerce that acquisition of a franchise by an individual state or city through eminent domain would impermissibly burden interstate commerce. The appellate court found that plaintiff claimed authority to bar indefinitely defendant football franchise from relocating. Plaintiff's proposed taking of intangible property more than indirectly regulated interstate commerce. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment because the burden that would be imposed on interstate commerce outweighed the local interest in plaintiff's proposed exercise of statutory eminent domain authority over defendant football franchise.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendants, football franchise and league, because the burden that would be imposed on interstate commerce by plaintiff city's proposed exercise of statutory eminent domain over defendant football franchise outweighed the local interest, and as such, violated the commerce clause and was unconstitutional.

Shafer v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. A028122


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


174 Cal. App. 3d 423; 220 Cal. Rptr. 59; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2752



November 15, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 11, 1985, and the Petition of Plaintiffs and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 20, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 812459, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment insofar as it provides that former section 75.5 and section 75.12 violated California Constitution, article XIII, section 1. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county assessors, defendant State Board of Equalization, and intervenor property developers challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), that found the supplemental assessments of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 75.10, 75.11 did not violate Cal. Const. art. XIII A, §§ 3, 4, but that the delayed supplemental assessment of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 75.12 violated Cal. Const. art XIII, § 1.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court found that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 75.10, 75.11 did not violate Cal. Const. art. XIII A, §§ 3, 4, but that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 75.12 violated Cal. Const. art XIII, § 1, plaintiff county assessors, defendant State Board of Equalization, and intervenor property developers sought review. Plaintiffs argued that the supplemental assessment provisions, §§ 75.10 and 75.11, imposed new ad valorem taxes. Defendant and intervenors argued that § 75.12 merely delayed the supplemental assessment until the property changed ownership. The appellate court reversed that portion of the trial court judgment that found § 75.12 violated Cal. Const. art XIII, § 1, because the legislature's decision not to subject certain property to supplemental assessment was not an exemption, as that the property would be assessed when it was sold. The appellate court otherwise affirmed the trial court judgment because the supplemental assessment provisions, §§ 75.10 and 75.11, merely imposed a new timing mechanism for valuation and collection and did not alter the tax rate or impose new taxes.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed that the supplemental assessment provisions were constitutional because the provisions merely affected the time at which the existing real property tax was calculated and imposed. However, the appellate court reversed the finding that the delayed supplemental assessment provision was unconstitutional because the provision was not an exemption, rather the assessment was merely deferred until the property was sold.

In re Marriage of Kahan

Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner wife appealed from a second interlocutory judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), on the dissolution of her marriage to respondent husband.


OVERVIEW: In this family law matter involving distribution of property, petitioner wife appealed the trial court's judgment that the family residence was community property. The evidence at trial established that petitioner purchased the home with separate property funds. The court reversed the trial court's judgment, and directed the trial court on remand to make a finding as to whether the parties had an agreement regarding co-ownership of the home. The court held that if such an agreement existed, no further proceedings would be necessary. Code § 4800.2.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment as to the family residence and remanded the cause to determine whether petitioner wife and respondent husband had an agreement regarding co-ownership of the home.

Planned Parenthood Affiliates v. Swoap


No. A031928


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


173 Cal. App. 3d 1187; 219 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2710



November 1, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 27, 1985.



DISPOSITION:  A peremptory writ shall issue, directing respondents to refrain from enforcing the unconstitutional provisions of the Budget Act 1985-1986 challenged herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, nonprofit corporations, family planning council, and state family planning services, filed a petition for writ of mandate in the Court of Appeals of California, First Appellate District, Division Two, seeking to compel respondent state officials to refrain from enforcing the Budget Act of 1985-1986, S.B. 150 § 33.35.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, nonprofit corporations, family planning council, and state family planning services, filed a petition for writ of mandate seeking to compel respondent state officials to refrain from enforcing the Budget Act of 1985-1986, S.B. 150 § 33.35. When S.B. 150 was enacted, it was discovered that the bill as adopted included § 33.35 due to staff error. Although the state legislature alerted the state governor to the error, he signed SB 150 into law. The court held that § 33.35, which received the necessary approval of the legislature and was approved by the governor, could not be impeached by extrinsic evidence that it did not genuinely reflect the will of the legislature. The court found that § 33.35 amended existing provisions of the family planning act, but violated the single subject rule. Further, even if the court had agreed with respondents that § 33.35 simply clarified funding arrangements for abortion and other services authorized under the family planning act, the court would have been nonetheless compelled to conclude that it impermissibly amended the act. The court issued the writ of mandate and found that petitioners were entitled to recover their costs.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ, directing respondent state officials to refrain from enforcing unconstitutional provisions of the budget act, because the act granted authority to a state agency that the agency did not otherwise possess, amended the family planning act, and violated the single subject rule.

Cal. Rest. Ass'n v. Henning


No. A019519


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


173 Cal. App. 3d 1069; 219 Cal. Rptr. 630; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2697; 27 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 698



October 31, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 795704, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff restaurant association appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied plaintiff's summary judgment motion, and entered judgment in favor of defendant State Labor Commissioner by declaring that Cal. Lab. Code § 93 was constitutionally valid and fully enforceable.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff restaurant association commenced a class action contesting the constitutionality of defendant State Labor Commissioner's administrative subpoena power. A trial court denied plaintiff's summary judgment motion and entered judgment in favor of defendant by declaring that Cal. Lab. Code § 93 was constitutionally valid and enforceable. Plaintiff appealed the trial court's judgment. The court reversed the trial court's judgment. The court found that defendant's admissions of the factual allegations of plaintiff's complaint, particularly the allegations regarding the existence and nature of the dispute, eliminated all questions as to the propriety of the declaratory relief. The court dismissed defendant's suggestion that subpoenas did not come within the purview of U.S. Const. amend. IV. because the use of subpoenas was an accepted and established part of the administrative process. The court found that the defects from which § 93 suffered were not hypothetical. As identified by plaintiff they were clear and present in every instance in which defendant issued an administrative subpoena. The court, therefore, concluded that § 93 was unconstitutional on its face and as applied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a trial court's order denying plaintiff restaurant association's summary judgment motion and entering judgment for defendant State Labor Commissioner because defendant's subpoena power under the California Labor Code was unconstitutional on its face and as applied. The court found that defendant's admissions to plaintiff's factual allegations in a complaint eliminated all questions as to the propriety of the declaratory relief.

Islander Yachts v. One Freeport 36-Foot Vessel


No. A020278


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


173 Cal. App. 3d 1081; 219 Cal. Rptr. 654; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2698



October 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified November 13, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 107773, Henry J. Broderick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant boat manufacturer sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Marin County (California), setting aside a right to attach order, quashing a writ of attachment, and granting summary judgment in favor of respondents, a boat and its owner, in an action whereby appellant sought to enforce a lien against respondent ship pursuant to Cal. Harb. & Nav. Code § 491, in order to obtain payment for the cost of construction.


OVERVIEW: Appellant boat manufacturer sought to enforce a lien against respondents, a boat and its owner, pursuant to Cal. Harb. & Nav. Code § 491. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondents. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that appellant's claim was properly brought under the state vessel lien statutes and not federal maritime law because a federal maritime lien did not lie to obtain payment for construction of a vessel. The court determined that appellant was estopped from asserting a state vessel lien under § 491 because appellant stated in a master carpenter's certificate, relied on by respondent owner and its financing bank, that it transferred 100 percent interest in the boat to the boat dealer, and was silent as to the existence of the lien. The court also found that respondent owner was a bona fide purchaser as he had no notice of the existence of the lien until appellant brought its action to foreclose on it. Respondent owner's purchase of the boat as a bona fide purchaser extinguished the lien. The lien was not enforceable until perfected by complaint and summons or by attachment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court finding that the order to set aside the attachment and to quash the writ of attachment, and the summary judgment, were properly granted, in favor of respondents, a boat and its owner. Appellant boat manufacturer was estopped from asserting a lien against respondents and respondent owner was a bona fide purchaser without notice.

 

931 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Lee v. City of Monterey Park


No. B009244


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


173 Cal. App. 3d 798; 219 Cal. Rptr. 309; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2672



October 25, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 495969, Irving A. Shimer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed as to the first and fourth causes of action alleging that measure K is not reasonably related to the public welfare. The judgment of dismissal is affirmed as to the second, third and fifth causes of action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff a limited partnership appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that dismissed its action after sustaining the demurrer of defendant City of Monterey Park in a taxpayer action for declaratory and injunctive relief regarding two initiative measures that limited the construction of residential units within the city.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff a limited partnership brought an action against defendant, City of Monterey Park, for declaratory and injunctive relief in regards to two voter-approved initiative measures, measure K and measure L, that limited the construction of residential units within defendant. Plaintiff alleged that measure K was an unconstitutional exercise of police power and violated the due process clauses of the California and United States Constitutions, that it did not comply with government code sections, and it was inconsistent with defendant's general plan; and alleged that measure L was an improper exercise of referendum power. The trial court entered a dismissal after sustaining defendant's demurrer. Plaintiff appealed. On appeal, the court reversed the dismissal of the cause of action that measure K was unconstitutional because pursuant to Cal. Evid. Code § 669.5, defendant had the burden to prove its ordinance was reasonably related to public welfare. The court affirmed the dismissal of the remaining causes of action because measure K complied with government code sections, was consistent with defendant's plan, and measure L was not an improper exercise of referendum power.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of plaintiff limited partnership's causes of action that alleged initiative "measure K" was an unconstitutional exercise of police power and violated the due process clauses of the California and United States Constitutions because defendant City of Monterey Park had the burden to show that the ordinance was reasonably related to the public welfare, and affirmed the dismissal of the remaining causes of action.

 

932 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Kim v. Dolch


No. E001411


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


173 Cal. App. 3d 736; 219 Cal. Rptr. 248; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2665



October 23, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. VCV 6676, LeRoy A. Simmons, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying petition for writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant business owner sought review of an order from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate in a case that challenged the constitutionality of a city ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of appellant, business owner's petition for a writ of mandate because it found that the city ordinance that regulated appellant's massage parlor was not unconstitutional. The court determined that a warrantless search of a business did not violate U.S. Const. amend. IV because the industry had been pervasively regulated and the expectation of privacy that an owner of a commercial property had was different than the expectation of individuals in their homes. The court found that the city ordinance was comprehensive in its directive regarding inspections of massage parlors and the requirement of licensing. The court held that the government had an important interest in the regulation of the industry and that a warrant requirement would frustrate the purpose of the ordinance. The court held that the lower court did not err when it denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate because it properly determined that the city ordinance did not violate U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of appellant business owner's petition for a writ of mandate because it found that the city ordinance did not violate the Fourth Amendment regarding warrantless searches because the government had an important interest in the regulation of the industry.

 

933 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Whaler's Vill. Club v. Cal. Coastal Com


No. B005654


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


173 Cal. App. 3d 240; 220 Cal. Rptr. 2; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2621



October 11, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied December 31, 1985. Mosk, J., and Lucas, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, Nos. SP-50494 and 75368, Robert J. Soares, Judge.

Previously Reported at: 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2502 and Withdrawn from the Bound Volume.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed in part to allow the Commission to reissue the permit with the condition that respondent irrevocably offer for a period of 21 years to dedicate to a public agency or private association approved by the executive director, an easement for public access and passive recreational use along the shoreline and that respondent assume liability for damage from erosion and waive claims against the Commission and any other public agency involved in issuance of the permit for any damage caused by erosion hazard. We remand to the trial court the issue of the Commission's action for injunctive relief and civil penalties for determination in light of this opinion. The judgment is otherwise affirmed.

Each party to bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant coastal commission, sought review of a judgment granting a writ of mandamus from the Superior Court of Ventura County, (California) to respondent village, who applied for a coastal development permit, and the denial of relief in appellant's action for injunctive relief and civil penalties.


OVERVIEW: Appellant coastal commission sought review of judgment granting a writ of mandamus to respondent village on the issue of granting of a coastal development permit to construct a rock revetment with conditions recommended by appellant. Respondents had sought a coastal development permit after they had constructed a revetment in order to stop erosion around coastal homes and appellant had granted the application with several conditions with which respondents take issue. Respondent argued that its right of privacy was constitutionally protected and may be abridged only where there was a compelling public need. The court reversed and held that the right of privacy does not extend to exclusion of public from access to public trust lands. The court held that courts have liberally construed the purposes of public trusts to the end of benefiting all people of the state and appellant could add conditions to the permit that forced respondents to allow public access to the beaches. The court held that the condition of offering to dedicate an easement for public access was reasonably related to one of the principal objectives of the coastal act and was supported by evidence in the record.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and permitted appellants to issue the coastal development permit with the condition that respondents irrevocable offer to dedicate an easement for public assess along the shoreline, and remanded on the issue of injunctive relief and civil penalties.

Sherwyn v. Department of Social Services


No. B010723


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


173 Cal. App. 3d 52; 218 Cal. Rptr. 778; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2606



October 9, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 500854, Philip F. Jones, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment is vacated and the trial court is directed to dismiss the action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, attorneys representing couples involved in surrogate parenting arrangements, challenged a decision from the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) for respondents, state social services department and county department of adoptions. Plaintiffs attacked the constitutional validity of Cal. Civ. Code § 7005, Cal. Evid. Code § 621, and adoption policy as applied to surrogate parenting arrangements.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, attorneys representing couples involved in surrogate parenting agreements, challenged a decision for respondents, county department of adoptions and state social services department, claiming that Cal. Civ. Code § 7005, Cal. Evid. Code § 621, and adoption policy as applied to surrogate parenting arrangements were unconstitutional. On appeal, the court held that there was no justiciable controversy. Plaintiffs, as individuals, were not party to a surrogate parenting arrangement, but merely alleged that they, as attorneys, represented couples who were parties to such arrangements; they therefore did not have standing in this action and could not state a valid cause of action. Further, the standard for the granting of declaratory relief which was in the nature of ripeness and standing was well established. The controversy must be one that admitted of definitive relief by judgment within the field of judicial administration, as distinguished from an advisory opinion. The judgment must decree, not suggest, what parties may or may not do. The judgment was vacated and the action dismissed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was vacated and the trial court was directed to dismiss the proceeding in an appeal by plaintiffs, attorneys representing couples involved in surrogate parenting arrangements, from a decision for respondents, county adoption department and state social services department. The court concluded that plaintiffs, who were not party to surrogate parenting arrangements, did not have standing in the proceeding.

City of Simi Valley v. Fair Employment & Housing Com.


No. B006765.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


172 Cal. App. 3d 1254; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2601; 39 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 863



October 8, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] As modified Oct. 18, 1985; Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to rule 976.1 of the California Rules of Court, the portion of the opinion to be published follows.


Rehg. granted Nov. 4, 1985.
Opn. filed May 8, 1986 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 81909, Joe D. Hadden, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant civil rights commission challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which granted respondent municipality a peremptory writ of administrative mandamus directing appellant to set aside its decision.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to its policy forbidding employment of relatives in the same department, respondent city refused to consider a husband's application for employment. Husband filed an employment complaint and an accusation was issued charging respondent with unlawful discrimination based on marital status. A hearing was held before appellant civil rights commission. It determined that respondent had discriminated against the husband on the basis of marital status. Respondent petitioned for a writ of administrative mandamus. The trial court issued a writ of mandate directing appellant to set aside its decision. On appeal of that order, the court determined that a conclusive case of marital status discrimination was made out when respondent stipulated that the husband was not considered for a decision because his wife worked in the same department. Once the discrimination was proven, the only remaining issue was the appropriate remedies to be afforded. The court stated that appellant was required to determine whether respondent established an affirmative remedial defense that the husband would not have been hired even absent the unlawful discrimination. Judgment was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment that granted respondent a peremptory writ of administrative mandamus directing appellant civil rights commission to set aside its decision was reversed and remanded because evidence showed that respondent city engaged in unlawful discrimination and a determination had to be made whether remedies were appropriate.

Hersch v. Citizens Sav. & Loan Ass'n


No. B009168


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


173 Cal. App. 3d 373; 218 Cal. Rptr. 646; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2633



October 3, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 29, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C316428, Charles Edward Jones, J.



DISPOSITION: The writ of execution authorizing interest at 10 percent per annum from date of judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, savings and loan association, challenged the actions of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which issued a writ of execution in favor of respondents, financially interested parties, that computed interest at the rate of 10 percent per annum on the unpaid judgment that was affirmed by the court previously.


OVERVIEW: In November 1980, judgment was entered in favor of respondents, financially interested parties, and against appellant, savings and loan association. Respondent demanded payment from appellants, which included the original judgment plus interest at 10 percent per annum from November 1980 to December 1983. Appellant argued the interest should have been 7 percent per annum. On respondent's motion, the trial court issued a writ of execution that computed interest at 10 percent per annum. At the time of judgment the legal rate of interest on unpaid judgments was 7 percent per annum. In July 1983, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 685.010, provided that interest on unsatisfied judgments should be 10 percent per annum. Appellant sought review. The court held § 685.010, was not intended to act retroactively on those portions of unpaid judgments remaining unpaid on July 1, 1983. The court found the legislature clearly intended, from history pertaining to the legislation prior to § 685.010, that the statute was not to be applied retroactively. The court reversed the writ of execution and remanded the case for correct computation by the trial court based upon a rate of 7 percent per annum.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the writ of execution authorizing interest at 10 percent per annum from the date of judgment in favor of respondents, financially interested parties. The court held the interest should be computed at the rate of 7 percent per annum on the unsatisfied judgment from November 20, 1980 to December 31,1982 and 10 percent thereafter. The court held the July 1983, civil procedure code enactment was not intended to be retroactive.

 

937 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Olivas


No. A027344


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


172 Cal. App. 3d 984; 218 Cal. Rptr. 567; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2577



October 1, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 31, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 90860, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted him of second degree murder arising from a vehicular homicide committed under the influence of phencyclidine (PCP).


OVERVIEW: Defendant led police on a high speed chase that ended in a collision causing the death of an infant and serious injury to the mother. Defendant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence to support the second degree murder conviction. The court found that a vehicular homicide committed while intoxicated involved implied malice and was second degree murder if a person knew that his conduct endangered the life of another yet acted deliberately with conscious disregard for life. The court found sufficient evidence of implied malice. Defendant had consumed enough PCP to impair his physical and mental faculties. He drove at extremely high speeds for a lengthy period of time, creating a great risk to other drivers. He was aware of this risk due to his collisions and near misses with other cars and his deliberate avoidance of two pursuing police cars. He continued this driving even after the danger was demonstrated. The absence of a finding that defendant took the PCP knowing that he would later drive did not preclude a finding that at the time of the fatal accident defendant acted deliberately with conscious disregard for a known, life-threatening risk. The conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because the evidence was sufficient to support the second degree murder conviction in that it demonstrated that defendant acted deliberately with conscious disregard of the great risk that he posed to other drivers.

Cole v. Superior Court


No. D003456


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


173 Cal. App. 3d 265; 218 Cal. Rptr. 905; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2622



September 30, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue ordering the trial court to vacate its order declining jurisdiction in this matter and to reschedule a jurisdictional hearing under the UCCJA at a time when petitioner can reasonably be expected to be ready for trial. The court shall reconsider the issue of the appropriate forum to determine the custody matter along the lines set forth in this opinion. The petition is otherwise denied. Our temporary stay of the order directing Candice to appear in Arizona shall remain in effect until the trial court has redetermined the jurisdictional issue as we have ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner wife sought review of the decision of respondent, the San Diego Superior Court (California), declining to exercise jurisdiction over a custody matter, deferring to the Arizona forum, in a proceeding brought under the Uniform Child Custody Jurisdiction Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 5150 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner wife left Arizona with her children, two of whom were also children of husband, after a stay of 10 months, moved back to California, and filed for a legal separation. Petitioner claimed that husband was abusive and that she was unable to obtain an attorney in Arizona because she had no money of her own. Respondent trial court, after discussing the matter with the trial court in Arizona and having a hearing on the matter for which petitioner's attorney did not have adequate time to prepare, declined to exercise jurisdiction over the custody matter, pursuant to the Uniform Child Custody Jurisdiction Act (UCCJA), Cal. Civ. Code § 5150 et seq. The court, in reversing respondent, found that since petitioner had alleged the basis for a possible emergency situation, she should have been given a reasonable time to gather her evidence to prove her contentions. The court found that respondent abused its discretion in not permitting petitioner a reasonable amount of time before the jurisdictional hearing to assemble her proof on the issues of emergency, home state, and clean hands issues under the UCCJA. The court concluded that she was entitled to some relief.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate ordering respondent trial court to vacate its order declining jurisdiction in this custody matter and to reschedule a jurisdictional hearing under the child custody statute in order to reconsider the appropriate forum issue. The court held that petitioner wife should have been given adequate time to assemble her proof on the issues of emergency, home state, and clean hands.

County of Butte v. Bach


Civ. No. 23325


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


172 Cal. App. 3d 848; 218 Cal. Rptr. 613; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2567



September 27, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Defendants and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 30, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 73702, Reginald M. Watt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: None of the other contentions or arguments warrants discussion. The judgment's award of damages to the Bachs is reversed with directions that judgment be awarded in favor of all cross-defendants on the Bachs' federal civil rights cross-complaint. Insofar as the judgment denies injunctive relief to the County of Butte against use of the subject property for law office purposes, it is reversed and the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. The judgment's denial of injunctive relief to the neighbors on their cause of action for violation of the restrictive covenant is reversed with the direction that such relief be accorded. With those exceptions the judgment and orders after judgment which are subjects of this appeal are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Butte County (California) denied plaintiff county an injunction against defendant landowners to prohibit their use of a house in a residential zone as a law office; awarded defendants damages against cross-defendants, board of supervisors, a supervisor, office of the county counsel, and neighbors; denied defendants' request for attorney's fees; and denied cross-complainant neighbors an injunction. All parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the trial court's denial to plaintiff county of injunctive relief against defendant landowners' use of a house for a law office. The zoning authorities did not abuse of their discretion. The regional welfare perspective did not apply as the zoning ordinance had no effect beyond municipal boundaries. Defendants acquired the property after the zoning ordinance was enacted, so plaintiff could not be estopped to enforce it against them. Defendants were not denied equal protection. Defendants' business use of the house was not, as required, limited to persons residing there. The court reversed the award of damages to defendants as contradicting a valid zoning ordinance and granted cross-complainant neighbors' motions for judgment notwithstanding the verdict. The court also reversed the denial of injunctive relief to cross-complainants as there was no substantial evidence that changed conditions outside of the tract governed by the covenant made it inequitable to enforce the restrictive covenant. The court affirmed the award of attorney's fees to cross-complainants as their communications to zoning officials were not actionable and the claim against them was meritless.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the award of damages to defendant landowners with directions that judgment be awarded in favor of all cross-defendants on the defendants' federal civil rights cross-complaint, reversed the denial of injunctive relief to plaintiff county, and reversed the denial of injunctive relief to the cross-complainant neighbors on their cause of action for violation of the restrictive covenant.

People v. Paoli


No. A020831.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


172 Cal. App. 3d 611; 218 Cal. Rptr. 435; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2549



September 26, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rev. den. Dec. 31, 1985, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 73304, Martin N. Pulich, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) convicting him of soliciting an act of prostitution, after the court reversed a magistrate's order suppressing evidence of tape-recorded conversations between appellant and his client.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was an attorney who acted as the defense counsel for a woman that was charged with forgery. She complained to the police that appellant had been making his best efforts as an attorney conditional upon her having sexual relations with him. After the trial court reversed a magistrate's order suppressing evidence of tape-recorded conversations between appellant and his client, appellant entered a negotiated plea of no contest to soliciting an act of prostitution, a misdemeanor. Appellant argued that the recorded conversations were inadmissible under Cal. Penal Code § 636. The court held that the conversations between appellant and his client were illegally recorded in contravention of § 636, because the protections of the law extended to both the attorney and the client. In addition, the court determined that the suppression of the evidence was an appropriate sanction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because the conversations between appellant and his client were illegally recorded in contravention of the penal Code. The code's protections extended to both the attorney and the client. The suppression of the evidence was an appropriate sanction.

City v. Thompson


No. A023426


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


172 Cal. App. 3d 652; 218 Cal. Rptr. 445; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2551



September 26, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 25, 1985, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 18, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. DA 8105, Donald B. King, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied appellant reimbursement from respondent father for public assistance funds paid to his child and her mother for any period prior to the date of a court order establishing respondent's liability for child support.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county sought reimbursement from respondent father for public assistance funds paid to his child and her mother. The trial court entered judgment for respondent, holding that reimbursement claims against fathers against whom there was no order for child support but whose children had received public assistance denied the fathers equal protection because there was no similar retrospective liability for fathers against whom there was no order for child support but whose children had not received public assistance. The court reversed the trial court. It found that there was a rational basis for the legislature's differential treatment embodied in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350 of parents whose offspring received public assistance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court denying appellant county reimbursement from respondent father for public assistance funds paid to his child and her mother. It held that the requirement of reimbursement did not deny respondent equal protection because there was a rational basis for the legislature's differential treatment of parents whose offspring received public assistance.

Lewis v. City of Hayward


No. A028473.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


173 Cal. App. 3d 178; 218 Cal. Rptr. 391; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2615



September 25, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Oct. 25, 1985 (See 177 Cal. App. 3d 103).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H-87801-4, Raymond L. Marsh, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants city resident and environmental groups sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted respondents city and developers summary judgment in an action challenging the constitutionality of the window period cancellation provision of former Cal. Gov't Code § 51282 as it applied to real party in interest landowners.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest landowners sought cancellation of the contractual restriction of use of their property under the Williamson Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 51200 et seq. Respondent city's cancellation was vacated by the California Supreme Court. In response, the legislature passed the window period cancellation provision of former Cal. Gov't Code § 51282, which allowed Williamson contract land owners a one-time opportunity to cancel. Appellants city resident and environmental groups sought declaratory and injunctive relief challenging the constitutionality of the window provision against respondents city and developers. Respondent developers sought summary judgment on the grounds that the window provision was constitutional. The trial court granted the motion. On appeal the court reversed because the provision was in conflict with Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 8. The court held that the provision's purpose was to allow termination of a Williamson contract where development was imminent, without concern for public interest. The court held the provision was not a hardship measure because it was unconstitutional, and severance would not invalidate the remainder of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of respondents city and developers and upheld appellants' city resident and environmental groups' claims because a statutory cancellation provision was unconstitutional where it permitted real party in interest landowners to terminate their land use restriction contracts when development was imminent and without concern for public interest.

California Coastal Com. v. Alves


No. A024184.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant coastal commission sought review of an order of the Superior Court, San Mateo County (California), which entered a judgment in favor of respondents, the owners, developers, and subdividers of certain real property, in an action under the California Coastal Act of 1976, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant coastal commission brought suit against respondents, the owners, developers, and subdividers of certain real property, under the California Coastal Act of 1976, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30000 et seq. Appellant contended that respondents unlawfully subdivided the property and unlawfully constructed a house and other improvements on one of the subdivided parcels. Appellant sought civil fines, additional penalties for intentional violations of the Act, and also requested an injunction. The trial court entered judgment in favor of respondents. Appellant sought review. The appellate court affirmed in part and reversed in part. It found that because the original subdivision was completed before the effective date of the Coastal Act, it was valid. The court found that the second subdivision of Parcel C was not exempt from the provisions of the Coastal Act, but the applicable one-year statute of limitations barred the request for additional civil fines and exemplary damages for the attempt to create it. The court said that only the claims for civil fines under § 30820 and injunctive relief survived.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed in part and reversed in part a judgment in favor of respondents, the owners, developers, and subdividers of certain real property, in an action under the California Coastal Act of 1976. The court held that certain claims for civil fines and injunctive relief survived.
