Tribune Newspapers W. v. Superior Court


No. B014796


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


172 Cal. App. 3d 443; 218 Cal. Rptr. 505; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2535



September 24, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified September 30, 1985. A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 9, 1985, and the Petitions of Tribune Newspapers West, Inc., and Mark B. for Review by the Supreme Court were Denied January 16, 1986. Bird, C. J., Mosk, J., and Lucas, J., were of the Opinion that the Petitions should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: The matter is remanded with instructions to conduct a hearing, duly noticed, for consideration of all relevant factors to be weighed in a determination of whether the minor has met his burden of establishing that the adverse publicity has created a reasonable likelihood of substantial prejudice to his right to a fair and impartial trial, and to provide the public and press an opportunity to overcome the minor's showing by a preponderance of the evidence that there is no reasonable likelihood of substantial prejudice. The alternative writ of mandate is discharged and the temporary stay order is dissolved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner newspaper sought a writ of mandate ordering respondent, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), to vacate its order closing a juvenile fitness hearing to the press and public. Despite a temporary stay by this court, respondent conducted further proceedings and modified its order by changing future hearings to "fitness hearings" and by allowing the press to contact parties present at the hearings.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner newspaper sought a writ of mandate ordering respondent court to vacate its order closing a juvenile fitness hearing to the press and public. The case involved a minor charged with two bank robberies and various other crimes while armed with a dangerous or deadly weapon. The appellate court granted the writ and remanded the case with instructions to conduct a duly noticed hearing for consideration of all relevant factors in a determination of whether the minor has met his burden of establishing that adverse publicity created a reasonable likelihood of substantial prejudice to the his right to a fair and impartial trial and to provide the public and press an opportunity to overcome the minor's showing by a preponderance of evidence that there was no reasonable likelihood of substantial prejudice. The trial court should consider the nature and extent of the media coverage, including circulation figures and geographical distribution; possible transfer to a different branch of the court; feasibility of transporting jurors from another area of the county; change of venue; protection afforded by a searching voir dire of potential jurors; and sequestration of the jury panel.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of mandate and remanded with instructions to conduct a hearing, duly noticed, for a determination of whether the minor has met his burden of establishing that the adverse publicity has created a reasonable likelihood of substantial prejudice to his right to a fair and impartial trial and to provide the public and press an opportunity to overcome the minor's showing.

 

945 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Benson


No. G001738


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


172 Cal. App. 3d 532; 218 Cal. Rptr. 384; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2540



September 24, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. M-4966, James L. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order issuing the writ of habeas corpus and declaring Fullerton Municipal Code sections 7.130.010 and 7.125.010 unconstitutional is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted a writ of habeas corpus declaring two municipal antigaming ordinances of appellant's unconstitutional, and enjoined further prosecutions under the ordinances. The trial court had held that Fullerton Municipal Code § 7.130.010 (gambling for commercial purposes) and § 7.125.010 (playing the game of panguingue for money) were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating certain code sections of appellant city; Fullerton Municipal Code § 7.130.010 (gambling for commercial purposes) and § 7.125.010 (playing the game of panguingue for money). Defendant demurred to the complaint against her and claimed the ordinances were unconstitutional. The municipal court overruled defendant's demurrer and she sought relief by petitioning for writ of habeas corpus in the superior court. The superior court agreed the two ordinances were unconstitutional, issued its writ of habeas corpus and enjoined the city from prosecuting others under the same code sections. Appellant challenged the decision, and the court reversed the superior court's order issuing the writ of habeas corpus and declaring the ordinances unconstitutional. The court held that the ordinances on their face were constitutional because they regulated and prohibited only gambling conduct. The regulation of gambling was an appropriate exercise of local police power when not preempted by state law. Thus, the ordinances on their face, and by their plain meaning, withstood constitutional scrutiny.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the superior court that declared certain gambling ordinances of appellant city unconstitutional. The court held that the municipal gambling ordinances were constitutional because were within the city's police power, and properly regulated activities not preempted by the state.

 

946 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Franklin v. Leland Stanford Junior University


No. A014999


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


172 Cal. App. 3d 322; 218 Cal. Rptr. 228; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2525



September 20, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 11, 1985, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 31, 1985. Bird, C. J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 277253, John A. Flaherty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff associate professor sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which upheld plaintiff's dismissal by defendant university.


OVERVIEW: During the Vietnam War, plaintiff associate professor incited students to engage in disruptive acts, interfered with police attempts to restore order, and acted with student antiwar protesters to disrupt defendant university's operations. Defendant dismissed plaintiff, who filed suit, alleging his dismissal violated his rights under U.S. Const. amend I. The trial court entered judgment for defendant and the court affirmed. Using the independent review standard, rather than substantial evidence review, the court held that balancing of the parties' competing interests showed plaintiff's words were not constitutionally protected because speech which resulted in disruption, materially interfered with school activities, or impaired discipline was not constitutionally protected against an employer's response. Further, the conduct standards under which defendant dismissed plaintiff were not unconstitutionally vague because their prohibitions were clearly defined so as to give notice and to preclude speculation on their meaning or differences on their application. Defendant's interpretation and application of its standards was neither inconsistent nor plainly erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment upholding the propriety of defendant university's dismissal of plaintiff associate professor because the speech in which plaintiff engaged had no constitutional protections due to the disruption and material interference it produced in defendant's activities and operations.

Whalers' Village Club v. California Coastal Com.


No. B005654.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


218 Cal. Rptr. 122; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2502



September 11, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] The Opinion Previously Reported at 172 Cal. App. 3d 62 has been Withdrawn from the Bound Volume and Republished at: 173 Cal. App. 3d 240.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, Nos. SP-50494 and 75368, Robert J. Soares, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the California Coastal Commission (commission), sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which granted appellee homeowners a writ of mandamus that ordered the commission to remove conditions from a new development permit and denied the commission's claim for injunctive relief and civil penalties against the homeowners.


OVERVIEW: The homeowners installed a rock revetment along their coastal property to prevent wave damage without a coastal permit. After notification from the commission that a coastal permit was necessary, the homeowner's applied for an after the fact permit. The commission approved the permit subject to dedication of an easement for public access along the shoreline and a deed restriction that recognized that the property was subject to extraordinary hazards from erosion. The homeowners filed a petition for a writ of mandate challenging the conditions. The commission sought injunctive relief and civil penalties against the homeowners. The superior court granted the writ of mandamus and denied the commission's request for penalties. The commission claimed on appeal that public policy favored allowing public access to shoreline areas and that the project was a new development under Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30212(b) that was subject to reasonable regulation. The court agreed with the commission's claims. The court ruled that the commission's conditions were reasonably related to one of the objectives of the Coastal Act and that they were supported by substantial evidence in the record.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment to allow the commission to reissue a coastal permit to the homeowner's association with a condition that the homeowner's association dedicate an easement for public access and assume liability for erosion damage.

Paterson v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. A027469


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


171 Cal. App. 3d 1126; 217 Cal. Rptr. 881; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2487



September 6, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 256103, David A. Dolgin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the order of the Superior Court, Contra Costa County (California), which denied his application for a writ of mandate to set aside his driver's license suspension pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(5).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff's driver's license was suspended by defendant motor vehicle department for his three drunk driving convictions within a period of five years as per Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(5). Plaintiff thereupon sought a writ of mandate in the trial court to set aside the suspension. The writ was denied. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that § 13352 provided a mandatory minimum suspension for violation of its conditions. The court held that the statute did not violate due process because it was reasonably related to a proper legislative goal. Also, the strong legislative policy to rid the state's highways of drunk drivers justified any non-rebuttable presumption having a reasonable relation to the presumed fact that may have been created by the statute. Lastly, the court found that § 13352(5) did not violate equal protection as applied in the instant case. The statute validly attempted to minimize the highway carnage caused by drunk drivers, as a class.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff's request for a writ of mandate to set aside his driver's license suspension because the vehicular code section providing for such suspension was not unconstitutional as applied. The court also found that any non-rebuttable presumption created by the statute having a reasonable relation to the presumed fact was justified.

People v. Soto


No. B014010


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


171 Cal. App. 3d 1158; 217 Cal. Rptr. 795; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2490



September 6, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Alhambra Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M-131417, John L. Martinez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The matter was transferred to the court after the appellate division of the superior court affirmed the order of the Municipal Court for the Alhambra Judicial District of Los Angeles (California), which found that Cal. Penal Code § 647(d) was unconstitutional. The case was transferred in order to secure uniformity of decision and to settle important questions of law.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was charged with violating Cal. Penal Code § 647(d), which provided that every person who loitered in or about any public toilet for the purposes of engaging in or soliciting any lewd or lascivious or any unlawful act was guilty of disorderly conduct, a misdemeanor. The municipal court sustained respondent's demurrer on two federal constitutional grounds and dismissed the complaint. The intermediate appellate court affirmed, holding that § 647(d) was unconstitutionally vague in violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The matter was transferred to the state supreme court in order to secure uniformity of decision and to settle important questions of law. The court affirmed the order of dismissal. Section 647(d) on its face encouraged arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement practices in contravention of constitutional principles of due process. Fatally absent from § 647(d) were objective criteria to guide a police officer's decision making process with regard to the criminality of an actor's conduct. Section 647(d) also allowed judges and juries who might widely differ to find criminal intent without the guidelines of objective predictors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing the criminal complaint filed against respondent and held that the criminal statute at issue was unconstitutionally vague because it encouraged arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement practices in violation of constitutional principals of due process.

 

950 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. De Juan


No. E000029


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


171 Cal. App. 3d 1110; 217 Cal. Rptr. 642; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2486



September 5, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On October 2, 1985, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 19, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCR-38254, Roy E. Chapman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: No error is demonstrated. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, San Bernardino County (California) which, upon a jury verdict, entered a judgment of conviction for two counts of first-degree murder. Defendant argued that the lower court erred in denying in part his motion to suppress evidence and that the introduction of the challenged evidence constituted prejudicial error.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of two counts of first-degree murder. Prior to defendant's arrest for the murders, a family member of the victim's hired a private investigation firm to find the killer. Several private investigators stopped defendant at a red light and searched his vehicle after handcuffing him. After finding bullets matching the ones that killed the victim, the investigators drove defendant to the police station where he then made statements to the police. The court held that the lower court did not err in failing to suppress the evidence found by the private investigators because the vehicle search was not constitutionally impermissible where it was carried out by private investigators acting as private persons serving private interests. Furthermore, the court found that defendant voluntarily made statements to police after being told he was not under arrest; therefore, the lower court properly declined to suppress such statements. The court affirmed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for two counts of first-degree murder, holding that the lower court did not err in failing to suppress evidence found in defendant's vehicle by private investigators because the vehicle search was not constitutionally impermissible where it was carried out by private investigators acting as private persons serving private interests.

 

951 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Wallace v. Hibner


Nos. F004264, F004505


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


171 Cal. App. 3d 1042; 217 Cal. Rptr. 748; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2477



September 3, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 306639-6, Eugene W. Krum, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patient sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Fresno County (California), which dismissed her medical malpractice claim against respondent hospital and granted judgment on the pleadings in favor of respondent doctor. Appellant argued that where respondents failed to remove the entire needle embedded in her foot, the needle fragment constituted a "foreign body" within Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.5.


OVERVIEW: Appellant patient consulted respondent doctor to obtain treatment for a puncture wound caused by a sewing needle that was traumatically embedded in her foot. Respondent doctor inadvertently failed to remove the entire needle during the operation and it was not until 13 years later that appellant discovered this fact. Appellant filed a medical malpractice action against respondents, doctor and hospital. The trial court dismissed the complaint against respondent hospital and entered judgment on the pleadings for respondent doctor. On appeal, the court held that under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.5, unless the needle fragment in appellant's foot constituted a "foreign body," appellant's medical malpractice action had to fail because it was filed more than three years after the date of injury. The court found that the needle fragment in appellant's foot was not a "foreign body" under § 340.5 because the "foreign body" exception only applied to medically inserted objects with no therapeutic or diagnostic purpose. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing appellant's complaint against respondent hospital and the judgment on the pleadings in favor of respondent doctor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondents, doctor and hospital, holding that the needle fragment left in appellant patient's foot did not constitute a "foreign body" under statute because it was not a medically inserted object that had no therapeutic or diagnostic purpose. Therefore, appellant's medical malpractice action was properly dismissed because it was not filed within three years after the date of injury.

Coalition for Econ. Survival v. Deukmejian


No. B008453


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


171 Cal. App. 3d 954; 217 Cal. Rptr. 621; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2469



August 30, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied November 14, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 450871, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), awarding respondent coalition attorney fees under the Civil Rights Attorney's Fees Awards Act of 1976, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988, and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state entered the fiscal year without a budget and because there was no appropriation for aid for families with dependent children (AFDC), no payments would be made. Respondent coalition filed suit for a temporary restraining order (TRO) requiring appellant to pay AFDC funds from its general revenue until a budget passed. The TRO was denied, but the state supreme court issued an interim stay ordering the payments. After the budget passed, the petition was dismissed, and the trial court awarded respondent attorneys' fees under the Civil Rights Attorney's Fees Awards Act of 1976, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment on two bases. First, the budget act restrictions on attorneys' fees did not apply to § 1988 because the Budget Act of 1982 § 4.50 excluded such fees from the restrictions except for Cal. Civ. Code § 1021.5. Second, respondent was the prevailing party and was entitled to attorneys' fees because the stay had the effect of a final judgment and provided the relief sought. The court noted that § 1988 allowed attorneys to instigate suits where no damages occurred but fees were recoverable by statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of attorneys' fees to respondent coalition in respondent's action that resulted in an interim stay order that required appellant state to make AFDC payments from its general funds until a budget was passed. The interim stay had the effect of a final judgment and provided the relief sought; thus, under the Civil Rights Attorney's Fees Awards Act of 1976, respondents were entitled to attorneys' fees.

San Dieguito Union High Sch. Dist. v. Rosander


No. D002925


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


171 Cal. App. 3d 968; 217 Cal. Rptr. 737; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2470



August 30, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied November 13, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 533658, Douglas R. Woodworth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant elementary school district sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California) which issued a peremptory writ of mandate that declared an elementary school withdrawal election invalid.


OVERVIEW: Appellant elementary school district operated two elementary schools and sought to remove itself from the junior high school system operated by respondents, a high school district and superintendent, and open its own junior high school. The trial court issued a peremptory writ of mandate and held that the withdrawal election proposed by appellant was violative of state law and would disenfranchise a segment of voters. Appellant contended that its withdrawal election that would have only permitted the electors in appellant's district to vote was proper pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 37085. The court held that § 37085 required a vote among all the electors in respondent high school district, not just the electors that resided in both respondent high school district and appellant's district. Appellant argued that only its electors should be able to vote because if all the affected electors were allowed to vote then appellant's electors will be overwhelmed by the larger group. The court such an argument raised equal protection concerns and stated that there was not a compelling interest to exclude voters because of how they might vote.


OUTCOME: Trial court's issuance of a writ of mandate and declaration that a withdrawal election was invalid was affirmed. In order for appellant elementary school district to hold a withdrawal election the entire electorate of appellant's district and that of respondent high school district must be given the opportunity to vote.

 

954 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Smith


No. F004794


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


171 Cal. App. 3d 997; 217 Cal. Rptr. 634; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2473



August 30, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, No. 27215, Walter H. Condley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide that appellant shall receive a consecutive term on count IX of one year, four months. The trial court shall prepare an amended abstract of judgment which reflects said modification and forward a certified copy of same to the appropriate authorities. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his convictions and sentences from the Superior Court, Kern County (California), for three counts of lewd and lascivious acts upon or with the body of a child under age 14 in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288, alleging that the statute of limitations barred his convictions on counts IX and XIV, and the trial court erred in computing his sentence for count IX.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of lewd and lascivious acts upon or with the body of a child under age 14 which violated Cal. Penal Code § 288, and he was sentenced to an eight-year upper base term on count XXIV, plus two consecutive terms for counts IX and XIV. The court treated appellant's challenge as a petition for habeas corpus and held that appellant's convictions on counts IX and XIV were not barred for four reasons. First, the information was timely filed, and because the offense was the same when the limitations period was amended, count XIV was not barred. Second, because count X in the original information was timely filed, count IX as successor count was not barred. Third, because the first extension of the statute of limitations did not violate ex post facto laws, the second also did not because they were effective before the period expired. Fourth, the delay did not prejudice appellant's defense, the amendments were retroactive to all actions not time-barred, and appellant admitted that all matters were committed within the statutory period in his nolo contendere plea. The court modified appellant's sentence for count IX because it violated the ex post facto prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions holding that counts XIV and IX were not barred by the statute of limitations because the initial information was timely filed when the statute of limitations was amended twice, count XIV was the same offense, and count IX was a successor count. The court modified appellant's sentence for count IX because it violated the ex post facto prohibition.

 

955 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


County of Los Angeles v. Thompson


No. B008815


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


172 Cal. App. 3d 18; 218 Cal. Rptr. 101; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2498



August 27, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D-950227, Richard Allen Lavine, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order (judgment) is reversed and remanded; the trial court is directed to grant defendant's motion to vacate the 1978 judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his motion to vacate a stipulated judgment of paternity that had been entered more than six years earlier, on grounds that defendant had waived his right to challenge the validity of the judgment against him by having pled guilty to contempt charges, and because he had not shown "due diligence" in pursuing his legal remedies.


OVERVIEW: Defendant signed an agreement admitting paternity and agreeing to pay child support; the agreement became a court order. Plaintiff county later filed for a contempt order, alleging there was a delinquent sum of child support. Defendant pled guilty to certain counts of the contempt citation; he thereafter obtained continuance after continuance, until he filed a motion to vacate the judgment in light of intervening precedent. The intervening decision, with retroactive implications, held that paternity judgments were voidable if the alleged father was unaware that his rights would be waived by executing an agreement; the motion was denied. On appeal, the court accepted as true defendant's allegations in a declaration that he was not informed of his rights when executing the agreement, as plaintiff had not challenged those assertions; he thus had not made a waiver of his rights in relation to the judgment when he pled guilty to the contempt charges. Defendant had also exercised due diligence in his efforts to set aside the judgment, as the record showed that defendant's continuances were obtained with no protest from plaintiff. The order below was reversed.


OUTCOME: Order denying defendant's motion to vacate a stipulated judgment of paternity against him, was reversed. Defendant's allegations in a declaration that he was not informed of his rights when executing the agreement that resulted in the judgment, were accepted as true; he thus had made no waiver as to his right to challenge the validity of the judgment. Moreover, defendant had also exercised due diligence in trying to set the judgment aside.

957 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Charles


No. A022571


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


171 Cal. App. 3d 552; 217 Cal. Rptr. 402; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2434



August 23, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied November 14, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 72637A, Martin N. Pulich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which sentenced appellant to a much-reduced sentence under a plea agreement. The trial court, however, conditioned the plea and sentence agreement on appellant's agreement to waive his right to appeal the trial court's prior ruling denying appellant's motion to suppress search evidence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant received a much-reduced sentence in return for a guilty plea. The trial court, however, conditioned the sentence and plea agreement on appellant's agreement not to appeal the trial court's earlier denial of appellant's motion to suppress search evidence. Appellant, contrary to the agreement, sought review. The court noted that Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(m) was an exception to the general rule that a defendant could not enter a plea of guilty conditioned upon retaining the right to appeal the denial of his motion to suppress evidence. The court also noted that the right of a defendant to appeal his conviction was purely statutory in nature. The court concluded that appellant could waive his or her statutory right to appeal the trial court's ruling on a suppression motion and that such waiver could be made a condition of a plea agreement. As long as the record established that the waiver was free, knowing, and intelligent, the court determined that it could be upheld as a part of the plea agreement; thus, appellant was barred from raising the search and seizure issue on appeal notwithstanding § 538.5(m). The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed because a criminal defendant could waive his or her statutory right to appeal the trial court's ruling on a suppression motion and such waiver could be made a condition of a plea agreement where the record established that the waiver was freely, knowingly, and intelligently made.

Casas v. Thompson


No. D001019.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


171 Cal. App. 3d 458; 217 Cal. Rptr. 471; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2428



August 22, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Sept. 6, 1985; Rev. granted Nov. 27, 1985 (See 42 Cal. 3d 131)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 455959, G. Dennis Adams, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ex-wife and defendant ex-husband challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California). Defendant appealed the judgment awarding plaintiff 30 percent community interest in his military retirement, contending that the award, if valid, was erroneously computed. Plaintiff also appealed, alleging, among other things, that the trial court erroneously denied her arrearages for 10 years.


OVERVIEW: At the time of plaintiff ex-wife and defendant ex-husband's divorce, none of the proceedings disposed of defendant's military retirement. Plaintiff alleged the military retirement was an omitted community asset subject to present partition. The trial court agreed and awarded her a 30 percent community interest. Defendant appealed the award. Plaintiff also appealed, arguing that the trial court erred in denying her arrearages. The court held that California courts had the power to award an ex-spouse an interest in the military retiree's pension; there was no complete federal preemption. The court held the trial court properly computed plaintiff's interest on defendant's gross retirement pay, and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to award plaintiff retroactive payments as inequitable, because defendant had provided sole support for the children and plaintiff did not contribute financially. The court reversed and ordered the trial court to award plaintiff her community property interest in defendant's gross retired pay, instead of net, between the date of filing her complaint and the date of judgment; in all other respects, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision in all respects except where it determined that the trial court erred in awarding plaintiff ex-wife her arrearages from the filing date of the complaint computed on the net, rather than the gross, benefits received. The court reversed and ordered the trial court to compute plaintiff's interest from the filing date based on the gross retired pay and to recompute the interest due.

 

959 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Briarwood Properties v. City of L.A.


Civ. No. 65105


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


171 Cal. App. 3d 1020; 217 Cal. Rptr. 849; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2475



August 22, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied November 20, 1985. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 326957, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Section 47.06 did not unlawfully impose additional conditions on Briarwood after tentative map approval nor unconstitutionally impair Briarwood's vested rights. The judgment is reversed and judgment is ordered entered in favor of Los Angeles.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, city and city council, sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that declared a relocation assistance ordinance unlawfully imposed an additional condition after respondent developer had received tentative map approval for a condominium conversion in violation of the California Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66410 et seq., and granted respondent's motion for a summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Respondent developer received tentative map approval for a condominium conversion project. One year later, appellants, city and city council, adopted a local ordinance that provided additional relocation assistance to tenants displaced by condominium conversions. Respondent sought declaratory relief and asserted that it had complied with the relocation assistance ordinance in effect at the time it received tentative map approval and appellant could not impose the more extensive relocation assistance requirements. The trial court granted respondent's motion for summary judgment because the local ordinance was invalid as applied to respondent in that it imposed additional conditions after tentative map approval in violation of the California Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66410 et seq. Appellant sought review. The appellate court reversed the trial court judgment and entered judgment in favor of appellant because the additional relocation assistance ordinance was a police power regulation that merely required all developers involved in condominium conversion satisfy certain requirements pertaining to tenant relocation and did not unlawfully impose a new condition.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court judgment in favor of respondent developer and entered judgment in favor of appellant, city and city council, because the more extensive relocation assistance ordinance, adopted after respondent received tentative map approval for a condominium conversion, was a police power regulation that did not unlawfully impose additional conditions.

Pyne v. Meese


Civ. No. 23926


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


172 Cal. App. 3d 392; 218 Cal. Rptr. 87; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2531



August 22, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Siskiyou County, No. 35443, James E. Kleaver, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed. Defendant City of Etna and David Bradford's request for an award of attorney fees on appeal is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owners appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Siskiyou County (California), which entered summary judgments in favor of defendants, Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV), director of DMV, DMV agents, city, police chief, county, and sheriff, in plaintiffs' action under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 alleging that defendants violated their U.S. Const. amend IV and due process rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV) issued an order to seize plaintiff owners' trailer and car because they failed to pay registration fees. With the aid of officers from defendants city and county, defendant DMV executed the order. Plaintiffs filed suit under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 alleging that defendants, DMV, director of DMV, DMV agents, city, police chief, county, and sheriff, violated their U.S. Const. amend. IV and due process rights. The trial court granted defendants' motions for summary judgment. The court affirmed, first finding that defendant director was properly dismissed because he was not the director at the time of the seizure. The court next found that defendants DMV and DMV agents were entitled to summary judgment because the latter properly executed a valid seizure order. The court also found that defendant DMV agents were qualifiedly immune from suit. Further, the court held that defendants county, sheriff, and city were entitled to summary judgment because plaintiffs failed to identify any policy that deprived them of their constitutional rights. Finally, the court found that defendant police chief was entitled to qualified immunity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgments entered in favor of defendants, Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV), director of DMV, DMV agents, city, police chief, county, and sheriff, because defendants DMV agents and police chief were immune from suit and plaintiff owners otherwise failed to raise a genuine issue of material fact that defendants violated their constitutional rights when they seized plaintiffs' trailer and car.

 

961 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Semsch v. Henry Mayo Newhall Mem'l Hosp.


No. B006143


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


171 Cal. App. 3d 162; 216 Cal. Rptr. 913; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2397



August 14, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NWC-80315, Joseph R. Kalin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Plaintiff and defendants did not agree in their respective briefs as to which portion of the gross $ 380,000 award constitutes economic as opposed to noneconomic damages. In the absence of a special verdict or interrogatory stating the amount of economic damages, ordinarily we would be required to remand the case for retrial on the issue on damages. However during oral argument counsel for plaintiff stated that the total economic damages may be considered as being $ 30,000, and counsel for defendants stated that such figure would be acceptable to defendants. Accordingly, in order to conserve judicial resources, we shall simply modify the judgment and reduce it to the net sum of $ 243,600. 1 If plaintiff is not satisfied with such a modification and desires instead to retry the issue of damages, then plaintiff may petition this court, within the time to petition for rehearing, requesting that the case be remanded to the trial court for retrial on the issue of damages only. Each party is to bear its own costs on appeal.



1 The gross verdict was $ 380,000. If the total economic damages be considered as totalling $ 30,000, the addition of $ 250,000 permissible noneconomic damages equals a gross total of $ 280,000. Reducing the gross total by the finding of contributory negligence of 13 percent results in a net judgment of $ 243,600.

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, hospital and nurse, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded plaintiff patient damages in plaintiff's action alleging negligence.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patient sought damages for injuries suffered while in the care of defendants, hospital and nurse. Defendants sought to introduce evidence regarding the reason for plaintiff's discharge from her employment and how that discharge impacted plaintiff's ability to obtain future employment. Defendants conceded liability but challenged the damages because the trial court erred by refusing to accept testimony bearing upon plaintiff's future employment possibilities and because the undifferentiated damage award exceeded the amount allowable under Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2. The court found that the trial court's exclusion of evidence for plaintiff's discharge was not an abuse of discretion because the offer of proof lacked specificity. The court found that, although defendants' proffer contained the names of witnesses who might testify and the purpose of the testimony, it did not give the precise testimony to be offered by either of those witnesses. The court modified the judgment, reducing the award of damages because the amount of damages for noneconomic losses exceeded $ 250,000, the limit set forth in Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's ruling excluding evidence proffered by defendants, hospital and nurse, regarding the reason for plaintiff patient's termination from her employment because the offer of proof lacked specificity. The court modified the judgment and reduced the amount of damages awarded because the amount exceeded the statutory limit for noneconomic losses.

 

962 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Arthur W.


No. B005905


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


171 Cal. App. 3d 179; 217 Cal. Rptr. 183; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2399



August 14, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, No. J 34722, Steven Z. Perren, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of wardship is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which declared him a ward of the juvenile court following his admission of driving a vehicle while under the combined influence of alcohol and drugs in a case brought by respondent, the people of California.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile challenged an order, which declared him a ward of the juvenile court following his admission of driving a vehicle under the influence of alcohol and drugs. Appellant contended that Cal. Veh. Code § 13352.3 violated the due process and equal protection clauses of the California and federal constitutions because it provided that the driver's license of a minor be revoked upon a finding that such minor committed the offense of driving under the influence. The court held that the right to drive was not protected by a specific constitutional guarantee and a fundamental right was not adversely affected. While a minor was clearly entitled to the protection of due process of law whenever the state initiated deprivatory action, it was equally well settled that the due process rights of minors were not coextensive with those of adults. The court held that § 13352.3 had a rational relationship to the legitimate goal of reducing fatal and injury traffic accidents involving youthful intoxicated drivers and that the statute was constitutional. The order of wardship was thus affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order of wardship over appellant juvenile was affirmed because the court held that the vehicle code had a rational relationship to the legitimate goal of reducing fatal and injury traffic accidents involving youthful intoxicated drivers and that the statute was constitutional.

People v. Superior Court (Doran)


No. B015092.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


170 Cal. App. 3d 1255; 216 Cal. Rptr. 902; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2373



August 12, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rev. den. Sept. 19, 1985, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought a writ of mandate requesting the court to direct respondent court to vacate it's 1985 order granting real party in interest defendant's motion to set aside the judgment of conviction based upon a lack of personal entry of the not guilty by reason of insanity plea in 1980.


OVERVIEW: In 1980, real party in interest defendant entered a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity on an arson charge. Defendant was committed to a state hospital until June 1985, absent an extended commitment. Petitioner sought a commitment extension in March 1985. Defendant filed a motion to set aside the judgment of conviction and insanity based upon a lack of personal entry of the not guilty by reason of insanity plea. Respondent court granted the motion. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate asking the court to order respondent to vacate the grant of defendant's motion. The court held, based upon the hearing transcript, defendant did not voice a personal entry of the not guilty by reason of insanity plea. The court held defendant's acknowledgement of understanding as to the potential consequences of a plea was not an unequivocal adoption of the plea. However, the court held defendant was not entitled to setting aside of the 1980 plea because his request for relief was untimely. The court held defendant was not in a position to collaterally attack the basis of his original commitment. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent vacate its order.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate to respondent court ordering it to vacate it's 1985 order granting real party in interest defendant's motion to set aside his 1980 plea of not guilty by reason of insanity. The court ordered that trial be set on petitioner state's commitment extension. The court held that defendant did not voice a personal entry of the not guilty by reason of insanity plea; however, his motion was untimely filed.

 

964 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Carleton v. Superior Court


No. D002197


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


170 Cal. App. 3d 1182; 216 Cal. Rptr. 890; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2368



August 9, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 11, 1985. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Application should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought writ of mandamus to compel respondent, Superior Court of San Diego County (California), to suppress blood and related tests in his trial for driving under the influence of alcohol under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(a), felony hit and run under Cal. Veh. Code § 20001, and two counts of vehicular manslaughter under Cal. Veh. Code § 23153(a)(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought writ of mandamus to compel respondent lower court to suppress blood and related tests in his trial for driving under the influence of alcohol under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(a), felony hit and run under Cal. Veh. Code § 20001, and two counts of vehicular manslaughter under Cal. Veh. Code § 23153(a)(b). The court denied the writ and held that a search warrant was not required to draw blood when defendant was under arrest, probable cause existed for taking the blood and the withdrawal was accomplished in a medically approved manner. The court held that even under an emergency doctrine, there was an emergency present on the facts of this case. The court held that the taking of defendant's blood sample was not constitutionally forbidden, and that considering the totality of the circumstances, the conscience of the court was not shocked because although the police officers had to use considerable strength and restraint on defendant, it was necessary because the defendant, himself, was resistive and aggressive.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's writ to suppress blood and related tests and held the court correctly admitted the blood test because considering the totality of the circumstances, the conscience of the court was not shocked when after the arrest, police used considerable strength and restraint on defendant to draw a blood sample in a medically approved manner, because it was necessary as defendant was resistive and aggressive.

Kern River Public Access Com v. City of Bakersfield


No. F003185


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


170 Cal. App. 3d 1205; 217 Cal. Rptr. 125; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2370



August 9, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petitions of Defendant and Appellant and Real Party in Interest and Appellant for Review by the Supreme Court were Denied October 31, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 179074, Lewis E. King, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court is directed to amend the judgment to provide "for dedication of a reasonable public easement along the bank of the river which borders or lies within the tract 4179A," consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. As amended, the judgment is affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which issued a peremptory writ of mandate that directed appellant to rescind the approval of subdivision maps until public easements had been established.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the trial court correctly determined that the offsite easements for public parking and access to the river did not comply with Cal. Gov't Code §§ 66487.1 to 66478.14. The court found that appellant city and real party in interest, developer, were required to provide reasonable public access to and an easement along the bank of the river that was lying within the proposed subdivision. The court found that under § 66478.4, appellant could provide public access by a public path through the subdivision or by another access route that would not cross the subdivision. The court directed the trial court to amend the judgment to provide for a reasonable public easement along the riverbank that bordered the subdivision. The court affirmed the award of attorney fees because they were reasonable and the award was within the trial court's discretion. The court found that the trial court correctly determined that the alternative access plan accepted by appellant did not comply with the statutory requirements because it only provided access to and an easement along the wrong part of the riverbank.


OUTCOME: The court amended the trial court judgment and ordered appellant city to provide a reasonable public easement along the riverbank that bordered the subdivision. The court held that the public was to have access to the riverbank and an easement across or around the subdivision in order to access the river.

Holt v. Dep't of Food & Agric.


Civ. No. 23788


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


171 Cal. App. 3d 427; 218 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2426



August 6, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 30, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sutter County, No. 30459, Terence J. Keeley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, crop duster and company, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Sutter County (California), which denied their petition for an administrative writ of mandate directing respondent department of food and agriculture to set aside its decision imposing discipline upon them.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, crop duster and company, challenged the lower court's judgment that denied their petition for an administrative writ of mandate directing respondent department of food and agriculture to set aside its decision imposing discipline upon them for spraying workers with a pesticide. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment and held that acquittal of appellant crop duster in prior criminal proceedings did not bar the administrative proceedings through res judicata because in a criminal case the burden of proof was higher and different and consequently the issues decided were not identical. The court held that the statutes and regulations provided sufficiently clear standards to give fair notice of the proscribed conduct and the custom in the industry did not conform to the standard of care required in handling such a dangerous material. The court held that the duty to operate safely in the aerial application of pesticides was nondelegable and in view of the serious potential for disastrous results, and appellants' insistence that they needed only exercise minimal care, the decision of the agency was not regarded as a clear abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that denied the petition for an administrative writ of mandate directing respondent department of food and agriculture to set aside its decision imposing discipline upon them. The court held that acquittal in a prior criminal proceeding did not bar the administrative proceedings through res judicata, and the custom in the industry did not conform to the required standard of care.

Wallace v. Consumers Cooperative of Berkeley, Inc.


No. A015062


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


170 Cal. App. 3d 836; 216 Cal. Rptr. 649; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2282



July 31, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 475824-0, Robert K. Barber and Robert L. Bostick, * Judges.


* Retired superior court judge sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is modified to delete those provisions which direct appellant and others to take steps to effectuate payment of the fee award from certain sources, which declare the fee award to be costs within the meaning of section 1028, and which declare funds appropriated for the operating expenses of the Department of Food and Agriculture to be funds appropriated within the meaning of section 1028. In all other respects, the order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Director of the Department of Food and Agriculture of the State of California challenged an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which awarded attorney's fees pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5 to appellee consumer interest groups. The trial court awarded the fees upon finding appellees were successful parties in litigation challenging the validity of mandatory minimum retail milk prices in the state.


OVERVIEW: After a restraining order was obtained against them, appellee consumer interest groups challenged the validity of the Milk Stabilization Act, which authorized appellant Director of the Department of Food and Agriculture of the State of California to set minimum prices for retail milk. The parties entered into a settlement agreement in which appellees agreed to a continuance of the litigation to allow public hearings to be conducted on suspending the minimum retail prices. After the public hearings, appellant issued orders suspending the minimum retail milk price regulations. The trial court awarded attorney's fees to appellees. Upon appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that appellants were prevailing parties under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5 because the litigation set in motion the process that eventually resulted in the suspension of the milk pricing. The court found the award to appellees properly included compensation for services rendered during the public hearings where the administrative hearings and the action were intertwined. The court reasoned it was implicit in the settlement agreement that appellees would participate in the administrative hearings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that awarded attorney's fees in favor of appellee consumer interest groups. The court held that appellees were entitled to attorney's fees because they were prevailing parties where their litigation set into motion the machinery by which milk prices ultimately were suspended by appellant state Director of the Department of Food and Agriculture.

Edwards v. Steele


No. A025229.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


170 Cal. App. 3d 676; 216 Cal. Rptr. 283; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2270



July 26, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rev. den. Oct. 17, 1985, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 721286, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administrator, appellant city, and appellant county sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) determining that they were not immune from liability for damages for their refusal to issue respondent homeowner a variance building permit.


OVERVIEW: Respondent homeowner sought a building permit to add a third floor. After appellant administrator denied the permit, respondent appealed to appellant city's board of permit appeals (board). The board decided in respondent's favor. During the pendency of the challenge, appellant city's attorney issued an opinion advising appellant administrator not to follow the decision. The appeal was ultimately resolved in respondent's favor, and the board's decision was affirmed. Respondent then sued appellants, administrator, city, and county, for damages. Appellants sought review after the lower court granted respondent's motion for summary judgment on the issue of their governmental immunity because appellant administrator had failed to perform a mandatory act. The court found that under Cal. Gov't Code § 815.6, a public entity was liable for injury proximately caused by its breach of a mandatory duty to be performed without exercise of discretion. When the board overruled a decision of appellant city's permit bureau and ordered issuance of the permit, the bureau was required to issue the permit as directed, regardless of any contrary opinion. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in respondent homeowner's favor determining that appellant city, appellant county, and appellant administrator were not governmentally immune from suit was affirmed. Compliance with board of permit appeals' directive to issue respondent a building permit was mandatory. Because appellants failed to perform this mandatory act, governmental immunity did not apply.

 

969 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thomas


No. F004216.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


170 Cal. App. 3d 683; 216 Cal. Rptr. 520; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2271



July 26, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rev. den. Oct. 3, 1985, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 307833-4, Eugene W. Krum, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged her conviction, entered in the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), of offering to sell phencyclidine. Appellant contended error in the denial of her motion to exclude testimony, insufficiency of the evidence, and that the prohibition on probation and suspension of sentence for any person convicted of offering to sell phencyclidine constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged her conviction of offering to sell phencyclidine (PCP). She contended that the trial court erred when it denied her motion to have excluded testimony of her comment on having made good on a bad sale. However, the comment was relevant to appellant's intent and her knowledge of PCP and was, therefore, properly admitted. Its probative value outweighed any prejudice. She also alleged the insufficiency of evidence to have sustained a conviction. To have proved an offer to sell PCP, it must have been shown that appellant offered to sell the drug with the specific intent to make a sale. The evidence was sufficient to have constituted an offer. That no sale took place was a factor, but was not determinative. A reasonable interpretation of the evidence was guilt, and that the jury found so was within their purview. Appellant also argued that Cal. Penal Code § 1203.07(a)(6) constituted cruel and unusual punishment. However, having considered the degree of danger that PCP presented to society, the Legislature's classification of PCP dealings as serious crimes and its determination that all persons involved in its sale should have been denied probation were reasonable.


OUTCOME: The judgment for offering to sell phencyclidine was affirmed. The evidence was properly admitted, as it was relevant and its probative value outweighed any prejudice, and there was substantial evidence of offer and intent to sustain the conviction. The sentence imposed had not constituted cruel and unusual punishment, as the legislature's concerns and actions were reasonable in light of the degree of danger presented.

Harris v. State Pers. Bd.


No. B003459


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


170 Cal. App. 3d 639; 216 Cal. Rptr. 274; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2267



July 25, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 21, 1985. Arguelles, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 24, 1985. Lucas, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 399722, Bruce R. Geernaert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the writ of mandate is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for mandamus seeking an order for back salary from respondent personnel board, after appellant was terminated by his state employer, pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 89541.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee filed suit against respondent personnel board after he was terminated by his state employer, pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 89541, for being absent from work without first obtaining consent. Appellant was still disabled due to a work-related back injury. The trial court denied appellant's petition for mandamus. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, and ordered the trial court to issue its peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent to reconsider the appellant's request for back salary. The court held that the failure to provide to the appellant, as a minimum, notice of the intent to terminate, with a statement of reasons therefor and with some opportunity for appellant to respond either orally or in writing constituted a violation of procedural due process and resulted in the deprivation of a fundamental property right of the appellant. The court found that there was no record before the court that appellant was an unsatisfactory employee, only that he was a disabled employee with a reasonable cause for his absence. The court found that appellant was entitled to minimal procedural due process safeguards.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying a writ of mandate, remanded with an order to issue the writ directing respondent personnel board to reconsider appellant employee's request for back salary. The court held that respondent's failure to provide to the appellant notice of the intent to terminate, with a statement of reasons therefor and with some opportunity for appellant to respond was a violation of procedural due process.

 

971 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Mendez v. Kurten


No. B007127


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


170 Cal. App. 3d 481; 215 Cal. Rptr. 924; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2253



July 23, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 14, 1985, and Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 23, 1985. Bird, C. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NEC 30926, Robert M. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is reversed, with directions to the superior court to make a new and different order declaring that appellant's payment has entitled him to obtain from respondent a full satisfaction of judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judgment debtor sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's motion to clarify the nature and extent of interest awarded on a judgment against him. Appellant challenged the compounded interest that respondent judgment creditor sought to assess under Cal. Civ. Code § 3291 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 685.010(a), on the basis that it violated Cal. Const. art. XV, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Respondent judgment creditor made an offer to compromise which appellant judgment debtor rejected. The resulting judgment was greater than the amount of the offer to compromise. The trial court granted respondent's motion to add 10 percent interest from the date of the offer to compromise, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 658.010(a). After receiving the principal and 10 percent interest from the date of the offer to compromise, respondent refused to mark the judgment satisfied, claiming that the interest under § 658.010(a) was in addition to regular 10 percent judgment interest provided by Cal. Civ. Code § 3291. In reversing the judgment of the lower court, the court found that § 658.010(a) interest was not to be compounded with the interest provided under § 3291. Rather, it merely marked the beginning date at which interest could be added to the judgment. The court held that because Cal. Const. art. XV, § 1 limited interest on judgments to 10 percent, this interpretation was consistent with the presumption that all legislation is valid and should be construed to avoid unconstitutional results.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court because the statute awarding interest where an offer of compromise was rejected and a subsequent judgment was awarded in a greater amount was not meant to be in addition to the standard rate of interest on judgments. Rather, this statute allowed a judgment creditor to begin assessing interest at a date earlier than the date of judgment, but nothing more.

 

972 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Amezcua v. City of Pomona


No. B008557


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


170 Cal. App. 3d 305; 216 Cal. Rptr. 37; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 2234



July 19, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 2, 1985. Bird, C. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. EAC 48855, Peter S. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The denial of appellants' motion for a preliminary injunction against the City is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ice cream vendors challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied their motion for a preliminary injunction which sought to prevent respondent city from applying an ordinance that prohibited the sale of food and beverage from non-motorized pushcarts and contended that the ordinance violated equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ice cream vendors sought a preliminary injunction to prevent respondent city from applying an ordinance prohibiting the sale of food and beverage from non-motorized pushcarts. They claimed that the ordinance violated equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7 because it created two classes of peddlers without a reasonable justification. Appellants challenged the order that denied the injunction and this court affirmed. The ordinance was constitutional. A rational basis for the prohibition was articulated by respondent who showed that the statute was primarily concerned with the safety of all the users of the city streets and sidewalks. The argument that pushcarts were singled out when motorized carts could have been equally as hazardous was rejected because restrictions could have been imposed at whatever rate respondent chose. The record did not support the claim that this ordinance invidiously discriminated against those of Mexican ancestry as it applied to all pushcart vendors. The contention that the ordinance was an attempt by respondent to control a public nuisance had been waived and was not supported by the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied appellant ice cream vendors' request for a preliminary injunction preventing the application of respondent's ordinance because the ordinance was constitutional.
